A) Usurpation is not considered a tort
B) The usurper already guarantees the value of the property if it perishes
C) Usufruct is not considered property
D) The owner is responsible for supervising his property

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule on usufruct guarantee for usurpers can be overridden by modern law when public interest demands it, reflecting the school's flexibility through siyasa shar'iyya.

The Historical Necessity for Codification of Fiqh
Historical argument for codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school argues that judges after the stabilization of fiqh schools must be bound by codified texts rather than exercising independent ijtihad.

Core Idea
Ijtihad was a temporary necessity; codification is required for legal certainty.

This lesson examines the historical argument presented by contemporary Hanafi scholars for why judicial ijtihad was permissible in early Islam but is no longer acceptable after the formation of the schools. The passage argues that the exceptional phase of independent judicial reasoning must give way to uniform, announced legal rules benefiting both the public and the consistency of the legal system.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the early Islamic judiciary
Identify why early judges were mujtahids: necessity, lack of settled schools, geographic dispersion of knowledge. The passage compares this to today's Court of Cassation having ijtihad temporarily.
The comparison shows that temporary ijtihad is accepted but not permanent.
2
Understand the shift after school stabilization
Once fiqh rules and schools became established, a judge can no longer choose freely. He must be bound by announced texts. Even a judge qualified in ijtihad must follow codified rulings.
The stabilization of schools marks a turning point in judicial methodology.
3
Identify the new role of judicial ijtihad
Confined to interpreting the codified text according to usul al-fiqh, and the interpretation should be known in advance to the public. The passage uses the analogy of a single binding interpretation by the Court of Cassation.
Interpretation is now limited to the text, not free derivation.

وفي صدر الإسلام كان القضاة مجتهدين، بحكم الضرورة على خلاف الأصل، إذ لم تكن قد تكوّنت وتبلورت آراء ومذاهب اجتهادية في فهم نصوص الشريعة في الكتاب والسنة ليمكن الاختيار من بينها للقضاء، فكان ذلك حالة استثنائية (كتبدل اجتهاد محكمة التمييز اليوم تقبل ولا تستمر بصورة دائمة حرصاً على مبدأ علنية النظام.
وإذا كان القضاة في صدر الإسلام ،مجتهدين، فقد كان ذلك حالة لابد منها ولا محيص عنها قبل أن تستقر قواعد الفقه وأصوله ومذاهبه. إذ كان الصحابة ومن تلقى عنهم من التابعين وتابعيهم متفرقين في الأمصار، وعند كل منهم علم من سنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وتفسيره للقرآن ما ليس عند الآخر، كما قاله مالك رضي الله عنه للرشيد. أما بعد ذلك وقد استقر الفقه على قواعده ومذاهبه فلا يجوز أن يبقى القاضي حراً يختار للحكم في كل قضية ما يراه هو حقاً وعدلاً، ولو كان هو من العلم والفقه في رتبة الاجتهاد، بل يجب تقييده بنصوص معلنة للملأ لا يخرج عنها. وينحصر اجتهاده عندئذ في فهم النص وتفسيره وفقاً لقواعد الفهم والتفسير في أصول الفقه، كما سلفت الإشارة إليه، حتى إن الرأي الاجتهادي للقاضي في فهم النص أيضاً أن يكون معروفاً مسبقاً للمكلفين ما أمكن إلى ذلك من

ملحق ثاني بالفصل (۲۲) تقنين الفقه والتقنين منه
متين من الفقه وإن المصلحة توجب خلافه وتدعو إلى التمسك بعلنية الأحكام كما أسلفنا.
ونصوص الفقهاء في كتاب القضاء صريحة بأن القضاء يتخصص بالزمان وبالمكان وبنوع القضايا (۱) ، وبالرأي المذهبي. فلو نصب قاض ليقضي بالمذهب الشافعي في أصح الأقوال فيه ليس له أن يقضي بسواه ولو خالف مذهبه هو . ذلك لأنَّ القضاء في الأصل شرعاً من حق الإمام الخليفة بعد أن كان لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في حياته .
وإن للإمام أن ينيب عنه في القضاء إذا كثرت الأعباء العامة عليه. فالقاضي شرعاً نائب الإمام أي وكيله والوكيل يتقيَّد بما يقيده به الموكل، لأنَّ الإرادة الأصلية للمنوب عنه، فإذا رأى صاحب السلطة الشرعية في النظام اختيار رأي شرعي معتبر يلزم به الناس نظاماً معلناً يطيعونه ويطبقونه، ويلزم نائبه القاضي أن يقضي به، فهذا صحيح شرعاً، وملزم للقاضي، وليس له تجاوزه والقضاء بسواه اجتهاداً منه .
وهذه المشكلة كانت قد بَرَزَت وحصل الانتباه إليها في أوائل العصر العباسي، ورفعها عبد الله بن المقفع إلى الخليفة المنصور في رسالة سماها (رسالة (الصحابة شرح له فيها أنه في البصرة يقضى بحل الأنكحة والأموال في حادثة، ويقضى في الكوفة بحرمتها لاختلاف اجتهاد القضاة. وأشار عليه بأن يوحد ما يقضى به بين الناس، وذلك بأن يأمر تلك الأقضية بجمع وآراء الفقهاء المتعارضة في مجموعة وترفع إليه في كتاب، ويرفع معها ما يحتج به كل قوم من سنة أو قياس، ثم ينظر هو فيها ويختار في كل قضية ما يلهمه الله أنه الصواب أو الأصوب، فيأمر بالعمل به وينهى عن القضاء ،بخلافه، فتصبح تلك الأحكام المختلطة الصواب بالخطأ، حكماً واحداً

(۱) أورد الأستاذ المحقق الدكتور محمد زكي عبد البر جملة من أقوال الفقهاء من المذاهب الأربعة في هذا الشأن (تقنين الفقه الإسلامي، قطر: دار إحياء التراث الإسلامي، ١٤٠٧ هـ، ط ٢)، ص ٣٦ - ٦٢.
Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Jurisprudence. New ijtihad replaces old, and we are not permanently in a vortex before multiple ijtihad opinions among which the judge selects, so the obligated person does not know his fate therein. 12/22 - Opinion on the judge's ijtihad: In early Islam, judges were mujtahids, by necessity contrary to the original principle, since opinions and schools of ijtihad in understanding the Sharia texts from the Book and Sunna had not yet formed and crystallized so that selection could be made from them for judgment. So that was an exceptional state (like the ijtihad of the Court of Cassation today is accepted but does not continue permanently out of concern for the principle of publicity of the system. And if the judges in early Islam were mujtahids, that was a necessary and unavoidable state before the rules of fiqh, its principles, and its schools became settled. For the Companions and those who learned from them among the Followers and their successors were scattered in the various provinces, and each of them had knowledge of the Sunna of the Messenger of Allah and his interpretation of the Quran that others did not have, as Malik said to al-Rashid. After that, when fiqh became settled upon its rules and schools, it is not permissible for the judge to remain free to choose for judgment in every case what he sees as right and just, even if he is of the rank of ijtihad in knowledge and fiqh. Rather, he must be bound by texts announced to the public from which he does not depart. His ijtihad then is confined to understanding the text and interpreting it in accordance with the rules of understanding and interpretation in Usul al-Fiqh, as previously indicated, so that even the judge's ijtihad opinion in understanding the text should be known in advance to the obligated persons as much as possible. Thus today in the world of law and judicial systems, the opinion of the Court of Cassation alone in interpreting the legal text is the binding judicial ijtihad, so that judicial ijtihad (like the legal text) becomes a single interpretive opinion known to the obligated persons. As for the assumption that the judge legally must be left to his ijtihad to judge accordingly and that it is not permissible to restrict him with a single legal opinion binding for judgment, this has no solid foundation in fiqh, and the interest requires otherwise and calls for adherence to the publicity of rulings as we have stated. The texts of fuqaha in the book of Qada are explicit that judgeship is specialized by time, by place, by type of cases, and by school opinion. So if a judge is appointed to judge by the Shafi'i school according to its most correct opinion, he may not judge by other than it, even if it contradicts his own school. That is because judgeship originally, legally, is the right of the Imam (Caliph) after it was for the Messenger of Allah during his lifetime. And the Imam may delegate judgeship if public burdens increase upon him. So the judge legally is the deputy of the Imam, i.e., his agent, and the agent is bound by what the principal binds him with, because the original will is that of the delegator. Therefore, if the possessor of legal authority in the system chooses a valid legal opinion and imposes it upon the people as an announced system that they obey and apply, and binds his deputy the judge to judge by it, this is legally sound, and binding upon the judge, and he may not exceed it and judge by other than it through his own ijtihad. This problem had emerged and been noticed in the early Abbasid era, and Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' raised it to the Caliph al-Mansur in a treatise called the Treatise of the Companions, explaining to him that in Basra it is judged that marriages and properties in a certain incident are lawful, while in Kufa they are judged unlawful due to the difference of the judges' ijtihad. And he suggested to him that he unify what is judged among the people, by ordering that those rulings and the conflicting opinions of fuqaha be collected in a set and presented to him in a book, along with what each group argues from Sunna or analogy; then he should examine them and choose in each case what Allah inspires him as correct or more correct, and order its implementation and forbid judgment contrary to it. So those mixed rulings, right and wrong, would become a single ruling. (1) The researcher Dr. Muhammad Zaki Abd al-Barr has presented a number of statements from fuqaha of the four schools on this matter (Taqnin al-Fiqh al-Islami, Qatar: Dar Ihya al-Turath al-Islami, 1407 AH, 2nd ed.), pp. 36-62.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical argument from contemporary Hanafi legal thought on the necessity of codifying Islamic fiqh for judicial practice. The author begins by establishing the historical context: in early Islam, judges were mujtahids by necessity because ijtihad schools were not yet formed. This was an exceptional phase, not the norm. After the fiqh schools stabilized, the author argues, a judge must not be free to choose any opinion he sees fit, even if he is a mujtahid. Instead, he must be bound by announced legal texts (codified law). His ijtihad becomes limited to interpreting the text according to established usul al-fiqh. The author refutes the notion that a judge must have unrestricted ijtihad, calling it unsupported. He then cites the well-known Hanafi principle (and of other schools) that judgeship is specialized by time, place, case type, and school opinion — if a judge is appointed to judge by the Shafi'i school, he cannot deviate even to his own school. The legal reasoning: judgeship originally belongs to the Imam (Caliph) as successor to the Prophet. The Imam can delegate; the judge is his deputy. A deputy is bound by the principal's instructions. So the ruler's choice of a valid school opinion and its codification are binding on the judge. Finally, the author supports his argument historically with the incident of Ibn al-Muqaffa' advising Caliph al-Mansur to unify judicial rulings across the empire to avoid contradictory judgments. This passage is foundational for understanding the Hanafi position on codification (taqnin) and the limitation of judicial ijtihad in favor of legal certainty and uniformity.

Key Terms
تقنين الفقه (taqnīn al-fiqh): The process of codifying Islamic jurisprudential rules into formal, binding legal texts for judicial application, similar to modern legal codes.
اجتهاد القاضي (ijtihād al-qāḍī): The independent reasoning of a judge in deriving a ruling from the sources. In the context of this passage, it is confined to interpretation of codified texts after the establishment of schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason judges in early Islam were mujtahids?
A) It was a necessity because settled fiqh opinions did not yet exist.
B) They were following the ideal of independent reasoning.
C) The Caliph forbade them from following any school.
D) There was no written fiqh available.

Key Takeaway
After the stabilization of fiqh schools, judges must be bound by codified texts to ensure legal certainty; the early phase of independent ijtihad was temporary and exceptional.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Legal Basis: Judge as Deputy of the Imam and the Binding Nature of Codified Law
Legal reasoning for judicial subordination to codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that the judge is the deputy of the Imam to explain why codified fiqh binds judges and limits their ijtihad to interpretation.

Core Idea
The judge is the Imam's deputy; he must follow the ruler's choice of law.

This lesson focuses on the core legal principle that justifies binding judges to codified fiqh: the judge is the deputy (na'ib) of the Imam. The passage uses this principle along with the historical precedent of Ibn al-Muqaffa' advising Caliph al-Mansur to unify judicial rulings. By understanding this, you will appreciate the systematic Hanafi justification for codification and the limits of judicial ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of judicial authority
Judgeship originally belongs to the Imam (Caliph) as successor to the Prophet. The Imam can delegate his authority to judges.
This establishes that the judge's authority is derived, not original.
2
Understand the deputy principle
The judge is a deputy (na'ib) and agent (wakil) of the Imam. An agent must follow the principal's instructions.
This is the key legal reasoning: the judge is bound by the Imam's choices.
3
Apply to codification
If the ruler chooses a valid opinion and codifies it, the judge must judge by it, even if it differs from his personal school or ijtihad. The passage gives the example of a judge appointed to judge by the Shafi'i school's most correct opinion.
The judge cannot deviate even to his own school.
4
Review the historical precedent
Ibn al-Muqaffa' suggested to Caliph al-Mansur that conflicting rulings be unified by collecting opinions and choosing one authoritative ruling for each case.
This early call for codification supports the principle.

نائب الإمام (nā'ib al-imām): The judge as the deputy of the caliph or ruler, meaning his authority is delegated, and he must follow the ruler's instructions in judicial matters.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must a judge appointed to judge by a specific school follow that school even if it contradicts his own?
A) Because that school is always correct.
B) Because the judge is the deputy of the Imam and must follow the Imam's choice.
C) Because the judge has no ijtihad whatsoever.
D) Because the school is chosen by the judge himself.

Key Takeaway
The judge is the deputy of the Imam; therefore, if the ruler chooses and codifies a valid school opinion, the judge is bound to apply it even if it differs from his own ijtihad or school.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Temporary Separation of Corpus and Usufruct Ownership in Wills
Explaining two scenarios of will that separate ownership of corpus and usufruct

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a will can separate ownership of corpus and usufruct temporarily, and identify when the usufruct reverts to the corpus owner.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a will can separate ownership of corpus and usufruct, but only temporarily until the usufruct period ends.

This passage presents a key rule (rasm) for muftis: the ownership of a thing's corporeal substance (raqabah) and its usufruct (manfa'ah) can be split through a will, but the separation is always limited in time. Understanding this temporary split is essential for issuing correct fatwas on inheritance and bequests. The author illustrates with two classic scenarios that clarify the legal relationship between the legatee and the heir.

Guided Path
1
State the core rule
Articulate that a will can separate ownership of corpus from usufruct temporarily. This is a recognized Hanafi principle.
The rule is a rasm – a practical guideline for fatwa.
2
Analyze scenario one: bequest of usufruct only
The testator bequeaths only the usufruct to a legatee; the corpus passes by inheritance. Identify that the legatee receives the usufruct on account of the testator, not the heirs, and the corpus is treated as if detained for the legatee's benefit.
The inheritor owns the corpus but cannot enjoy its benefits until the legatee's period ends.
3
Analyze scenario two: separate bequests of corpus and usufruct
The testator bequeaths the corpus to one person and the usufruct to another for a specific period or life. The corpus legatee owns only the corpus during that period; the usufruct legatee enjoys the benefit.
Both legatees have ownership rights, but one is limited in time.
4
Identify the temporal limitation
In both scenarios, the separation is temporary. The usufruct reverts to the corpus owner when the specified period expires or the usufruct legatee dies.
This reversion is automatic; no additional transfer is needed.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a new set of facts (e.g., a will bequeathing usufruct for 10 years and corpus to another), determine the ownership positions at different times: at the testator's death, during the 10 years, and after the period ends.
The mufti must clearly state who owns what and when.

ثانياً: إذا أوصى مالك العين لشخص بمنفعتها مدة معينة أو مدة حياته، وأوصى لآخر برقبتها، فإنَّ الموصى له بالعين يكون مالكاً لرقبتها وحدها مدة انتفاع الموصى له بالمنفعة حتى تنقضي بانتهاء المدة المحددة له أو بموته.
وفي كلتا هاتين الصورتين يكون انفكاك ملك الرقبة عن ملك المنفعة موقوتاً بمدة استحقاق المنتفع للمنفعة فمتى انتهت عادت المنفعة إلى تبعيتها للرقبة، فيصبح مالك الرقبة مالكاً للمنفعة على حكم الأصل.
They own by inheritance only the corpus of that thing, but its usufruct is owned by the legatee, and its inheritance is delayed, so the legatee receives it on account of the testator, not on account of the heirs, and the object is considered as if it is detained on the ownership of the testator with respect to the bequeathed usufruct. Second: if the owner of the object bequeaths its usufruct to a person for a specific period or for his life, and bequeaths its corpus to another, then the legatee of the corpus owns only its corpus during the period of the usufruct legatee's enjoyment until it ends upon the expiry of the specified period or his death. And in both of these scenarios, the separation of ownership of the corpus from ownership of the usufruct is limited to the period of the beneficiary's entitlement to the usufruct; when it ends, the usufruct returns to its subordination to the corpus, so the owner of the corpus becomes owner of the usufruct according to the original rule.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal rule (rasm) of separating ownership of the corpus (raqabah) from ownership of the usufruct (manfa'ah) through a will. The author presents two scenarios: first, when the testator bequeaths the usufruct to someone and the corpus passes by inheritance; second, when the testator bequeaths the corpus to one person and the usufruct to another for a limited period. In both, the separation is temporary. Once the period ends or the usufruct legatee dies, the usufruct reverts to the owner of the corpus. This is a Hanafi rule reflecting the principle that usufruct is a distinct owned entity (mamluk) but not considered 'mal' (wealth) in the strict sense.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqabah): The corporeal substance or ownership of the thing itself, as opposed to its usufruct.
المنفعة (al-manfa'ah): The usufruct or benefit derived from the object, considered as a separate owned entity in Hanafi law.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A will or bequest that transfers ownership of either the corpus or usufruct to a legatee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It reverts to the owner of the corpus
C) It goes back to the testator's estate
D) It is divided between the two legatees

Key Takeaway
A will can temporarily split ownership of corpus and usufruct; once the usufruct period expires or the legatee dies, the usufruct automatically reverts to the corpus owner.

Distinguishing Tamlik from Ibaha: The Mufti's Application
Defining the legal difference between granting ownership and mere permission to use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik (granting ownership) and ibaha (mere permission) in Hanafi fiqh, and apply the maxim 'al-ibrat fi al-uqud li al-maqasid' to determine the legal ruling when a permission is given, citing the example from the text.

Core Idea
Permission to use (ibaha) does not transfer ownership; only when context shows intent to give ownership does it become a transfer.

This lesson examines a crucial distinction in Hanafi muamalat: the difference between granting ownership (tamlik) and mere permission to use (ibaha). Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti when issuing fatwas, as the same expression ('I permit you') can have different legal effects depending on the context. The passage also introduces a key maxim that guides the mufti: contracts are interpreted by intentions and meanings, not literal words.

Guided Path
1
Define Tamlik and Ibaha
Read the first part of the passage to understand the definitions. Tamlik is granting ownership of a thing or usufruct. Ibaha is permission to consume or use without transferring ownership. Note the author’s emphasis that ibaha is 'below' tamlik.
Also note that ibaha does not make the recipient a owner; only the owner can grant ownership or permission.
2
Identify the Legal Effects of Ibaha
From the examples given (eating food, using tools), deduce that the recipient of ibaha can only use or consume the item. They cannot sell, give away, or permit others to use it. This is because they have no ownership rights.
The text states: 'the one permitted does not own anything.'
3
Recognise the Exception: When Ibaha Becomes Tamlik
The passage explains that if evidence (dalil) indicates the permission was intended as a gift or loan, then the legal effect changes: ibaha is metaphorically used, and ownership transfers. The example given is when someone requests a gift and the owner says 'I permit you to take it'.
The key here is the context: the request (talab) shows intent.
4
Apply the Maxim 'al-ibrat fi al-uqud li al-maqasid'
The passage concludes with the maxim 'Consideration in contracts is given to intentions and meanings, not to words and forms.' Apply this to the example: the literal word 'ibaha' is overridden by the intent to give a gift. Identify the illa (reason) for this ruling: the qara'in (indicators) of intent.
This maxim is a fundamental tool for a mufti in interpreting contracts.
5
Synthesise the Rule for a Mufti
As a mufti, when faced with a case where someone says 'I permit you' to another, you must examine the context: Was there a request for a gift or loan? Any other evidence of intent? Default is ibaha, but if context shows a transfer was intended, rule accordingly.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that mere permission does not transfer ownership unless intent is clear.

غير أنه إذا قامت الدلائل على أن المراد بها الهبة أو الإعارة، فعندئذ تنعقد سبباً لتمليك العين أو المنفعة؛ كمن استهدى من آخر شيئاً، أو طلب إعارته فأجابه بقوله : أبحته لك فخذه، فإن الإباحة قد عبر بها عندئذ عن معنى الهبة أو الإعارة مجازاً؛ ومن القواعد المقررة أن «العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني، لا للألفاظ والمباني».
٣٧٣
Chapter Twenty-Six: The Difference Between Tamlik (Granting Ownership) and Ibaha (Permission), and Between Ownership of Usufruct and Right of Usufruct. Section One: The Difference Between Tamlik and Ibaha. 1/26 - The jurists distinguish between the ruling of tamlik and ibaha: Ibaha is permission to consume a thing or to use it; it does not make it owned, rather it is below tamlik. So if a person permits another to eat from his food or from the fruit of his garden, the one permitted does not own anything of the food or fruit, nor does he have the right to give it away or sell it to others; he only has the right to eat, because only the owner has the right to grant ownership or permission, and the one permitted is not an owner. Similarly, if a person permits another to use whatever he wishes of his tools or clothes, the one permitted may use them without giving them away or permitting others to use them. From this it is known that mere ibaha from an owner to another is not a gift (hiba) nor a loan (i'arah); rather it is a license and permission (see Majalla article 139). However, if evidence indicates that what was intended was a gift or a loan, then it becomes a cause for the transfer of ownership of the item or usufruct; for example, if someone requests a gift from another, or asks for a loan, and the other responds by saying, 'I permit you to take it,' then ibaha is used here metaphorically to mean gift or loan. And from the established principles: 'Consideration in contracts is given to intentions and meanings, not to words and forms.'
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition clarifying the distinction between tamlik (granting ownership) and ibaha (mere permission). The author, a Hanafi jurist, begins by stating that jurists differentiate between the two concepts. He defines ibaha as permission to consume or use a thing, but it does not transfer ownership; it is lesser than tamlik. He gives an example: if a person permits another to eat from his food, the recipient does not own the food and cannot give it away or sell it; he can only eat it. Similarly, permission to use tools does not grant the right to lend them to others. The key point is that only the owner can grant ownership or permission. The text then notes that mere ibaha is not a gift or loan, but if context shows that the permission was intended as a gift or loan (e.g., in response to a request), then it is treated as such, because contracts are interpreted by intentions, not literal words. This is a rule for muftis when issuing fatwas: they must look beyond literal expressions to the true intent of the parties.

Key Terms
التمليك (al-tamlik): Granting ownership of a thing or its usufruct to another person
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permission to consume or use a thing without transferring ownership

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The person becomes the owner of the item.
B) The person can sell the item to others.
C) The person can use or consume the item but cannot transfer ownership or grant permission to others.
D) The person's permission automatically becomes a gift after use.

Key Takeaway
Mere permission (ibaha) does not transfer ownership; the mufti must look to the parties' intent, applying the maxim that contracts are judged by meanings, not literal words.

Rukn: The Pillar in Hanafi Legal Theory
Defining the term 'rukn' with examples and scholarly critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-rukn in Hanafi terminology, distinguish it from muqawwimat, and apply the distinction to contracts and acts of worship.

Core Idea
The rukn is an integral part of the essence; the contracting parties are muqawwimat, not arkan.

In Hanafi legal theory, precise terminology is essential for understanding the formation of contracts and acts of worship. This lesson focuses on the term 'rukn' (pillar), distinguishing it from other elements such as the agent and the subject matter. We will explore its linguistic and technical definitions, see examples from qiyas and 'ibadat, and examine a scholarly dispute about what constitutes a rukn in a contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions
Compare the Arabic etymology (corner of a house) with the legal definition (integral part of essence). Note that the rukn is what the thing stands upon.
The linguistic meaning helps understand why it is called a 'pillar'.
2
Recognize the examples given
Note how rukn applies to qiyas (similarity of illa), contract (offer and acceptance), and salah (bowing, prostration, recitation).
Each example shows that without these, the thing loses its identity.
3
Distinguish rukn from muqawwimat
Understand that the agent and subject matter are necessary for existence but not part of the essence; they are muqawwimat (constituents), not arkan.
Muqawwimat include rukn plus external necessities.
4
Analyze the scholarly disagreement
Contrast the precise Hanafi view with the broader definitions of al-Ghazali and al-Qarafi, who include the contracting parties and subject matter as arkan.
The Hanafi response: this is a laxity in terminology.
5
Apply the distinction in a contract
Given a contract scenario, identify which elements are rukn and which are muqawwimat.
Offer and acceptance = rukn; parties and subject matter = muqawwimat.

Chapter Twenty-Eight: Five Technical Terms in the Theory of Contract: The Pillar (Rukn), the Cause (Sabab), the Effective Cause ('Illah), the Condition (Shart), the Preventer (Mani'). 1/28 - In order to understand the formation of a contract precisely, distinguishing the effect of each element on which its formation and creation depend, it is good to preface by clarifying some technical terms in usul al-fiqh, knowledge and distinction of whose scientific concepts are needed here and in many future discussions. These terms are: al-rukn, al-sabab, al-illah, al-shart, and al-mani. 2/28 - a) The Pillar (Rukn). Linguistically, the rukn of something is the strong side that holds it; like the pillars of a house, which are its corners that hold its structure. And the rukn in the terminology of the fuqaha' and usuliyyin is: that by which the subsistence and existence of something are constituted, such that it is considered an integral part of its essence (see al-Misbah al-Munir and al-Jurjani's Definitions). For example, the rukn of qiyas upon which it is built is the unity or similarity in the effective cause of the ruling between the original case and the new case, because qiyas subsists by this similarity like the subsistence of a house on its four pillars; if this similarity is lost, the matter is no longer qiyas but rather an independent ruling for the new incident (see al-Kashf, Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi, beginning of chapter on rukn of qiyas, 3/344). And the rukn of a contract is what expresses the agreement of the two wills, i.e., offer and acceptance, or what takes their place as we will see, because they are the intrinsic elements in the essence of the contract and its meaning; by their legal connection the contract stands and is formed, just as water is formed from the union of two chemical elements: hydrogen and oxygen. In matters of worship, bowing, prostration, and recitation of the Quran are considered arkan of prayer, because its essence and reality are constituted by these actions. Accordingly, the doer (agent) is not considered a rukn in his action according to the technical meaning of rukn, because the agent is not an integral part in the meaning and essence of the action, although every action necessarily has an agent. Thus, the contracting party is not called a rukn in the contract. However, some fuqaha' like Imam al-Ghazali consider the contracting party a rukn in the contract on the basis that he is one of two fundamental sides in its formation (see al-Jurjani's Definitions and al-Misbah al-Munir under 'rukn'). And al-Shihab al-Qarafi, a Maliki jurist, also counted in his book 'al-Furuq' the two compensations and the two contracting parties among the arkan of the contract (al-Furuq, vol. ?, p. 70). This is a laxity in terminology, and the precise truth is what we have explained. So the two contracting parties and the subject matter of the contract are considered constituents (muqawwimat) of the contract, but they are not arkan in the technical sense of rukn. Thus, 'constituents' is more general than 'arkan' because it includes everything without which the contract cannot actually exist, whether a rukn, a contracting party, or a subject matter, as we will see (cf. para 1/29). 28/3 - b) The Cause (Sabab): Linguistically, sabab originally means the rope by which a person climbs or moves. The path of a road is called sabab by analogy with the stretched rope. Subsequently, sabab is also used for anything by which one reaches something else and leads to it. Accordingly, Allah's saying: 'And we gave him (Dhul-Qarnayn) from everything a sabab' (Quran 18:84), meaning we taught him the means that lead to results in every matter (see Mufradat al-Raghib). And the sabab in the terminology of the fuqaha' and usuliyyin is:
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a technical definition of the term 'rukn' (pillar) as used in Hanafi fiqh and usul al-fiqh. The author begins by contrasting the linguistic meaning (strong side that supports) with the technical meaning: an integral part of the essence (mahiyya) of something, without which that thing would not exist. He provides examples from qiyas (analogy) and contracts to illustrate. Importantly, he distinguishes the rukn from the agent (fāʿil) and other elements. He notes that some scholars, like al-Ghazali and al-Qarafi, loosely include the contracting parties or subject matter as arkan, but the precise Hanafi view is that only the offer and acceptance (or their equivalents) constitute the rukn of a contract. The parties and subject are 'muqawwimat' (constituents) necessary for existence but not part of the essence. The passage also begins defining 'sabab' (cause) but the definition is cut short at the page break. The discussion of sabab is incomplete here, but the key point is that a sabab is an external means that leads to a result, unlike a rukn which is internal.

Key Terms
الركن (al-rukn): ما يكون به قوام الشيء ووجوده بحيث يعد جزءاً داخلاً في ماهيته
السبب (al-sabab): في اللغة: الحبل الذي يتعلق به الإنسان. وفي الاصطلاح: ما يتوصل به إلى غيره (تعريف غير مكتمل)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The two contracting parties
B) The offer and acceptance
C) The subject matter of the contract
D) The price and consideration

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi legal theory, al-rukn is the integral part of the essence; for a contract, the rukn is solely the offer and acceptance, not the parties or subject matter.

The Rule of Interpreting Ambiguous Offers as Lesser Binding Contracts
Explaining the requirement of clarity and the rule for ambiguous words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that if a word used in an offer could indicate two contracts, it is interpreted as the lesser, and identify when this rule is triggered.

Core Idea
If a word could mean a stronger or weaker contract, it is interpreted as the weaker – acting on certainty.

A contract requires clear expression of intent. However, sometimes the words used can be ambiguous, pointing to more than one possible contract. The Hanafi school has established a clear rule to resolve such ambiguity: the word is interpreted as indicating the lesser (less binding) contract. This lesson explains the rationale and application of this rule, which is a key tool for a mufti dealing with ambiguous contract terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous word
Read the offer (ijab) and notice the word used. Check if it could logically indicate more than one contract type. For example, 'I give you this for ten dirhams' could indicate a sale or a gift with a condition.
The ambiguity must be real, not imagined.
2
Determine the stronger and weaker contract
Among the possible contracts, identify which is more binding (stronger) and which is less binding. For example, a sale is more binding than a gift because a sale creates reciprocal obligations.
Strength is measured by the degree of obligation, not the number of terms.
3
Apply the rule: interpret as the weaker
According to the Hanafi rule, the word is interpreted as indicating the weaker contract. This is based on the principle of certainty: you cannot impose a greater obligation without confirmed intent.
This protects the parties from being bound to obligations they did not clearly intend.
4
Check if a contextual evidence (qarina) is present
If the word is an allusive expression (kinaya), it may not be interpreted without a qarina. But if the word itself is explicit, no qarina is needed.
The presence of a qarina may override the default interpretation.
5
Derive the ruling for the case
State the final contractual classification: e.g., it is a sale (if price is mentioned) or a gift (if no price).
The ruling must be consistent with the lesser obligation unless a qarina indicates otherwise.

صيغة العقد وما إليها
ما يجب أن يتوافر في صيغة العقد اللفظية
انعقاد العقد بالكتابة وبالإشارة، وبالدلالة - نوعا الدلالة : التعاطي ولسان الحال - العقود العينية.
المطلب الأول : صيغة العقد
1/30 - الأصل في معنى العقد اتفاق الإرادتين، أي التراضي وليست الألفاظ إلا ترجمة عنهما .
ولما كانت الإرادة من الأمور الباطنة وكانت للعقود نتائج ذات بال في الأموال والأعمال أوجب الفقه الإسلامي ظهور الإرادتين بشكل واضح بين لا شك فيه ؛ وذلك بأن تتوافر في الصيغة اللفظية المعبرة عنهما، وهي الإيجاب والقبول ثلاثة أمور أساسية :
-
جلاء المعنى.
وتوافق الإيجاب والقبول.
- وجزم الإرادتين .
وإليك إيضاح هذه النواحي :
2/30 - :أولاً : جلاء المعنى في صيغة العقد:
المراد بجلاء المعنى في صيغة العقد أن تكون مادة اللفظ المستعمل للإيجاب والقبول في كل عقد تدل دلالة واضحة عرفاً على نوع العقد المقصود للعاقدين. وذلك لأن العقود يختلف بعضها عن بعض في الموضوع والأحكام، فإذا لم يُعرف بيقين أن العاقدين قد قصدا عقداً بعينه لا يمكن إلزامهما بأحكامه الخاصة به .
ففي مبادلة المال بالمال يجب أن يستعمل لفظ «البيع» أو ما يؤدي معناه من تمليك الأعيان بعوض.
وفي المبادلة على المنفعة أن يجب يستعمل لفظ «الإجارة» أو ما يفيد من تمليك المنفعة بعوض ؛ وهلم جراً.
ولا فرق بعد هذا بین أن تكون دلالة اللفظ على العقد المقصود دلالة حقيقية أو مجازية فإنَّ الجلاء والوضوح يتحقق في طريقي الحقيقة والمجاز .
ومتى احتمل اللفظ المستعمل في الإيجاب أحد عقدين أحدهما أقوى وأكثر إلزاماً لأحد العاقدين والآخر أدنى، فإنه يحمل على الأدنى جرياً مع اليقين، إذ لا يسوغ إلزام أحد بالتزامات عقدية لم يتحقق قصده إلى العقد الموجب لها. (ر ) الدر المختار ج ه، أول كتاب الإيداع).
وعلى هذا كانت ألفاظ الكنايات» في أحد العقود يحتاج في عقده بها إلى قرينة ترجّح أن العاقد قد أراد بها ذلك العقد، لأن لفظ الكناية يحتمله كما يحتمل غيره فلا بد من مرجح، وهو القرينة.
فينعقد البيع بلفظ الهبة (۱) المقرون بالثمن، بأن يقول أحد المتبايعين :
وهبتك هذا بكذا دراهم، فيقبل الآخر (ر: فتح القدير، ٤٥٨/٥). وكذا ينعقد النكاح بلفظ الهبة أو الصدقة أو نحوهما مما يفيد الملك الدائم إذ قُرن بالمهر، بأن تقول المرأة للرجل مثلاً: وهبتك نفسي على مهر قدره ،كذا فيقبل الرجل (ر ): الهداية وشروحها ١٠٥/٣ - ١٠٨ ) .
الدلالة
(۱) لفظ الهبة يعتبر صريح الدلالة لا يحتاج إلى قرينة إذا استعمل في معنى التمليك المجاني بلا عوض أما إذا استعمل في نحو البيع أو النكاح فيعتبر كناية في عليهما، فيحتاج إلى قرينة ترجح أنه أريد به العقد المقصود بيعاً أو نكاحاً .
Chapter Thirty: The Contract Formulation and Related Matters. What must be present in the verbal formulation of a contract. Formation of a contract by writing, by gesture, and by implication – two types of implication: mutual dealing and the language of the situation – real contracts. First Section: The Contract Formulation. 1/30 – The essence of a contract is the agreement of two wills, i.e., mutual consent, and words are but a translation of them. Since the will is internal and contracts have significant consequences in property and actions, Islamic jurisprudence required the two wills to appear clearly and unmistakably; by providing in the verbal formulation expressing them, namely offer and acceptance, three essential matters: clarity of meaning, correspondence of offer and acceptance, and certainty of the two wills. Here is an explanation of these aspects: 2/30 – First: Clarity of meaning in the contract formulation: What is meant by clarity of meaning in the contract formulation is that the material of the word used for offer and acceptance in each contract should indicate clearly by custom the type of contract intended by the two contracting parties. This is because contracts differ from one another in subject matter and rules, and if it is not known with certainty that the two parties intended a specific contract, they cannot be obligated by its specific rules. Thus, in exchanging property for property, the word "sale" must be used, or what conveys its meaning of transferring ownership of goods for a consideration. In exchanging for a benefit, the word "lease" must be used, or what conveys its meaning of transferring ownership of a benefit for a consideration, and so on. After this, there is no difference whether the indication of the word to the intended contract is real or figurative, for clarity and obviousness are achieved in both real and figurative modes. And whenever a word used in the offer could indicate one of two contracts, one of which is stronger and more binding for one of the parties and the other lesser, it is interpreted as the lesser, acting on certainty, for it is not permissible to impose contractual obligations on anyone unless his intention towards the contract that necessitates them has been confirmed. (See: al-Durr al-Mukhtar, part 5, beginning of the Book of Deposit). Based on this, the words of "kinayat" (allusive expressions) in any contract require, for the contract to be formed with them, a contextual evidence (qarina) that indicates that the contracting party intended that contract thereby, because an allusive word could indicate it as well as something else, so a weight must be preferred, which is the contextual evidence. Thus, a sale is concluded by the word "hiba" (gift) when accompanied by a price, such as one of the two parties saying: "I gift you this for such-and-such dirhams," and the other accepts (see: Fath al-Qadir, 5/458). Similarly, a marriage (nikah) is concluded by the word "hiba" or "sadaqa" or similar expressions that imply permanent ownership when accompanied by a dower (mahr), such as a woman saying to a man: "I gift you myself for a dower of such-and-such," and the man accepts (see: al-Hidayah and its commentaries, 3/105-108). Footnote: The word "hiba" is considered explicit and does not require contextual evidence if used in the sense of gratuitous transfer without consideration. But if used in the context of sale or marriage, it is considered allusive in those contexts and requires contextual evidence to confirm that it is intended to mean sale or marriage.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam discusses the fundamental requirement of clarity in the verbal formulation (sigha) of a contract. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that a contract is fundamentally an agreement of two wills, and words are merely external expressions of that internal will. Because the law gives significant effect to contracts, Islamic jurisprudence requires that the intention be clearly manifested. Three essentials are listed: clarity of meaning (jala' al-ma'na), correspondence of offer and acceptance, and certainty of intention. The passage focuses on the first: clarity. The words used for offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) must clearly indicate, according to customary usage ('urf), the type of contract intended. For example, a sale requires a word meaning transfer of ownership for a price, while a lease requires a word meaning transfer of a benefit for a price. Importantly, the indication may be real (haqiqi) or figurative (majazi) as long as it is clear. A key rule derived from the principle of legal certainty (al-yaqin) is that if a word could indicate two contracts, one more binding and one less binding, it is interpreted as the less binding one, because imposing a greater obligation without certain intention is unjust. This leads to the discussion of allusive expressions (kinayat). A kinaya is a word that carries multiple possible meanings; it requires a contextual evidence (qarina) to determine which contract is intended. Examples include using "hiba" (gift) for a sale when a price is mentioned, or for marriage (nikah) when a dower is mentioned. In these cases, the word "hiba" is not explicit for those contracts; it is a kinaya that needs a qarina to indicate the specific contract intended. The footnote clarifies that "hiba" is explicit for a pure gift, but becomes kinaya when used for other contracts. This is a practical rule for issuing fatwas regarding ambiguous contract terms.

Key Terms
صيغة العقد (sīghat al-'aqd): The verbal formulation of a contract, consisting of offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul).
جلاء المعنى (jalā' al-ma'nā): Clarity of meaning: the words used must clearly indicate the type of contract intended by custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a word used in an offer could indicate two contracts, one more binding than the other, how does Hanafi law interpret it?
A) It is interpreted as the more binding contract to protect the other party.
B) It is interpreted as the less binding contract, acting on certainty.
C) The contract is void due to ambiguity.
D) It depends on the parties' customary usage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi contract law, ambiguous offers are interpreted as the lesser binding contract unless a contextual evidence (qarina) indicates otherwise.

Allusive Expressions (Kinayat) and Contextual Evidence (Qarina) in Contracts
Explaining the use of kinayat and qarina in contract formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a word used in a contract offer is an allusive expression (kinaya) and determine the role of contextual evidence (qarina) in specifying the intended contract.

Core Idea
A word like 'gift' can form a sale or marriage when paired with a qarina like a price or dower.

In the previous lesson, we learned the general rule for ambiguous words. Now we examine specific examples where words that are normally explicit for one contract (like 'gift' for gratuity) become allusive (kinaya) when used in a different context. This lesson explores the role of contextual evidence (qarina) in clarifying the true contractual intent, using the classic Hanafi examples of hiba (gift) used for sale and marriage.

Guided Path
1
Define kinaya (allusive expression)
A word that has a primary literal meaning but is used to indicate something else, requiring contextual evidence to determine the intended meaning.
Kinaya is contrasted with sarih (explicit) which is clear without context.
2
Identify the qarina
Look for any additional words or circumstances that indicate the intended contract. For example, mentioning a price (thaman) or a dower (mahr) serves as a qarina.
The qarina must be suitable to the contract type.
3
Apply to the first furu': hiba with price
When someone says 'I gift you this for ten dirhams,' the word 'gift' is a kinaya for sale. The mention of the price is the qarina that specifies sale, not a gift. The contract is a valid sale.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.
4
Apply to the second furu': hiba with dower
When a woman says 'I gift you myself for a dower of such-and-such,' the word 'gift' is a kinaya for marriage (nikah). The mention of dower (mahr) is the qarina indicating marriage.
This contract is a valid marriage.
5
Derive the rule for mufti practice
When a mufti encounters such phrases, he must examine the presence and strength of the qarina. If the qarina is clear, the contract is the specific type indicated. If ambiguous, the default rule (lesser obligation) applies.
The mufti should also consider cultural context; what is a clear qarina in one region may not be in another.

Chapter Thirty: The Contract Formulation and Related Matters. What must be present in the verbal formulation of a contract. Formation of a contract by writing, by gesture, and by implication – two types of implication: mutual dealing and the language of the situation – real contracts. Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An allusive expression that is not explicit for a particular contract but can indicate it if accompanied by a contextual evidence (qarina).
قرينة (qarīnah): Contextual evidence or indicator that clarifies the intended meaning of an ambiguous expression.
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit word that clearly indicates a specific contract without needing contextual evidence.

According to the passage, what makes the word 'gift' (hiba) form a valid sale?

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi contracts, a word that is normally explicit for one transaction can become allusive when used with a suitable contextual evidence, thereby forming a different contract.

The Imperative Form in Contracts: General Rule and Marriage Exception
Refining the rule on imperative form; exception for marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rule that the imperative form does not constitute an offer, explain the marriage exception and its two justifications, and evaluate the role of custom in potentially generalizing the exception.

Core Idea
The imperative form is a mere request, not a contract offer — except for marriage, where custom permits it.

In Hanafi contract law, the words used for offer and acceptance (sigha) must indicate a firm intention. The imperative form (e.g., 'Sell me') is considered a request, not an offer. However, an important exception exists for the marriage contract (nikah), where the imperative is accepted as valid. This lesson examines the rule, its exception, and the role of custom in modern application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The imperative form (sīghat al-amr) is not a valid offer or acceptance. For example, if someone says 'Sell me' and the other replies 'I sold,' the contract is not concluded until the one commanding explicitly accepts.
Refer to Majalla article 172.
2
Recognize the marriage exception
In marriage, if a man says 'Marry me to her' or 'Marry so-and-so to me,' and the guardian responds 'I have married you,' the marriage is valid despite the imperative form.
This is a specific exemption from the general rule.
3
Understand the two Hanafi explanations
Some jurists consider the imperative in marriage a true exception (istithna) from the rule, treating it as a valid offer. Others interpret it as a proxy (tawkīl), where the guardian, by responding, becomes an agent for both parties.
Both explanations lead to the same result: validity.
4
Note the optional generalization
The author mentions that if contemporary custom treats the imperative as an offer in all contracts, it could be adopted because custom (urf) is legally considered. This shows the dynamic nature of Hanafi fiqh.
Generalization requires evidence that the custom is widespread and consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳۰) صيغة العقد وما إليها
فيه، وذلك لأنها في قوة قوله : بعتك فخذه فقد بنوا على المدلول المقدر ر المجلة (۱۷۲)
أما الإيجاب أو القبول نفسه فلا يكون أبداً بصيغة الأمر. فلو قال : بعني أو أجرني بكذا فقال : بعتك أو آجرتك، لا ينعقد العقد بذلك حتى يقبل الآمر لأنَّ الأمر هنا مجرد طلب وتكليف . (ر: المجلة / ۱۷۲).
وقد استثنوا عقد النكاح خاصة فقرروا فيه انعقاد العقد إذا قال الرجل للمرأة زوجيني نفسك، أو قال لوليها أو وكيلها زوجني فلانة، فأجيب: زوجتك .
فمنهم من اعتبر الأمر هنا إيجاباً على سبيل الاستثناء من القاعدة. ومنهم من اعتبره توكيلاً، فالطرف الثاني بإجابته يكون قد تولى طرفي العقد بحكم هذه الوكالة.
على أنه لو عمم اليوم هذا الحكم فاعتبرت صيغة الأمر إيجاباً في جميع العقود كما في عقد النكاح لكان حسناً، لاتجاه عرف الناس إليه، والعرف في ذلك معتبر شرعاً.
Section (30): The Form of the Contract and Related Matters
In it, and that is because it is in the strength of his saying: 'I sold it to you, so take it'; they built on the implied meaning (Majalla, 172).
As for the offer or acceptance itself, it is never in the imperative form. If one says 'Sell to me' or 'Lease to me for such' and the other says 'I sold to you' or 'I leased to you', the contract is not concluded thereby until the one commanding accepts, because the command here is merely a request and assignment. (Majalla, 172).
They have specifically exempted the marriage contract, ruling that it is concluded if a man says to a woman 'Marry yourself to me' or says to her guardian or agent 'Marry so-and-so to me' and the reply is 'I have married her to you'.
Some consider the command here as an offer by way of exception from the rule. Others consider it a proxy, so the second party, by responding, becomes the one who handles both sides of the contract by virtue of this agency.
However, if this ruling were generalized today and the imperative form were considered an offer in all contracts as in marriage, it would be good, due to the inclination of people's custom towards it, and custom is considered legally in that regard.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the general rule about the imperative form. It first reiterates that the imperative form (e.g., 'Sell me') does not constitute a valid offer or acceptance; rather, it is a mere request. The contract only concludes if the person commanding explicitly accepts the other's response. However, an exception is made for the marriage contract (nikah). If a man says 'Marry me to so-and-so' and the guardian says 'I have married you', the marriage is valid. The Hanafi scholars explain this either as an exception to the rule (considering the imperative as an offer) or as a proxy (the guardian acts as agent for both parties). The author also notes that contemporary custom might justify extending this exception to all contracts, as customs are legally considered. This reflects the Hanafi principle that custom (urf) can influence legal rulings.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (sīghat al-amr): The imperative verb form used as a request (e.g., 'sell me')
توكيل (tawkīl): Agency; interpretation that the imperative in marriage creates an agency for the other party
استثناء (istithnāʾ): Exception from the general rule; the marriage contract is exempt from the rule that imperative is not valid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the ruling when a man says 'Sell me your car' and the seller replies 'I sell it'?
A) The contract is valid because both expressed intent
B) The contract is invalid because the imperative is a request
C) The contract is valid only if the buyer then explicitly accepts
D) The contract is valid because it is akin to marriage

Key Takeaway
The imperative form is not a valid offer except in marriage, where it is accepted either as a true exception or as a proxy; custom may extend this to other contracts.

The Mute's Gesture in Hanafi Legal Maxims
Defining the legal effect of mute's gestures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'الإشارة المعهودة للأخرس كالبيان باللسان' to classify gestures in transactions and identify when gestures are invalid due to literacy or lack of custom.

Core Idea
The mute's accustomed gesture equals speech—but only if he cannot write and the gesture is habitual.

This passage introduces a key Hanafi maxim governing the legal effect of gestures by mute persons. You have likely wondered whether a mute can validly conclude a marriage or divorce by nodding. The text provides a clear rule: the gesture substitutes for speech, provided it is habitual and understood. We will also examine two important qualifications: the dominance of writing over gesture, and the special case of a temporarily mute person.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its wording
Identify the exact Arabic wording of the maxim: الإشارة المعهودة للأخرس كالبيان باللسان, and note its concise rendition in the text as a sughra qa'ida.
2
Identify the core condition
The gesture must be 'accustomed' (معهودة) and 'understood' (مفهومة), and the mute must be illiterate. If literate, writing supersedes.
This is the dabt of the maxim.
3
Apply the rule to transaction types
List the categories mentioned: contracts (sale, marriage), unilateral creations (will, endowment), relinquishments (divorce, release), and others (claims, oaths). For each, confirm the gesture suffices.
4
Evaluate the exception for literacy
Recognize that the preponderant view (al-ra'j al-rajih) requires writing for literate mutes. Gesture is inferior to writing in clarity.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on temporary muteness
Compare the two opinions: suspension until death vs. immediate effect after one year. Identify the preferred view (mufta bihi) as the one-year rule due to hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : الإشارة :
وأما إشارة الأخرس فإنها تقوم مقام النطق باللسان باتفاق الفقهاء، إذا كان لا يعرف الكتابة في جميع تصرفاته التعاملية القولية التي يكون له فيها إشارة معهودة مفهومة جرت عليها عادته سواء منها ما كان عقداً كالبيع والشراء والنكاح، أو كان إنشاء منفرداً في التمليكات كالوصية لجهة خيرية والوقف، أو في الإسقاطات كالطلاق والإبراء وإسقاط الشفعة، أو في غير ذلك كالدعوى والإقرار وحلف اليمين.
وعلى هذا الأساس وضعت القاعدة القائلة :
الإشارة المعهودة للأخرس كالبيان باللسان (م (۷۰) .
أما إذا كان الأخرس كاتباً فالرأي الراجح فقهاً أنه لا تصح تصرفاته إلا بالكتابة لأنها في الدلالة كالنطق من كل وجه. أما الإشارة فهي دون الكتابة دلالة، فلا تقبل مع إمكان الكتابة.
وإذا كان الخرس غير أصلي بل طارئاً، وهي حالة معتقل اللسان فالرأي المرجّح عند فقهائنا أنه إذا كان لا يعرف الكتابة وتكونت له إشارات معهودة اعتادها للإفصاح عن مراده تقبل إشارته هذه في تصرفاته إذا دامت عقلة لسانه إلى الموت.
وعلى هذا تكون تصرفاته بإشارته المعهودة موقوفة إلى موته : فإذا مات على عُقلته نفذت تصرفاته مستندة إلى تاريخ صدورها، أي انها يكون لها أثر رجعي.
(1) أما إذا كانت الكتابة قد علق فيها الطلاق على بلوغ الكتاب بأن كتب الرجل إلى زوجته إذا وصلك كتابي هذا فأنت طالق، فلا يقع الطلاق عندئذ إلا بعد وصوله، كما في العقود. وانظر في أحكام الكتابة وشرائطها (رد المحتار ٤٢٨/٢ ، والأشباه لابن نجيم مع حاشية الحموي ج ۲، الفن الثالث، ص/١٩٦ - ١٩٧).
ومن الفقهاء من يرى الاكتفاء بدوام عقلته سنة، فيصبح بعدها كالأخرس الأصلي، وتقبل إشارته المعتادة، ويكون لها نفاذ فوري. وهذا أوجه وأدفع للحرج، فإنّ الرأي الأول فيه حرج عظيم، فقد تمتد حياة معتقل اللسان عشرات السنين بعد عقلته فلا ينبغي وقف تصرفاته طوال ذلك أنه قد تكونت له إشارة معتادة ( ر ) رد المحتار ٤٢٥/٢)
وسيأتي إيضاح النظر الفقهي في إشارة الأخرس ومعتقل اللسان في كلامنا على القاعدة السالفة الذكر في القسم الثالث من هذا الجزء.
Second: Gestures:
As for the gesture of the mute, it stands in place of spoken articulation by consensus of the jurists, provided he does not know writing, in all his verbal transactional dispositions for which he has an accustomed, understood gesture that has become his habit. This includes whether it is a contract such as sale, purchase, and marriage, or a unilateral creation in transfers such as a will for a charitable purpose and endowment, or in relinquishments such as divorce, release, and waiver of pre-emption, or otherwise such as claims, acknowledgments, and taking oaths.
Upon this basis, the maxim was formulated:
The accustomed gesture of the mute is like verbal expression. (M. 70)
If the mute is literate, the preponderant opinion in fiqh is that his dispositions are not valid except by writing, because writing in indication is like speech from every aspect. Gesture is below writing in indication, so it is not accepted when writing is possible.
If the muteness is not congenital but temporary, which is the state of being tongue-tied (mu'taqal al-lisan), the preponderant opinion among our jurists is that if he does not know writing and has developed accustomed gestures that he habitually uses to express his intent, these gestures are accepted in his dispositions if his tongue-tied state continues until death.
Upon this, his dispositions by his accustomed gesture are suspended until his death: if he dies in his tongue-tied state, his dispositions are executed based on their date of issuance, meaning they have retroactive effect.
(1) As for if the writing included a conditional divorce upon receipt of the letter, such that a man wrote to his wife: 'When my letter reaches you, you are divorced,' then divorce does not occur until it reaches, as in contracts. See the rulings and conditions of writing in (Radd al-Muhtar 2/428, and al-Ashbah li Ibn Nujaym with Hashiyat al-Hamawi vol. 2, al-Fann al-Thalith, pp. 196-197).
Some jurists consider it sufficient that his tongue-tied state lasts one year; after which he becomes like a congenital mute, his accustomed gestures are accepted, and they take immediate effect. This is more appropriate and more repelling of hardship, for the first opinion involves great hardship, as the life of the tongue-tied person may extend for decades after his condition, and it is not proper to suspend his dispositions throughout that [period] when he has developed an accustomed gesture. (Radd al-Muhtar 2/425)
And the clarification of the juristic view on the gesture of the mute and the tongue-tied person will come in our discussion on the aforementioned maxim in the third part of this volume.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a rasm_rule: it establishes the legal effect of gestures performed by a mute person. The rule is that the accustomed gesture of a mute (al-akhras) stands in place of spoken language in all verbal transactional dispositions, provided the mute does not know how to write. This applies to contracts (sale, marriage, etc.), unilateral acts (wills, endowments), relinquishments (divorce, release), and other matters (claims, oaths). The foundation is the maxim: 'The accustomed gesture of the mute is like verbal expression.' 

The passage then addresses two special cases: (1) If the mute is literate, the preponderant view (al-ra'j al-rajih) is that writing is required because it is like speech in clarity; gesture is inferior and thus not accepted when writing is possible. (2) If the muteness is temporary (mu'taqal al-lisan), two opinions are given. The first (weaker) view suspends his dispositions until death with retroactive effect. The second (preferred by the author, Zarqa) holds that after one year of being tongue-tied, he is treated like a congenital mute—his gestures become immediately effective. This is chosen to avoid hardship, as the person might live for decades. The passage ends by noting that further elaboration will follow in the discussion on the maxim in Part Three. The footnote clarifies that a conditional divorce via writing is effective only upon reaching, similar to contracts.

Key Terms
إشارة (ishārah): Habitual, understood bodily gestures used by a mute to communicate intent in legal transactions.
أخرس (akhras): A person who is born mute or loses the ability to speak permanently.
معتقل اللسان (mu'taqal al-lisān): A person who becomes tongue-tied temporarily, unable to speak but not permanently mute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a mute person is literate (knows writing), what is the ruling on his gestures?
A) Gestures are valid and preferred over writing because they are natural.
B) Gestures and writing are both acceptable equally.
C) Only writing is valid; gestures are not accepted.
D) It depends on the type of transaction.

Key Takeaway
The accustomed gesture of a mute substitutes for speech only when illiterate and the gesture is habitual; writing always takes precedence.

The Hanafi Maxim: Prohibition Does Not Imply Voidness Without a Qarina
Clarifying that prohibition does not equal voidness; a qarina is required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a Shariah prohibition does not automatically void a contract, identifying when a qarina (contextual indicator) is required and distinguishing between non-formation and corruption.

Core Idea
A Shariah prohibition does not void a contract unless a contextual indicator (qarina) shows the Lawgiver intended invalidity.

In Hanafi law, not every Shariah prohibition renders a contract void. The prohibition may only make the act sinful, while the contract remains valid but possibly corrupt. The key is whether there is a contextual indicator (qarina) that the Lawgiver intended nullification. This lesson unpacks that principle using the example of selling during Friday prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act and its textual basis
Determine what act is prohibited and the explicit text. Here, selling during Friday adhan is prohibited by Quran 62:9.
2
Determine the nature of the prohibition
Is the prohibition on the essence of the contract or an external circumstance? The selling itself is not prohibited, only the timing.
3
Search for a qarina of invalidity
Does the text indicate that the contract becomes void? The verse does not mention invalidity, only a command to leave trade.
4
Classify the contract's legal status
Without a qarina, the contract is formed but may be corrupt (fāsid) or sinful (makrūh). If a qarina exists, it is void (bāṭil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يعود سبب المنع لأمر خارجي عن محل العقد وموضوعه كالبيع وقت أذان الجمعة عند من يراه من الفقهاء باطلاً للنهي القرآني عنه بقوله تعالى : .. فَاسْعَوْا إِلَى ذِكْرِ اللَّهِ وَذَرُوا الْبَيْعَ ) (سورة الجمعة : ٩/٦٢). ويجب أن يلحظ أيضاً في هذا المقام أن مجرد النهي الشرعي عن عقد من العقود، ولو بنص خاص صريح لا يستلزم أن يكون العقد ممنوعاً تحت طائلة البطلان ما لم تقم قرينة على هذه الطائلة (الجزاء) في قصد الشارع. فمجرد النهي الشرعي عن عقد في نظر الاجتهاد الحنفي تارة يكون أثره عدم الانعقاد، وتارة يكون العقد منعقداً رغم مخالفة النص، ولكنه «فساداً»، كما سنرى في بحث البطلان والفساد من نظرية المؤيدات التشريعية.
And the cause of prohibition may be external to the subject matter and object of the contract, such as selling at the time of the Friday adhan, according to those jurists who consider it void due to the Quranic prohibition: '... hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave off trade' (Surah al-Jum'ah 62:9). It must also be noted in this context that mere Shariah prohibition of a contract, even by an explicit specific text, does not entail that the contract is prohibited under penalty of invalidity unless there is an indication (qarina) of this penalty in the intent of the Lawgiver. For in the view of Hanafi ijtihad, mere Shariah prohibition sometimes results in non-formation, and sometimes the contract is formed despite violating the text, but is 'fasad' (corrupt), as we will see in the discussion of nullity and corruption in the theory of legislative sanctions.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage introduces an important Hanafi methodological rule: not every Shariah prohibition makes a contract void (bāṭil). The prohibition must be accompanied by an indicated penalty (qarina) showing that the Lawgiver intended invalidity. The author illustrates with the Quranic prohibition of trade during Friday adhan; some jurists consider it void, but Hanafis may treat it as makrūh or corrupt (fāsid) depending on the evidence. The rule distinguishes between 'adam al-in'iqād (non-formation) and 'in'iqād ma'a al-fasād (formation with corruption). This is a key uṣūl al-iftā' principle for determining the effect of prohibitions in transactions.

Key Terms
قرينة (qarīnah): A contextual indicator that reveals the Lawgiver's intent regarding the penalty for prohibition.
فساد (fasād): Corruption; a contract that is formed (mun'aqid) but defective due to a flaw that can be rectified in some cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on the effect of a Shariah prohibition on a contract?
A) The contract is always void (bāṭil)
B) The contract is always valid but sinful
C) The contract may be valid, corrupt, or void depending on whether a qarina of invalidity exists
D) Prohibition only affects the afterlife, not worldly contracts

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a Shariah prohibition does not automatically invalidate a contract; a contextual indicator (qarina) is required to determine if voidness was intended.

Contracts Void Due to Public Order or Pre-existing Duty
Discussion of principles voiding contracts conflicting with public order or pre-existing religious duties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contracts that are void under Hanafi law due to conflict with public order or pre-existing religious duties, and apply the distinction between diyana and qada'.

Core Idea
A contract is void if it restricts a lawful freedom or pays for a pre-existing religious duty — you cannot contract to do what is already required.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work outlines fundamental limits on contractual freedom in Hanafi law. Even mutual consent cannot validate a contract that undermines public order (like restricting lawful trade) or that pays someone for an obligation they already bear (like refraining from crime). Understanding these limits is crucial for a mufti to distinguish valid from invalid agreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two grounds for voiding contracts
First, the contract must not conflict with public order (al-nizam al-'amm), which includes freedom to choose lawful means of earning. Second, it must not obligate payment for something already required by Shariah (diyanatan).
Public order in Islam is broader than in secular systems; it includes ethical and religious values.
2
Examine the examples given
The author lists five: (1) agreeing not to trade, (2) paying someone to refrain from crime, (3) sulh on something already due, (4) hiring a wife to manage the home, (5) hiring for religious duties. Each illustrates the two grounds.
Pay attention to the underlying reasoning: the contract either restricts a lawful freedom or duplicates an existing obligation.
3
Note the exceptions
Hiring for adhan, imama, and teaching the Quran is permitted due to necessity (darura). This shows that the rule has pragmatic limits.
Reciting Quran is not permitted because it is a personal act of worship, though some later scholars allowed it for teaching purposes.
4
Understand the diyana vs. qada' distinction
In the wife example, the wife's duty is diyanatan (religiously required but not legally enforceable in qada'). This distinction is central to Hanafi fatwa methodology.
The mufti must determine whether an obligation is enforceable in court or only a matter between the person and Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳۱) الشرائط العامة لانعقاد العقود
وكذا لا يعتبر في قواعد الاجتهاد الحنفي التعاقد بين اثنين على عدم الاشتغال في التجارة مثلاً، لأن حرية الإنسان في اختيار الوسيلة المشروعة لاكتسابه هي من النظام العام في الإسلام، وهذا العقد يمنعه من ذلك، فيكون غير مفيد. وكذلك لا يعتبر التعاقد على عوض يأخذه أحد العاقدين لقاء أمر واجب عليه شرعاً بدون عقد، كما لو تعاقد على أخذ عوض لقاء كفه وامتناعه عن فعل جريمة فهذا التعاقد باطل لا يستحق فيه العوض، لأن الشخص ملزم شرعاً بهذا الامتناع من غير حاجة إلى عقد.
وعلى هذا يقرر الفقهاء في قواعد عقد الصلح أن الصلح على بقاء ما هو مستحق البقاء باطل، فإذا دفع فيه بدل فللدافع استرداده. ر الفوائد البهية في القواعد الفقهية للشيخ محمود حمزة ص (١٤٨.
ويقررون أيضاً في الإجارة أنه لا ينعقد استئجار الرجل زوجته على خدمة بيت الزوجية وإدارته بأجرة شهرية لأنَّ هذه الإدارة يوجبها الشرع عليها ديانة بحسب استطاعتها دون تعاقد (۱) . وبوجه عام يقررون عدم جواز استئجار شخص للقيام بشيء من الواجبات الدينية التي يجب عليه القيام بها قبل التعاقد. وقد استثنوا من ذلك جواز الاستئجار على الأذان والإمامة وتعليم القرآن للضرورة، ولم يجيزوه على قراءة القرآن (ر: البدائع ١٩١/٤ (۱۹۲-
(۱) لكن الزوجة لا تلزم بها قضاءً. وهذا مما يفترق فيه حكم القضاء عن حكم الديانة، على ما تقدم في أول هذا المدخل (ر: ف ٤/٢).
Chapter (31): General Conditions for the Validity of Contracts. Likewise, in the principles of Hanafi ijtihad, a contract between two parties to not engage in trade, for example, is not considered valid, because a person's freedom to choose a lawful means of earning is part of public order in Islam, and this contract prevents him from that, so it is useless. Similarly, a contract for compensation that one of the contracting parties takes in exchange for something that is religiously obligatory upon him without a contract is not considered valid, such as if he contracts to take compensation for refraining from committing a crime; this contract is invalid and he does not deserve the compensation, because the person is religiously obligated to refrain without need for a contract. Based on this, the jurists decide in the rules of the contract of sulh that a sulh for something that is already due to continue is invalid; if a substitute is paid in it, the payer can reclaim it. See al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya fi al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya by Shaykh Mahmud Hamza, p. 148. And they also decide regarding ijarah that it is not valid for a man to hire his wife for the service of the marital home and its management for a monthly wage, because this management is obligated by the Shariah upon her as a matter of religious duty (diyanatan) according to her ability, without a contract (1). In general, they decide that it is impermissible to hire a person to perform any of the religious obligations that he must fulfill before the contract. They have excepted from that the permissibility of hiring for the adhan, imama, and teaching the Quran out of necessity, and they did not permit it for reciting the Quran (see: al-Bada'i', 4/191-192). (1) However, the wife is not legally compelled to it in qada'. This is a matter in which the ruling of qada' differs from the ruling of diyana, as previously mentioned at the beginning of this introduction (see: § 4/2).
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the general conditions for contract validity in Hanafi fiqh. It establishes that contracts conflicting with public order (al-nizam al-'amm) or obligating someone to pay for a pre-existing religious duty are invalid. The author gives several examples: First, an agreement to not engage in trade is void because it restricts the person's freedom to choose a lawful livelihood, which is part of public order. Second, paying someone to refrain from a crime is invalid because the person is already obligated to refrain. Third, a sulh (settlement) on something that is already due to continue is void; if payment is made, it can be reclaimed. Fourth, hiring one's wife to manage the household is invalid because the wife already has a religious duty (diyanatan) to do so without a contract; however, she is not legally compelled in qada'. Fifth, hiring someone to perform religious obligations that are already obligatory on them (like prayer, fasting) is not allowed, with exceptions for adhan, imama, and teaching the Quran due to necessity, but not for reciting the Quran (although some later Hanafis differentiate). This principle reflects the Hanafi emphasis on preventing exploitation and ensuring that contracts do not create obligations that are already required by Shariah.

Key Terms
النظام العام (al-nizam al-'amm): Public order in Islam, which includes the freedom to choose lawful means of earning and the prohibition of restricting it.
ديانة (diyanatan): As a matter of religious duty, not legally enforceable in qada'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi principles, why is a contract to pay someone for refraining from a crime invalid?
A) Because it is a form of bribery.
B) Because the person is already obligated to refrain without a contract.
C) Because it encourages crime.
D) Because it is not a valid subject of contract.

Key Takeaway
A contract is void if it either restricts a lawful freedom (public order) or pays for a pre-existing religious duty, with limited exceptions for necessity.

بقاء الإيجاب صحيحاً إلى وقوع القبول
Statement of a condition for contract validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an offer is invalidated by revocation, death, or loss of capacity before acceptance, especially in contracts by correspondence.

Core Idea
An offer is not binding; it is destroyed by revocation, death, or incapacity before acceptance.

This passage states a fundamental condition for contract formation in Hanafi law: the offer must persist until acceptance. The offeror may revoke freely, and events like death or insanity automatically nullify the offer. This is particularly relevant in written or distant transactions where time lapses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The offer must remain valid until acceptance occurs. This is a temporal condition linking offer and acceptance.
2
List the invalidating events
Three events destroy the offer: (1) revocation by the offeror, (2) death of the offeror, (3) loss of legal capacity (e.g., insanity) of the offeror.
3
Understand the rationale
The contract requires a meeting of wills; the offeror's will is not final until acceptance. Hence, any change in will or capacity voids the offer.
This protects the offeror from being bound against subsequent change of circumstances.
4
Apply to correspondence
In contracts by mail or messenger, if the offeror dies or revokes before the offeree's acceptance reaches him, the acceptance is invalid.
The offer is considered destroyed at the moment of the event, even if unknown to the offeree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادساً : بقاء الإيجاب صحيحاً إلى وقوع القبول : فرجوع الموجب عن إيجابه قبل أن يقبل الطرف الآخر مبطل للإيجاب، فلا يعتبر القبول بعده. وكذا وفاة الموجب أو زوال أهليته قبل القبول . وهذا قد يكثر في حالات التعاقد بالكتابة بين الغائبين، فقد يرسل شخص كتاباً يتضمن إيجاب العقد من طرفه ثم يموت أو يجن قبل وصول الكتاب، فلا يعتبر عندئذ قبول المرسل إليه عندما يصل إليه الكتاب ويقرؤه، لانهدام الإيجاب المبني عليه بوفاة الموجب قبل وقوع القبول.
Sixth: The offer remains valid until acceptance occurs. The revocation of the offer by the offeror before the other party accepts invalidates the offer, so acceptance after that is not considered. Likewise, the death of the offeror or the loss of his legal capacity before acceptance. This may often occur in cases of contracting by correspondence between absent parties. A person may send a letter containing an offer of contract from his side, then he dies or becomes insane before the letter arrives. In that case, the acceptance of the recipient when the letter reaches him and he reads it is not considered, because the offer upon which it was based is destroyed by the death of the offeror before acceptance occurs.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage states the sixth condition for contract validity: the offer must remain in effect until acceptance. In Hanafi law, once an offer is made, the offeror can revoke it at any time before acceptance. If the offeror dies or loses legal capacity (e.g., insanity) before acceptance, the offer is automatically void. This is particularly relevant in written contracts between absent parties. For example, if A sends a letter offering to sell his car, and then dies before the letter reaches B, B's subsequent acceptance is invalid because A's offer was no longer existent at the time of acceptance. The ruling is based on the principle that the contract requires a meeting of minds, and the offer is a unilateral act that can be withdrawn.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): The initial offer made by one party in a contract.
قبول (qabul): The acceptance of the offer, which must occur while the offer is still valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
