A) The contract is valid upon B's acceptance.
B) The acceptance is valid because the offer was made.
C) The acceptance is invalid because the offer was destroyed by death.
D) The contract is valid if B accepts immediately upon reading.

Key Takeaway
An offer is invalidated by revocation, death, or loss of capacity before acceptance; acceptance after any of these is void.

Jest (al-Hazl) in Contracts: Void or Defective?
Defines hazl, its methods, and the dispute over its effect, settling on voidness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on jest contracts, identifying when a contract is void due to lack of intention and distinguishing it from the defective view.

Core Idea
A contract made in jest is void ab initio because intention is essential for formation.

Jest (al-hazl) occurs when a person utters contract words playfully without intending legal effects. The Hanafi school, as refined by its later verifiers, holds such a contract void (bāṭil) and not concluded. This lesson examines the definition, proofs, and the preferred opinion, illustrating how simulation (ṣūriyya) renders contracts ineffective. Understanding this distinction is crucial for issuing fatwas on ambiguous transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three methods of hazl
Recognize that hazl can be proven by explicit declaration, prior collusion, or circumstantial evidence. Each method shows the absence of real intention.
Circumstantial evidence is weakest; the claimant of seriousness is believed due to the maxim that speech is presumed effective.
2
Determine the legal effect according to the preferred view
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi position, a contract made in jest is void (bāṭil) and has no legal effect. Even possession does not transfer ownership.
This is based on the absence of intention regarding the essence of the contract.
3
Contrast with the alternative defective (fāsid) view
Some early jurists held the contract is concluded but defective, meaning the formula is present but the intention is missing, so no effects arise. The later school rejects this because the contract never formed.
The practical outcome may appear similar, but the legal characterization differs; voidness means there is no contract at all.
4
Apply the rule when parties dispute
If one party claims serious intent and the other claims jest, the claim of seriousness is accepted because giving effect to speech is preferred over ignoring it.
The burden of proof lies on the one alleging jest.

Source Text

Second Topic: Jest
5/33 - Jest is the opposite of seriousness (with kasra on jim). It is the speech of a playful, joking, or mocking person who does not intend for his speech to have legal consequences and effects.
Jest in verbal dispositions is realized by one of the following methods:
- Either by a declaration contemporaneous with the contract by both parties or one of them, e.g., saying: 'I sell, or give, or lend in jest.'
- Or by prior collusion, by which the two parties agree that the contract they will conclude is jest or play, not intending its ruling, then they conclude it based on this understanding.
- Or by circumstantial evidence indicating that the speaker is jesting or mocking. The jester, even if he speaks the formula of the contract with his will and consent, is playful and does not want the ruling, rather he rejects it.
Therefore, the contract made in jest is considered a simulated contract, void, not concluded, so it has no legal effect whatsoever. So if it were a sale, it does not effect transfer of ownership of the sold item even if the buyer takes possession, due to the absence of real intention (1).
(1) The apparent from the expressions of the jurists is that when there is a dispute between the parties as to whether a contract is jest or serious, the word is given to the one who insists on seriousness, because that is the original in speech; and it is a rule that 'giving effect to speech is better than ignoring it'; and the other party must prove jest, as was explained in collusion, because jest and collusion are brothers.
6/33: Further, simulation is a garment that can clothe all contracts and verbal dispositions, causing them to lose their validity and effect, except in three instances where Islamic law does not accept simulation, rather it gives the apparent verbal disposition its effect.
(1) The juristic view on the contract of a jester differed: it was said it is concluded but defective and has no effect – so the jester buyer does not own the item by taking possession, and the jester borrower may not use the borrowed item, etc. – and it was said it is void and not concluded due to the absence of intention regarding the essence of the contract, evidenced by the fact that it has no effect by agreement of all jurists, and this is the result of voidness. The verifiers among our jurists hold this second opinion, as they mention on various occasions that the contract of a jester is void and not concluded. Reference: al-Badā’i‘ 5/176, Fatḥ al-Qadīr 5/459, Radd al-Muḥtār 4/7. This is the more correct view considering the general principles we have established about contractual intention and its types and rulings; therefore we have preferred this basis considering it void, and counted it among simulated contracts.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage defines 'hazl' (jest) and its effect on contracts. Jest is when a person uses the words of a contract without intending its legal consequences. It can be proven by explicit statement, prior agreement, or circumstantial evidence. The Hanafi view presented is that a contract made in jest is void (bāṭil) and not concluded, so no legal effects follow—e.g., no transfer of ownership even if the buyer has possession. The passage then notes a dispute: some said it is merely defective (fāsid) but still concluded, though with no effect. The author favors the void view as the opinion of the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) from the Hanafi school, citing al-Badā'i‘, Fatḥ al-Qadīr, and Radd al-Muḥtār. This view aligns with the principle that intention is essential for contract formation. The author includes a rule: when parties dispute whether a contract was jest or serious, the one claiming seriousness is believed because applying speech is prioritized over ignoring it.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Speech uttered in jest, play, or mockery, without intention to produce legal effects.
صورية (sūriyya): Simulation; a contract that appears valid but lacks genuine intention, rendering it void or ineffective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Valid but unenforceable
B) Void (bāṭil) and not concluded
C) Valid only if the jester confirms it
D) Defective (fāsid) with no effect

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a contract made in jest is void ab initio for lack of real intention, and this is the mufta bihi position.

Majority vs. Hanafi on Coercion and Jest: A Fatwa Hierarchy Lesson
Comparing majority and Hanafi positions on coercion and jest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and majority positions on coercion vs. jest, identify the thamarah al-khilaf, and apply the tarjih to a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
Coercion removes choice; jest does not — so the majority rejects the Hanafi analogy.

This passage presents the majority view that coercion and jest are fundamentally different in their effect on legal acts. While the Hanafis analogize them, the majority argue that the coerced person lacks free will, and Shari'ah texts exempt him from liability. As a mufti, understanding this distinction is crucial when issuing fatwas on coerced transactions and determining liability.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by recalling the Hanafi view that coercion and jest are analogous and thus ineffective in three specific transactions (divorce, manumission, oath).
The Hanafis rely on qiyas: both involve a mismatch between action and intention.
2
Identify the majority opposition
The majority of mujtahids reject this analogy. They argue that in jest, the actor is free (mukhtar) but does not intend the legal effect, so the law can impose it. In coercion, the actor is not free.
They cite Quranic texts and hadith about not holding a coerced person accountable.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Focus on the element of choice (ikhtiyar). The jester chooses to speak but not the result; the coerced does not choose the act itself. This is the basis for different rulings.
For non-overwhelming coercion (ikrah ghayr mulji'), the coerced may still bear financial liability.
4
Determine the praktical consequence
Under majority view, coerced contracts are void or voidable, while jester's contracts are binding. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
In Hanafi view, both are ineffective; under majority, only coercion is ineffective.
5
Apply the tarjih for fatwa
The author states that the majority view is 'more appropriate and consistent with legislative wisdom.' As a mufti, you would follow this tarjih unless you have strong evidence to adopt the Hanafi position.
Note that this is a later Hanafi author preferring the majority, which is common in mufti hierarchy when a position is stronger in dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أنَّ جمهور المجتهدين خالفوا الحنفية ولم يقبلوا هذا القياس، ففرّقوا بين الإكراه والهزل في هذه التصرفات الثلاثة التي لا يؤثر فيها الهزل، وقرروا أنَّ الإكراه يؤثر فيها ويمنع صحتها أو لزومها (على الاختلاف بين الاجتهادات الإسلامية في أثر الإكراه كما في سائر التصرفات. وذلك بخلاف الهزل، لأن الهازل مختار في مباشرة السبب لكنه لا يريد ثبوت حكمه، فيمكن إثبات الحكم رغماً عنه إذا اقتضت المصلحة التشريعية ذلك .
أما المتصرف بالإكراه فغير مختار فلا يسوغ إلزامه، ولا سيما أن نصوص الشريعة صريحة في عدم مؤاخذة الإنسان بما استكره عليه .
ورأي الجمهور هذا في نظرنا أوجه وأجرى مع حكمة التشريع
(۱)
=
إنسان بإكراه غير ملجيء يكون هو المسؤول بالمال دون الحامل المكره، لنقصان الإكراه غير الملجيء بالنسبة إلى خطورة الفعل المكره عليه لأنه ليس مفسداً لاختيار الفاعل .
وإن كانت مما لا يباح عند الضرورة، كقتل الغير، فإنه لا يبيحه الإكراه مطلقاً ولو أكره عليه بالقتل. لكن إذا أطاع الإكراه فقتل من يراد قتله فإن العقوبة الخاصة وهي القصاص تكون على الحامل المكره لا على الفاعل.
But the majority of mujtahids opposed the Hanafis and did not accept this analogy. They distinguished between coercion and jest in these three transactions in which jest has no effect, and ruled that coercion does affect them and prevents their validity or bindingness (according to the difference among Islamic ijtihads on the effect of coercion as in other transactions). This is contrary to jest, because the jester is free in undertaking the cause but does not intend its legal effect, so the effect can be imposed upon him unwillingly if legislative interest requires that. As for the person acting under coercion, he is not free, so it is not permissible to hold him liable, especially since the texts of the Shari'ah are explicit in not holding a person accountable for what he is coerced into. In our view, the majority opinion is more appropriate and more consistent with the wisdom of legislation. (1) A person [acting under] non-overwhelming coercion is liable for financial compensation, not the coercer, because non-overwhelming coercion is less severe relative to the gravity of the coerced act; it does not destroy the doer's free choice. If the act is not permissible even in necessity, such as killing another, then coercion does not permit it at all even if the coercion is under threat of death. But if he obeys the coercion and kills the intended victim, then the specific punishment—namely qisas—falls on the coercer, not on the doer.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage presents the majority opinion of the mujtahids opposing the Hanafi analogy between coercion (ikrah) and jest (hazl) in three specific transactions where jest is ineffective. The majority distinguishes: in jest, the person freely performs the action but does not intend the legal result, so the law can impose the effect regardless. In coercion, the person is not free in his action, so it is unjust to hold him liable. They support this with the clear texts that a coerced person is not held accountable. The text then discusses non-overwhelming coercion (ikrah ghayr mulji'): for permissible acts, the coerced person bears financial liability, not the coercer. For impermissible acts like murder, coercion does not permit the act, but if carried out, qisas is on the coercer. This is a rule for fatwa: the effect of coercion varies by type and severity.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will.
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or joking, where the words of a contract are uttered without intending their legal effect.
الإكراه غير الملجيء (al-ikrah ghayr al-mulji'): Non-overwhelming coercion that does not completely destroy free choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the coerced person is not free in his action, while the jester is.
B) Because jest is prohibited, while coercion is permissible.
C) Because the jester intends the legal effect, while the coerced does not.
D) Because the majority considers both to be ineffective.

Key Takeaway
The majority view distinguishes coercion from jest based on free choice, and this view is preferred for fatwa over the Hanafi analogy.

Parsing the Hadith of Khilabah: Deception and the Option of Stipulation
Defining khilabah and deriving rulings from the hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the hadith of Habban into two distinct rulings and identify the Hanafi and Hanbali interpretations, articulating the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The hadith of Habban contains two rulings: an absolute prohibition of deception giving a rescission option, and a permissive option of stipulation for deliberation.

This lesson takes you into the heart of a classical hadith that has shaped contract law in the Hanafi school. You will learn to parse the Prophet’s instruction to Habban into two distinct legal components: one prohibiting deception (khilabah) and the other permitting a stipulative option (khiyar al-shart). By examining the Hanafi and Hanbali approaches to this hadith, you will understand how the same text yields different rulings and how a mufti navigates such differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sentences in the hadith
Separate the Prophet's statement into its two parts: 'If you sell, say: no deception' and 'you have the option for three days'.
The first sentence is a prohibition, the second is a permission.
2
Classify each sentence by its legal effect
The first sentence prohibits khilabah (deception) - this establishes a haram act. The second sentence permits stipulating an option - this establishes a mubah act that requires a stipulation.
3
Derive the right to rescind from the prohibition
The prohibition of khilabah implies that the deceived party should have a remedy. By qiyas, this gives the deceived an option to annul (khiyar al-khilabah) to remove harm.
This is the Hanbali emphasis.
4
Distinguish the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart)
The second sentence explicitly allows stipulating an option for up to three days. This is not automatic; it requires agreement.
This is the Hanafi emphasis.
5
Compare Hanafi and Hanbali ijtihad
Hanafis focus on the second sentence, using the hadith primarily for khiyar al-shart. Hanbalis focus on the first sentence, deriving an automatic option for any deception. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the buyer can rescind without a stipulation.
Both readings are legitimate ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس والثلاثون
عيوب الرضا - الخلابة (١)
/١/٣٥ - الخلابة في العقد هي:
أن يخدع أحد العاقدين الآخر بوسيلة موهمة قولية أو فعلية تحمله على الرضا في العقد بما لم يكن ليرضى به لولاها.
وأصل فكرتها الفقهية ما ورد في الحديث النبوي أن حَبَّان (۲) بن المنقذ الأنصاري كان يُغبن في المبايعات فقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : «إذا بايعت فقل : لا خلابة ولي الخيار ثلاثة أيام (۳).
فهذا الحديث النبوي قد تضمن جملتين :
الأولى تمنع في المبايعة كل خديعة وهي الخلابة. وهي منع ثابت بحكم الشرع وإن لم يشترطه المتعاقد، وإنما ذكره للتنبيه والتحذير .. ومقتضى هذا المنع ثبوت الخيار للمخدوع : فإن شاء أمضى العقد وإن شاء أبطله إذا ثبتت الخديعة وظاهر أنَّ غير البيع نظير البيع في هذا الحكم، لأن الخديعة ممنوعة شرعاً في المعاملات بوجه عام، وفي جميع الأحوال. والمقصود من إيجاب هذا الخيار رفع الضرر الحاصل للعاقد من الخديعة.
والجملة الثانية تنص على خيار التروي المسمى : خيار الشرط وقد تقدَّم ذكره في أمثلة القياس. وهذا يحتاج إلى اشتراط لأن المقصود منه أن يأخذ العاقد المحتاج إلى التروي والمشورة فرصة كافية يتمكن بها من تفكر، وليس سببه أمراً ممنوعاً شرعاً كالخداع والغش وليس كل عاقد يحتاج إلى هذا التروي؛ فلذا لا يثبت هذا الخيار إلا لمن يشترطه من العاقدين. والخلابة حالة عامة من شوائب الإرادة العقدية لا تنحصر في صور ووسائل معينة بل كل وسيلة من وسائل التمويه على العاقد وإيهامه لحمله على التعاقد.
وقد تلقى جميع فقهاء الشريعة هذا الحديث بالقبول لكن اختلفت اتجاهاتهم في الاستنباط منه :
فمنهم من يجعله وارداً في جواز خيار الشرط في المعاوضات المالية، كما تقدمت الإشارة إليه في بحث القياس (ر) ف ٧/٣ تاسعاً) وهذا ما ذهب إليه الاجتهاد الحنفي ر: الهداية وفتح القدير، باب خيار الشرط من كتاب البيوع ٤٩٨/٥) وهذا الاتجاه تركيز للنظر على الجملة الثانية من الحديث المتضمنة الإرشاد إلى اشتراط الخيار مدة من الزمن للتروي.
ومنهم من ينظر أيضاً إلى الجملة الأولى على أنها ذات دلالة مستقلة، وهي منع الخلابة (أي الخديعة في المعاملات ويتخذون منه دليلاً على أن كل خديعة يلجأ إليها أحد المتبايعين تجاه الآخر ويترتب عليها زيادة في التزامات المخدوع أو نقص من حقه فإنها توجب له الخيار.
وهذا الاتجاه هو الظاهر في الاجتهاد الحنبلي (ر ): المغني مع الشرح الكبير على المقنع ٤ / ٦٩ - ٩٠ و ١١٣). ۹۰
Chapter Thirty-Five: Defects of Consent - Al-Khilabah (Deception).
/1/35 - Al-Khilabah in contract is: that one of the two contracting parties deceives the other by a misleading means, verbal or actual, which leads him to consent to what he would not have consented to without it.
The origin of its fiqhi concept is what is mentioned in the prophetic hadith that Habban ibn al-Munqidh al-Ansari used to be cheated in sales, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "When you make a sale, say: no deception, and you have the option for three days."
This prophetic hadith contains two sentences:
The first prohibits any deception in sale, which is al-khilabah. This is a prohibition established by Shari'ah even if the contractors do not stipulate it, and it is mentioned only for notification and warning. The consequence of this prohibition is the establishment of the option for the deceived: if he wishes, he ratifies the contract, and if he wishes, he annuls it, when deception is proven. It is apparent that non-sale transactions are analogous to sale in this ruling, because deception is prohibited by Shari'ah in transactions in general and in all circumstances. The purpose of establishing this option is to remove the harm caused to the contractor by the deception.
The second sentence stipulates the deliberative option, called the option of stipulation, which has been previously mentioned in examples of qiyas. This requires a stipulation, because its purpose is that the contractor in need of deliberation and consultation takes a sufficient opportunity by which he can think, and its cause is not something prohibited by Shari'ah like deception and fraud, and not every contractor needs this deliberation; therefore, this option is only established for those who stipulate it among the contractors. Al-Khilabah is a general condition of defects of contractual will, not limited to specific forms or means, but every means of deluding the contractor and misleading him to induce him to contract.
All fiqh scholars have accepted this hadith, but their directions in deriving rulings from it differed:
Among them are those who consider it to be about the permissibility of the option of stipulation in financial exchanges, as was referred to in the discussion of qiyas (see para 7/3 ninth), and this is what the Hanafi ijtihad adopted (see: Al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir, Chapter of Option of Stipulation from the Book of Sales, 5/498). This direction focuses on the second sentence of the hadith, which contains guidance to stipulate an option for a period for deliberation.
And among them are those who also look at the first sentence as having independent significance, which is the prohibition of khilabah (i.e., deception in transactions). They take from it evidence that every deception resorted to by one of the two parties towards the other, which results in an increase in the deceived's obligations or a reduction in his right, entitles him to the option.
This direction is the apparent one in Hanbali ijtihad (see: Al-Mughni with Al-Sharh al-Kabir 'ala al-Muqni', 4/69-90 and 113).
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of khilabah (deception) in contracts and traces its legal basis to a specific hadith. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by giving a precise definition: khilabah occurs when one party deceives the other through a misleading statement or action, inducing consent that would not have been given otherwise. The hadith of Habban ibn Munqidh provides the foundation; the Prophet instructed him to declare 'no deception' and granted him a three-day option. The author then parses the hadith into two distinct legal rulings: first, an absolute prohibition of deception in sales (and by analogy, all transactions) which gives the deceived party the right to rescind (khiyar al-khilabah). This option exists by virtue of Shari'ah, not by stipulation. Second, the hadith also mentions the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart), which is a deliberative option that must be explicitly agreed upon. The author highlights a madhhab difference: Hanafis focus on the second sentence (option of stipulation) and use this hadith primarily to support khiyar al-shart, whereas Hanbalis emphasize the first sentence (prohibition of deception) and derive a general option for any fraud. The passage thus illustrates a classic usul al-iftaa issue—how different madhabs extract different rulings from the same textual evidence.

Key Terms
الخلابة (al-khilabah): The act of deceiving the other contracting party by a misleading statement or action, inducing them to consent to something they would not have consented to otherwise.
لا خلابة (la khilabah): A declaration meaning 'no deception' that the Prophet advised Habban to say when selling, as a warning and reminder of the prohibition of deception.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option of stipulation: a right granted by agreement to one or both parties to rescind the contract within a specified period (up to three days) for deliberation.
خيار التروي (khiyar al-tarawwi): Deliberative option: another term for khiyar al-shart, emphasizing its purpose of allowing time to consider the deal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on using the hadith 'if you sell, say: no deception, and you have the option for three days'?
A) The hadith primarily establishes the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) for deliberation.
B) The hadith primarily grants an automatic option to rescind for any deception.
C) The hadith only applies to Habban and has no general legal significance.
D) The hadith prohibits deception but does not create any legal option.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Habban yields two distinct rulings: prohibition of deception (implying a rescission option) and permission to stipulate a deliberative option—Hanafis rely on the latter, Hanbalis on the former.

Defining Tadlis al-'Ayb and Establishing Khiyar al-'Ayb
Defines concealment of hidden defects and establishes the universal right of khiyar al-'ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the legal definition of tadlis al-'ayb to identify when a buyer has khiyar al-'ayb and construct a fatwa on a contract defect case.

Core Idea
Concealment of a hidden defect gives the buyer the right to rescind the contract, even if the seller was unaware.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa defines tadlis al-'ayb and establishes the universal right of khiyar al-'ayb based on ijma'. It clarifies that the seller's liability for hidden defects is strict, and discusses the nuanced interpretation of Abu Hanifa's position on the musarrah sheep. As a mufti, you must be able to parse this definition and apply the underlying legal maxims.

Guided Path
1
Define tadlis al-'ayb
Extract the elements: one party hides a known hidden defect in the subject matter from the other party in exchange contracts (sale, lease).
The concealment is active or passive; the defect must be hidden and known to the concealer.
2
Identify the legal consequence
The deceived party has the right to rescind the contract (khiyar al-'ayb) by ijma'.
This option exists even if the seller did not know the defect.
3
Determine the scope of seller liability
The seller is liable for any pre-existing defect found in the subject matter, unless a valid disclaimer (bara'ah) is stipulated.
The basis is that consent is premised on a defect-free item.
4
Evaluate the musarrah example
Understand the author's defense of Abu Hanifa: the ruling of no return applies only when the animal still has enough milk to be a dairy animal; otherwise, rescission is allowed because the missing attribute is like a stipulation.
This uses the maxim 'What is recognized by custom is like what is stipulated by condition.'
5
Apply the definition to a new case
Use the legal definition dissector to test a scenario: a car with a hidden engine defect.
Identify the defect, its concealment, and the resulting khiyar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعاً): تدليس العيب:
وأما تدليس العيب فهو كتمان أحد المتعاقدين عَيْباً خفياً يعلمه في محل العقد عن المتعاقد الآخر في عقود المعاوضة، كالبيع والإجارة. وفقهاء الإسلام مجمعون على أنَّ تدليس العيب يوجب للمتعاقد المدلَّس عليه حقاً في إبطال العقد يسمى خيار العيب، فيرد المبيع أو المأجور المعيب على البائع المدلس أو على المؤجر، ويفسخ العقد. وليس معنى ذلك أن المشتري ومن في معناه لا يثبت له خيار العيب إذا لم يكن البائع عالماً بالعيب عند البيع، بل الخيار يثبت شرعاً للمشتري على كل حال إذا ظهر في المبيع عيب كان خفياً على المشتري ولو لم يكن عالماً به عند البيع، لأنَّ المفروض في كل عقد أن رضا المتعاقد على أساس سلامة محل العقد من العيوب، وأن البائع متعاقد مسؤول بكل عيب قديم يظهر في هذا المحل وضامن له ما لم يشترط في العقد براءة البائع من هذا الضمان.
وإن المجلة لم تبحث في هذه المسألة، وكان عليها أن تتناولها وتختار رأي أبي يوسف
حق
وقد غلا بعض رجال المذاهب الأخرى، ومنهم ابن القيم في كتابه إعلام الموقعين في انتقاد قول أبي حنيفة بعدم رد الشاة المصراة. والذي أراه لزوم حمل رأي أبي حنيفة على ما إذا كانت الشاة، رغم التصرية، ذات لبن كاف لوصفها عادة بأنها لبون، وإنما التصرية فيها لإيهام زيادة في لبنها. أما إذا كانت بعد حلبها لا يجتمع لها لبن صالح لوصفها بأنها حلوب، فلا شك أن أبا حنيفة يقول بجواز فسخ العقد وردها، لأنّ إلزام المشتري بها عندئذ، ولو مع الرجوع بنقصان قيمتها ينافي قواعد مذهب أبي حنيفة نفسه في خيار فوات الوصف، فإنه يثبت الخيار بفوات الوصف المشروط شرطاً صريحاً أو ضمنياً. ولا شك أن الصفة الظاهرة الناشئة من التصرية هي في قوة اشتراط كون الدابة لبوناً. ومن قواعد مذهب أبي حنيفة أنّ المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً». ولا شك أن قواعد مذهب كل مجتهد هي التي يجب أن تحكم في تفسير وتفصيل بعض الآراء المروية عنه بصورة مجملة مطلقة
Fourth: Concealment of Defect (Tadlis al-'Ayb): As for concealment of a defect, it is when one of the contracting parties hides a hidden defect that he knows in the subject matter from the other contracting party in contracts of exchange, such as sale and lease. The jurists of Islam are unanimous that concealment of a defect gives the deceived contracting party a right to annul the contract called khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect), so he returns the defective sold item or leased item to the defrauding seller or lessor, and the contract is rescinded. This does not mean that the buyer and those like him do not have the option of defect if the seller was unaware of the defect at the time of sale; rather, the option is established legally for the buyer in any case if a defect appears in the sold item that was hidden from the buyer even if the seller was not aware of it at the time of sale, because the presumption in every contract is that the consent of the contracting party is based on the safety of the subject matter from defects, and the seller is a contracting party responsible for every pre-existing defect that appears in this subject matter and is a guarantor for it unless a disclaimer of this guarantee is stipulated in the contract. Al-Majalla did not discuss this issue, and it should have addressed it and chosen the opinion of Abu Yusuf. Some scholars of other schools have been extreme, among them Ibn al-Qayyim in his book I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, in criticizing the statement of Abu Hanifa that the musarrah sheep is not returned. What I see is that it is necessary to interpret Abu Hanifa's opinion as referring to the case where the sheep, despite the tasriyah, has sufficient milk to be normally described as a dairy animal, and the tasriyah was only to suggest an increase in its milk. But if after milking it does not have enough milk to qualify as a milch animal, then there is no doubt that Abu Hanifa would permit the rescission of the contract and its return, because obliging the buyer to retain it, even with a reduction in price, contradicts the rules of Abu Hanifa's own school regarding the option for missing description (khiyar fawat al-wasf), as the option is established by the loss of a description stipulated explicitly or implicitly. There is no doubt that the apparent attribute resulting from tasriyah is equivalent to stipulating that the animal is a milch animal. One of the rules of Abu Hanifa's school is: 'What is recognized by custom is like what is stipulated by condition.' There is no doubt that the rules of each mujtahid's school are what must govern the interpretation and elaboration of some opinions transmitted from him in a general and absolute manner.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage defines tadlis al-'ayb (concealment of a hidden defect) as an act by one party hiding a known defect in the subject matter from the other party in commutative contracts (sale, lease). The author states there is consensus (ijma') that such concealment gives the deceived party the right to rescind the contract – called khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). Critically, the author clarifies that this right exists even if the seller was unaware of the defect; the rationale is that consent is premised on the item being free of defects, and the seller is responsible for any pre-existing defect unless validly disclaimed. The author then criticizes al-Majalla for not addressing this issue, suggesting it should have adopted Abu Yusuf's view. The passage concludes with a defense of Abu Hanifa against Ibn al-Qayyim's criticism regarding the musarrah sheep. The author argues that Abu Hanifa's ruling of no rescission applies only when the animal still has sufficient milk to be considered a milch animal despite the tasriyah. If the animal has no milk at all, Abu Hanifa would allow rescission because the missing attribute (being milch) is equivalent to a stipulation, and the Hanafi legal maxim 'custom is like a stipulation' applies. Thus, the author harmonizes Abu Hanifa's reported opinion with the school's own rules on khiyar fawat al-wasf.

Key Terms
تدليس العيب (tadlis al-'ayb): Concealment of a hidden defect by one contracting party from the other in contracts of exchange.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to rescind a contract due to a pre-existing defect in the subject matter.
العيب القديم (al-'ayb al-qadim): A defect that existed in the subject matter at the time of contract, even if discovered later.
خيار فوات الوصف (khiyar fawat al-wasf): The option to rescind a contract due to the absence of a stipulated or implied attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the premise for establishing khiyar al-'ayb even when the seller did not know of the defect?
A) The seller is always negligent.
B) The buyer's consent is based on the assumption the item is defect-free.
C) The defect must be major to affect the price.
D) The seller must have guaranteed the item.

Key Takeaway
The buyer has a right to rescind the contract for any hidden pre-existing defect, regardless of the seller's knowledge, based on ijma and the implied condition of a defect-free subject matter.

Seller's Knowledge and the Exemption Clause in Sale
Distinguishing between knowing and unknowing seller regarding exemption from liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on exemption clauses to determine validity based on seller's knowledge of defect.

Core Idea
The seller's knowledge transforms a valid exemption clause into invalid circumvention.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents a critical refinement in the law of sale: a clause exempting the seller from liability for defects can be either valid or invalid depending on the seller's knowledge and intention. The mufti must investigate the seller's state of mind to issue a correct fatwa. The passage contrasts the Hanafi position with the strongest ijtihad views and highlights the difference between precaution and circumvention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seller's knowledge state
Determine whether the seller knew of the defect at the time of contract. This is the key fact that changes the ruling.
If the seller was ignorant, the exemption clause is valid by consensus.
2
Distinguish precaution from circumvention
If the seller did not know, the clause is precaution (ihtiyat) and valid. If the seller knew and concealed, the clause is circumvention (ihtiyal) and invalid according to the strongest view.
The Hanafi school does not make this distinction; they validate the clause in both cases.
3
Apply the strongest ijtihad view
The author states that in the strongest ijtihad views, a knowing seller cannot benefit from an exemption clause because it is fraudulent.
This view is attributed to Caliph Uthman and adopted by modern civil law.
4
Note the Hanafi contrary position
The Hanafi school holds the clause valid even with knowledge, but this is considered weaker by the author.
The mufti must be aware of this ikhtilaf and the hierarchy of views.
5
Issue the fatwa accordingly
In a Hanafi fatwa context, one may follow the traditional Hanafi view, but the stronger view may be adopted for taqwa or in contemporary practice.
The author's preference is for the stronger view, but he presents both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن البائع ومن في حكمه إذا كان عالماً بالعيب فكتمه كان مدلساً للعيب وداخلاً تحت حكم الخلابة. أما إذا لم يكن البائع عالماً بالعيب فهو ضامن له أيضاً بمقتضى العقد، ولكنه ليس بمدلس. فالفرق بين تدليس العيب وعدمه لا يظهر بالنسبة إلى ضمان العيب لأن ضمانه حكم مشترك في الحالين وإنما يظهر في بعض أحكام أخرى لها طابع الجزاء، كما لو شَرَط البائع في عقد البيع براءته من ضمان العيب، أي عدم مسؤوليته عما يمكن أن يظهر من عيوب في المبيع)، فرضي المشتري بهذا الشرط اعتماداً على السلامة الظاهرة، ثم ظهر في المبيع عيب قديم: أ - ففي هذه الحال إذا كان البائع جاهلاً وجود العيب في مبيعه، وإنما شَرَط هذا الشرط للاحتياط، فشرطه هذا صحيح مسقط لضمان العيب عن عهدته باتفاق المذاهب الاجتهادية فيعتبر المشتري مشترياً على مسؤولية نفسه كما لو كان عالماً بعيب المبيع فاشتراه راضياً به. - أما إذا كان البائع عالماً بعيب المبيع فكتمه عن المشتري، واشترط البراءة من ضمان العيب ليحمي بهذا الشرط سوء نيته، ففي أقوى الاجتهادات لا يصح شرطه هذا ويبقى خاضعاً للضمان، لأن شرطه هذا للاحتيال لا للاحتياط، فهو سيىء النية بشرطه هذا فلا يجوز أن يستفيد منه (۱) . (۱) بهذا قضى الخليفة الثالث سيدنا عثمان رضي الله عنه، وبهذا أخذ قانوننا المدني الحالي والاجتهاد الحنفي على خلاف ذلك يصحح هذا الشرط ويعفي به البائع من ضمان العيب رغم علمه بالعيب وسوء نيته ر) كتابي عقد البيع في الشريعة الإسلامية وهو الجزء الرابع من هذه السلسلة ف / ۱۲۷/ وبداية المجتهد لابن رشد ٤٦٧ .(١٥٣/٢ - ١٥٤
But the seller and whoever is in his position, if he knew of the defect and concealed it, he is a concealor of the defect and falls under the ruling of deceit (khilabah). As for if the seller was not aware of the defect, he is also liable for it by virtue of the contract, but he is not a concealor. The difference between concealment of the defect and its absence does not appear with respect to liability for the defect because liability is a common ruling in both cases; rather it appears in some other rulings that have a punitive nature, such as if the seller stipulates in the contract his immunity from liability for defect, i.e., his non-responsibility for whatever defects may appear in the sold item. Then the buyer agrees to this stipulation relying on apparent safety. Then an old defect appears in the sold item: (a) In this case, if the seller was ignorant of the existence of the defect in his sold item and only stipulated this condition for precaution, then this condition is valid and removes liability for the defect from him by agreement of the ijtihad schools. The buyer is considered to have bought at his own responsibility, as if he knew of the defect and bought it willingly. (b) But if the seller knew of the defect in the sold item and concealed it from the buyer, and stipulated immunity from liability for the defect to protect his bad faith with this condition, then in the strongest ijtihad views, this condition is not valid and he remains subject to liability, because this condition is for circumvention (ihtiyal) not precaution (ihtiyat). He is ill-intentioned with this condition, so he cannot benefit from it. (1) (1) This was the ruling of the third caliph, our master Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, and our current civil law adopts it. The Hanafi ijtihad, on the contrary, validates this condition and absolves the seller from liability for the defect despite his knowledge of the defect and his bad faith. (Reference: my book 'The Contract of Sale in Islamic Law' which is Part 4 of this series, p. 127; and Bidayat al-Mujtahid by Ibn Rushd, 2/153-154, p. 467.)
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the ruling on a seller's stipulation of exemption from liability for defects, distinguishing the case where the seller is aware of the defect versus unaware. The author states that if the seller is unaware, the stipulation is valid by agreement among the schools of ijtihad. However, if the seller knows of the defect and conceals it, the majority of strong ijtihad views invalidate the stipulation, considering it a form of circumvention (ihtiyal) rather than precaution (ihtiyat). The text notes that the Hanafi school differs, where the stipulation would still be valid even if the seller knows of the defect. This is a rasm rule: the ruling depends on the seller's knowledge and intention. The difference between concealment (tadlis) and ignorance does not affect the seller's liability for damages, which is present in both cases; rather it affects punitive rulings such as the validity of an exemption clause.

Key Terms
خلابة (khilabah): Deception or cheating in a contract that affects consent.
شرط البراءة (shart al-bara'ah): A clause in a sale contract where the seller disclaims liability for any defects.
تدليس (tadlis): Concealment of a defect with knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a seller's stipulation of exemption from liability for defect if the seller is aware of the defect but conceals it?
A) The stipulation is valid and absolves the seller.
B) The stipulation is invalid in the strongest ijtihad views.
C) The stipulation is valid only if the buyer agrees.
D) The stipulation is always invalid.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an exemption clause in sale hinges on the seller's knowledge of the defect: if known, the clause is invalid in the strongest view, but Hanafis hold it valid.

Clear Error in Contracts: Genus vs. Description
Rule on clear error in contracts with genus/description distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an error in a contract is clear (wadih) or hidden, and apply the Hanafi ruling on its effect (obstructive vs. option to annul).

Core Idea
A clear error in genus voids the contract; a clear error in description gives an option only if the subject matter was unseen.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa lays out the Hanafi rule on clear error (al-ghalat al-wadih) in contracts. The key distinction is between error in genus (jins) and error in description (wasf). Understanding this rule is essential for a mufti when evaluating whether a contract can be annulled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of clear error
Read the passage to find the two ways an error can be 'clear': (a) the mistaken party explicitly reveals his intent, or (b) intent appears from circumstantial evidence. The passage then divides clear errors into those affecting genus (jins) and those affecting description (wasf).
The passage uses the example of specifying genus in the contract.
2
Apply the ruling for genus error
When error is in genus (e.g., buying a stone as ruby but it is glass), the contract does not conclude at all. This is called obstructive error (ghalat mani').
This is because the subject matter is effectively non-existent.
3
Distinguish description error by observability
For error in description, the ruling depends on whether the subject matter was present and observable. If absent or unobservable, the buyer has khiyar al-wasf (option to annul). If present and observable, the contract is binding because the error is not clear.
The passage gives examples of broadcloth quality and a horse's color.
4
Recognize the hidden error exception
The passage contrasts with error in identity (e.g., selling one horse thinking it is another). This is considered hidden error because the contract's apparent will was directed to a specific item; the error is not clear, so no effect.
This is to protect transactional stability.
5
Apply the principle in a fatwa scenario
As a mufti, first determine if the error is clear (explicit or apparent). If clear, check if it is in genus → void; if in description and item unseen → option; if item seen → binding. If hidden → no effect.
The underlying maxim: 'A clear error affects the contract; a non-clear error does not.'

واضحة. فإعطاء الغالط في هذه الحال حق الإبطال هو الذي يفرضه مبدأ احترام الإرادة العقدية، ولا يخل باستقرار التعامل، لأن العاقد الآخر يكون على بينة من غلط رفيقه، فلا يكون في الإبطال مفاجأة له.
٣/٣٦ - كيف يكون غلط العاقد واضحاً:
والظاهر من تتبع نصوص الفقهاء أنَّ غلط العاقد في عقده يعتبر واضحاً إذا كشف العاقد بنفسه عن مراده كشفاً صريحاً خلال التعاقد أو كان مراده مكشوفاً ظاهراً من القرائن والدلائل. فهاتان حالتان :
٤/٣٦ - (الحالة الأولى) : كشف العاقد عن مراده بنفسه كشفاً صريحاً :
فأما حالة كشف العاقد الغالط عن مراده بنفسه صراحة فكما إذا عين في العقد جنس المعقود عليه، أو إذا وصفه بصفة، ثم ظهر على خلاف ذلك. وفي هذه الحال للفقهاء حكم تفصيلي حول درجة تأثير الغلط في العقد :
( أ ) فإذا كان الغلط واقعاً في جنس المعقود عليه، كما لو باع أو اشترى فص خاتم على أنه ياقوت فإذا هو زجاج، أو اشترى كيساً من الأرز فإذا هو قمح، فإنَّ العقد لا ينعقد أصلاً في الاجتهاد الحنفي، لأن اختلاف الجنس يجعل المحل المعقود عليه معدوماً، وهو أحد مقومات العقد المتقدمة الذكر (١/٢٩).
(١) وهذا ما يشترطه الفقه الأجنبي في اتجاهه الحديث بنظرية الغلط، حيث يشترط علماء القانون لجواز إبطال العقد بسبب غلط أحد العاقدين أن يكون العاقد الآخر على علم بغلط رفيقه . (٢) وهذا نظير ما يسميه علماء القانون الغلط المانع Erreur obstacle أي الذي يمنع انعقاد العقد. ومن صوره عندهم أن يغلط العاقد في ذات الشيء المعقود عليه، كما لو باع إحدى فرسيه والمشتري يظن أنه قد اشترى الأخرى. وهذا النوع، أي الغلط المانع لا يعد في عداد عيوب الرضا لأنه يمنع الانعقاد، بينما :
(ب) وأما إذا كان الغلط في الوصف فإنه يميز في حکمه بین حالتين :
١ - إذا كان المعقود عليه معيناً بالذات لكنه غائب عن مجلس العقد، أو كان حاضراً فيه لكن العاقد لا يدرك وصفه بالمعاينة، كما لو باع نسيجاً معيناً من الجوخ على أنه إنكليزي المصدر، أو باعه على أنه أحمر وكان المشتري أعمى أو كان البيع ليلاً في الظلام، ثم تبيَّن أن المبيع على خلاف الوصف الذي سمي في العقد، فإنَّ المشتري مخير في إبطال العقد، لفوات وصف مشروط في محل العقد، فلا يعتبر رضاه سليماً بفواته. وهذا يسمى خيار الوصف.
٢ - وإذا كان المعقود عليه حاضراً في مجلس العقد مكشوفاً مشاراً إليه تحت مشاهدة العاقد وكانت صفته مما يدرك بهذه المشاهدة كالألوان والحجوم بأن قال البائع مثلاً : بعتك هذا المهر الأبيض الصغير بكذا، وهو حصان أسود كبير ؛ أو قال بعتك هذه الفرس المحجلة؛ وهي غير محجلة، أو هذه السيارة الخضراء وهي في الواقع سوداء، فقبل المشتري = أن عيوب الرضا كلها شوائب فرعية ينعقد معها العقد لكمال مقوماته، ولكنها تجعل العقد قابلاً للإبطال بمشيئة الطرف الذي شيب رضاه بأحد تلك الشوائب. ويلحظ في هذا المقام أن اختلاف الجنس الذي يعتبر في الفقه الإسلامي من قبيل الغلط المانع لا يعتبر في النظر القانوني غلطاً مانعاً، بل هو من الغلط الذي يعيب الرضا فقط، فينعقد معه العقد قابلاً للإبطال .
وعكس ذلك يلحظ في الغلط في ذاتية المبيع في المثال الآنف الذكر، (لو باع إحدى فرسيه، وهو أو المشتري يظن أنها الأخرى فإنه في الفقه القانوني غلط مانع لا ينعقد معه العقد، وفي الفقه الإسلامي هو غلط غير مؤثر أصلاً، فينعقد معه العقد لازماً لا خيار فيه، لأن الإرادة الظاهرة بالإيجاب والقبول لما وردت في العقد على واحدة معينة من الفرسين انصرف العقد إليها وبقي الغلط في حيز الخفاء ليس عليه دليل قائم، فلا يكون غلطاً واضحاً، فلا يؤثر، حرصاً على استقرار التعامل. بخلاف مسألة اختلاف الجنس، فإن تسمية الجنس المقصود في صلب العقد، تجعل الغلط في الجنس المخالف غلطاً واضحاً، فيؤثر في العقد. وبما أن اختلاف الجنس في حكم فقدان العين لا ينعقد العقد لعدم المحل.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence
Clear. So giving the mistaken party in this case the right to annul is what the principle of respect for contractual will requires, and it does not disrupt the stability of transactions, because the other contracting party is aware of his companion's mistake, so annulment is not a surprise to him.
3/36 - How the contractor's mistake is clear:
It appears from tracing the texts of the jurists that the mistake of a contractor in his contract is considered clear if the contractor himself reveals his intent explicitly during the contract, or if his intent is disclosed and apparent from circumstantial evidence and indications. These are two situations:
4/36 - (First situation): The contractor reveals his intent himself explicitly:
As for the case where the mistaken contractor reveals his intent himself explicitly, such as when he specifies in the contract the genus of the subject matter, or describes it with a description, and then it turns out otherwise. In this case, the jurists have a detailed ruling on the degree of the effect of the mistake on the contract:
(a) If the mistake is in the genus of the subject matter, such as if he sells or buys a ring stone taking it to be ruby but it is glass, or buys a bag of rice but it is wheat, then the contract does not conclude at all in the Hanafi ijtihad, because the difference in genus makes the subject matter non-existent, and it is one of the essential elements of the contract previously mentioned (1/29).
(1) This is what foreign jurisprudence requires in its modern trend on the theory of mistake, where legal scholars stipulate for allowing annulment of a contract due to the mistake of one of the contracting parties that the other contracting party be aware of his companion's mistake. (2) This is analogous to what legal scholars call obstructive error (Erreur obstacle), i.e., that which prevents the conclusion of the contract. Its examples according to them include the contractor being mistaken about the identity of the thing contracted upon, such as if he sells one of his two horses and the buyer thinks he has bought the other. This type, i.e., obstructive error, is not counted among defects of consent because it prevents conclusion, while:
(b) As for if the mistake is in the description, its ruling is distinguished between two situations:
1 - If the subject matter is identified by itself but is absent from the session of contract, or is present in it but the contractor does not perceive its description by sight, such as if he sells a specific piece of broadcloth assuming it is English-made, or sells it assuming it is red while the buyer is blind or the sale is at night in darkness, then it turns out that the sold item is contrary to the description mentioned in the contract; then the buyer has the choice to annul the contract, due to the loss of a conditionally stipulated description in the contract's subject matter, so his consent is not considered sound with its loss. This is called the option of description.
2 - If the subject matter is present in the contract session, uncovered and pointed to under the observation of the contractor, and its description is such that it can be perceived by this observation, like colors and sizes, such as if the seller says, for example: 'I sell you this small white foal for such-and-such,' and it is a large black horse; or he says, 'I sell you this fetlocked mare,' and she is not fetlocked; or 'this green car' and it is actually black, and the buyer accepts = that all defects of consent are secondary flaws; with them the contract is concluded because its essential elements are complete, but they make the contract subject to annulment at the will of the party whose consent was tainted by one of those flaws. It is noted in this regard that difference in genus, which in Islamic jurisprudence is considered a type of obstructive error, is not considered obstructive error in legal theory; rather, it is an error that only vitiates consent, so with it the contract is concluded subject to annulment.
The opposite is noted regarding mistake in the identity of the sold item in the aforementioned example (if he sells one of his two horses and he or the buyer thinks it is the other: in legal theory it is an obstructive error with which the contract does not conclude, while in Islamic jurisprudence it is a completely ineffective error; with it the contract is concluded as binding with no option, because the apparent will by offer and acceptance when directed in the contract to one specific of the two horses, the contract is directed to it, and the mistake remains hidden with no evidence standing, so it is not a clear error, so it has no effect, out of concern for stability of transactions. Contrast this with the issue of difference in genus: specifying the intended genus in the body of the contract makes the error in the contrary genus a clear error, so it affects the contract. And since difference in genus is in the ruling of loss of the thing, the contract does not conclude for lack of subject matter.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the Hanafi ruling on clear error (al-ghalat al-wadih) in contracts. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that an error is considered 'clear' if the mistaken party explicitly reveals his intent during the contract, or if his intent is apparent from circumstantial evidence. He then distinguishes between two types of error: error in the genus (jins) of the subject matter and error in description (wasf). 

When the error is in the genus, such as buying a stone thinking it is ruby but it is glass, the Hanafi school holds that the contract does not conclude at all because the subject matter (mahall) is effectively non-existent. This is analogous to what Western legal scholars call 'obstructive error' (Erreur obstacle), but note the difference: in Islamic law, genus error prevents formation, while in Western law it is merely a defect of consent. 

When the error is in a description, the ruling depends on whether the subject matter was present and observable at the time of contract. If it was absent or unobservable (e.g., the buyer was blind or it was dark), the buyer has the option to annul the contract (khiyar al-wasf). If it was present and observable, such that the description could be verified by sight, then the error is not considered clear because the visible reality contradicts the stated description; here the contract is binding with no option, as the visible fact takes precedence. 

The underlying principle is that clear errors affect the contract to protect the stability of transactions, while hidden errors do not. This passage also contrasts Islamic and Western legal theories on this point, highlighting the Hanafi emphasis on apparent consent and transactional security.

Key Terms
الغلط (al-ghalat): A mistake that leads to a misunderstanding about the subject matter or its description in a contract.
الغلط الواضح (al-ghalat al-wadih): A mistake that is apparent either from the explicit statement of the party or from surrounding circumstances.
خيار الوصف (khiyar al-wasf): The option to annul a contract when the description fails, provided the subject matter was absent or unobservable.
الغلط المانع (al-ghalat al-mani'): A mistake that prevents the contract from being formed (e.g., error in genus in Hanafi fiqh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi jurisprudence, what is the effect of a mistake in the genus (jins) of the subject matter, such as buying a ring stone thinking it is ruby but it is glass?
A) The contract is valid but the buyer has the option to annul (khiyar al-wasf).
B) The contract does not conclude at all because the subject matter is non-existent.
C) The contract is binding with no option because the error is hidden.
D) The contract is suspended until the parties renegotiate.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only clear errors (genus or description where item was unseen) affect the contract; hidden errors are ignored to protect transactional stability.

Exceptional Case: Option of Sight as Excused Error
Discussing the exceptional case where error is excused even if hidden: the option of sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the legal basis for khiyar al-ru'ya as an exception to the rule that non-obvious error does not affect contracts, and apply the Hanafi ruling in sales of unseen items.

Core Idea
The option of sight is an exception: Islamic law presumes error when an item is unseen.

You have learned that hidden error generally does not affect a contract. But there is a key exception: khiyar al-ru'ya. In this lesson, we examine why Islamic law grants the right to rescind a contract when the object was not seen, and how this fits into the larger framework of legal rules.

Guided Path
1
Restate the general rule
Recall the principle that non-obvious (hidden) error does not invalidate a contract. This is the baseline.
Refer to the previous lesson on error.
2
Identify the exception
Note that khiyar al-ru'ya is an exception: the contract of sale for an unseen item can be rescinded because the law presumes possible error.
The exception is based on the likelihood of error (ghalat muhtamal).
3
Analyze the rationale
The passage states that 'the sale does not bind the seller and the purchase does not bind the buyer, because the intent is disclosed by the naming or stipulation.' This means that by specifying the item, the parties implicitly intend to see it; if they are mistaken, the error is considered obvious in that context.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the nature of the transaction.
4
Apply to furu' cases
Consider the cases in the qawida panel: a buyer of an unseen item has khiyar al-ru'ya; similarly, a seller may have it in some opinions. Practice applying to these scenarios.
Use the furuat from the toolbox.
5
Note the istithna' (exception to the exception)
The option of sight does not apply if the item was seen before the contract or described in a way that removes uncertainty. That would negate the presumption of error.
This preserves the general rule against uncertainty (gharar).

7/36 - Exceptional case in which error is considered an excuse even if hidden:
Option of Sight:
There is a case that Islamic law considered a likely place for error of the contracting party; so it granted him the right to annul if he wishes because of the possibility of his error, and that is the case of buying something he has not seen.
The sale does not bind the seller and the purchase does not bind the buyer, because the intent is disclosed by the naming or stipulation, so the error is obvious. (See: Masadir al-Haqq by Professor al-Sanhuri, p. 123).
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents an exceptional rule to the previous principle: the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya). In a sale of an unseen item, Islamic law presumes that the contracting party may be mistaken about the object, so it grants him the right to rescind the contract upon seeing it. This is considered an excused error even though the error is hidden at the time of contract, because the law recognizes that the true intent (to see the item) is implicit. The reasoning is that the seller's or buyer's intent is 'disclosed by the naming or stipulation'—meaning by specifying what is being sold, they indicate a desire to see it, so any error becomes obvious upon inspection. This is an exception to the general rule that hidden error does not affect the contract.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option for a party to cancel a contract upon seeing the object of the contract if it was unseen at the time of contract.
الغلط المحتمل (al-ghalat al-muhtamal): An error that is likely to occur, given the circumstances of not seeing the object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the error in such cases is always obvious.
B) Because Islamic law presumes error in unseen sales, granting rescission.
C) Because the buyer has a right to regret any purchase.
D) Because the seller is always bound by the contract.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'ya is an exception based on the presumption of error in sales of unseen items, allowing rescission until inspection.

Buyer's Option When Item is Mortgaged or Leased
Ruling on buyer's option when sold item is found to be mortgaged or leased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'A contract between two parties cannot affect rights acquired by others' to determine the buyer's option when a sold item is encumbered by a mortgage or lease.

Core Idea
A sale cannot override pre-existing third-party rights; the buyer may wait or rescind.

When a buyer discovers that the item he purchased is mortgaged to a creditor or leased to a tenant, his ability to take immediate possession is blocked. This disruption of performance (ikhtilal al-tanfidh) is treated as a defect in consent, giving the buyer a khiyar (option) similar to khiyar al-'ayb. Understanding this ruling requires applying a key legal maxim about the inviolability of third-party rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-existing rights
Recognize that the mortgagee has a right to retain the item until debt repayment, and the lessee has a right to use the item for the lease term. These rights are protected and cannot be invalidated by a later sale.
These are rights in rem (ayn) against the property itself.
2
Apply the core maxim
State the maxim: 'A contract between two parties cannot affect rights acquired by others.' The sale contract between seller and buyer does not nullify the prior rights of the mortgagee or lessee.
The maxim is wusta (intermediate) and governs sales involving third-party rights.
3
Determine the consequence for performance
Since the third-party rights prevent immediate delivery, the sale's performance is disrupted. The buyer cannot demand possession until the encumbrance is removed.
This disruption is considered a defect in contractual performance (ikhtilal al-tanfidh).
4
Classify the defect by analogy
The disruption of consent due to non-delivery is analogous to a physical defect (khiyar al-'ayb). Imam al-Kasani explains that the buyer's desire for soundness (including freedom from encumbrances) was unfulfilled, so his consent is vitiated.
The analogy is based on the shared illa: the buyer's consent is based on an assumed state that is absent.
5
State the buyer's options
The buyer may either wait until the lease expires or the mortgage is released, or demand annulment (faskh) of the sale. This is the mufta bihi position.
The option is a khiyar established by the Hanafi school, not by specific textual evidence but by qiyas and istihsan.

4/37 - (c) Appearance of the sold item as mortgaged or leased: If a person sells something, and then it becomes apparent that the sold item is mortgaged to another person, or leased to him and the lease period has not yet expired, and the buyer does not know that at the time of purchase, he has an option (khiyar). This is because the general principle in contracts is that a contract between two parties cannot affect rights acquired by others, because the contracting parties have authority only over their own rights. (1) We remind here of what we previously transmitted from Imam al-Kasani in the commentary on paragraph (5/36) of his statement in explaining the option of defect: '...And because soundness was desired by the buyer and it was not achieved, his consent was disrupted.' Chapter (37) Defects of consent due to disruption of contract performance: In our example, the lessee's right to the benefit of the leased item for the remainder of the lease period, and the creditor-mortgagee's right to retain the mortgaged item until repayment of the debt, are both protected rights that are not invalidated by the sale of the owner-lessor or mortgagor. The result is that the buyer cannot demand delivery of the item he purchased until the lease expires or the mortgage is released. Thus, the sale performance is disrupted, and consequently the buyer's consent is disrupted, as he only bought on the basis that he would receive the item immediately, unaware that it was mortgaged or leased. Therefore, the jurists consider this situation a defect affecting the buyer's consent, granting him an option to either wait for the lease to end or the mortgage to be released to receive the item, or to demand annulment of the sale. (Ref: Majallah articles 590 and 747).
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling: when a sold item is found to be mortgaged or leased to a third party without the buyer's knowledge, the buyer has an option (khiyar). The reasoning is based on the general principle that a contract between two parties cannot infringe upon the pre-existing rights of third parties. The lessee's right to use the item and the mortgagee's right to retain the item are inviolable. Consequently, the buyer cannot obtain immediate possession, which disrupts the performance of the sale and vitiates his consent. The author cites Imam al-Kasani to reinforce that the buyer's desire for soundness (including freedom from encumbrances) was unfulfilled, leading to disrupted consent. The option allows the buyer to either wait for the encumbrance to clear or rescind the contract. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that defects in performance (ikhtilal al-tanfidh) are treated similarly to defects in the subject matter.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A mortgage or pledge; a right in rem that allows the creditor to retain the item until debt repayment.
الإجارة (al-ijarah): Lease; a contract for temporary use of an item for a fee.
اختلال التنفيذ (ikhtilal al-tanfidh): Disruption of contractual performance due to external factors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal basis for granting the buyer an option when the sold item is found to be mortgaged?
A) The mortgage contract becomes void upon sale.
B) The buyer's consent is disrupted because he cannot take immediate possession.
C) The seller committed fraud by not disclosing the mortgage.
D) The mortgagee's rights are automatically transferred to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The inviolability of pre-existing third-party rights grants the buyer an option when a sale's performance is disrupted.

Limits of Judicial Compulsion in Contractual Conditions
Ruling on when a party refuses to fulfill a condition that cannot be judicially enforced

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a condition in a contract cannot be judicially enforced according to Hanafi fiqh and explain the reasoning based on the nature of the obligation.

Core Idea
A condition requiring a voluntary act, such as giving a pledge, cannot be enforced by court because consent cannot be compelled.

In contracts, valid conditions are generally enforceable by the court. However, certain conditions involve acts that require mutual consent and physical delivery—acts that cannot be coerced. This passage explores a key exception in Hanafi fiqh: when a party refuses to fulfill a condition like providing a pledge or a guarantor, the court cannot compel performance. Understanding this nuance is essential for a mufti when assessing the enforceability of contractual stipulations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Recall that valid contractual conditions are ordinarily enforceable by judicial compulsion. The passage states this as the baseline rule.
General reference to earlier paragraph (137).
2
Recognize the exception
Note that some conditions involve acts that, by nature, cannot be compelled. The passage provides the example of providing a pledge (rahn) or a guarantor (kafil).
These acts require voluntary consent and delivery.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the exception
Understand that a pledge contract is a real contract (عقد عيني) that does not become binding without delivery. Similarly, a guarantor cannot be forced to consent. Therefore, judicial compulsion is impossible.
The key is the nature of the act: it requires a new consensual contract.
4
Apply the exception to the scenario
In the given case, the buyer refuses to provide the specific pledge or guarantor. Determine that the court cannot compel the buyer because the required act (giving rahn or kafala) depends on the buyer's consent and delivery.
The condition itself is valid, but enforcement is not possible.
5
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
The preponderant opinion (al-ra'y al-fiqhi al-rajih) is that the buyer cannot be judicially forced. The remedy lies elsewhere (e.g., voiding the contract or seeking alternatives).
This ruling is based on the nature of rahn as a contract in rem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥/٣٧ - (د) امتناع العاقد عن الوفاء بشرط لا يمكن الإجبار فيه :
العاقد إذا امتنع عن الوفاء بشرط صحيح اشترطه عليه العاقد الآخر في العقد، فالأصل في ذلك أن يجبر الممتنع عن تنفيذ الشرط بسلطان القضاء، كما تقدمت الإشارة إليه ف (۱۳۷) وهذا ما عليه الاجتهاد الحنفي واجتهادات أخرى.
ولكن قد تعترض هذا النظر صور وحوادث لا يمكن بحسب طبيعتها الإجبار القضائي فيها وذلك كما لو باع شخص شيئاً بثمن مؤجل إلى أجل، واشترط على المشتري أن يعطيه رهناً معيناً أو أن يكفله كفيل معين، و انبرم العقد على هذا الشرط، ثم امتنع المشتري عن تقديم الرهن أو الكفيل، أو رفض الشخص الثالث أن يكفل .
فالرأي الفقهي الراجح في هذه الحال أنه لا يمكن إجبار المشتري قضائياً على إعطاء الرهن المطلوب. ذلك لأنَّ الرهن عقد عيني كالتبرع فلا تاماً ولازماً في حق الراهن إلا بالتنفيذ والتسليم. وهو على كل حال يصير عقد لا ينعقد إلا بالتراضي، ولا يمكن انتزاع الرضا جبراً على صاحبه.
5/37 - (d) Refusal of the contracting party to fulfill a condition that cannot be enforced: If a contracting party refuses to fulfill a valid condition stipulated by the other party in the contract, the principle is that the refusing party is compelled to execute the condition by judicial authority, as previously indicated in paragraph (137), and this is the Hanafi ijtihad and other ijtihads. However, there may arise instances and occurrences that by their nature cannot be subject to judicial compulsion, such as when a person sells something with a deferred price and stipulates that the buyer give him a specific pledge (rahn) or provide a specific guarantor (kafil), and the contract is concluded on this condition, then the buyer refuses to provide the pledge or guarantor, or the third party refuses to act as guarantor. The preponderant legal opinion in this case is that the buyer cannot be judicially compelled to give the required pledge. This is because the pledge is a real contract (contract in rem) like a donation; it does not become complete and binding on the pledgor except by execution and delivery. In any case, it is a contract that is not concluded except by mutual consent, and consent cannot be forcibly extracted from its owner.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case in contract law: a party's refusal to fulfill a condition that is valid but involves a voluntary act, such as giving a pledge or providing a guarantor. The general principle is that courts can compel performance of contractual conditions. However, Hanafi jurists recognize an exception: conditions that require a consensual act (like a pledge contract) cannot be judicially enforced because such contracts require mutual consent and physical delivery. The pledge (rahn) is a real contract that only becomes binding upon delivery; similarly, a guarantor cannot be forced to agree. Therefore, if a buyer refuses to provide a pledged item or a guarantor as stipulated, the court cannot compel him. The ruling is that the condition becomes unenforceable, and the other party's remedy is limited (the contract may be voidable or other options may exist, but the text focuses on the impossibility of compulsion). This is a nuance in Hanafi fiqh regarding the enforceability of contractual conditions.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge; a contract in rem that requires delivery to become binding.
الإجبار القضائي (al-ijbar al-qada'i): Judicial compulsion to force a party to fulfill an obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a buyer not be judicially compelled to give a specific pledge as stipulated in a sale contract?
A) Because the condition was invalid from the start.
B) Because a pledge contract requires mutual consent and delivery to be binding.
C) Because the seller waived the right to compulsion.
D) Because the court lacks jurisdiction over sales.

Key Takeaway
A valid condition requiring a consensual act like pledge or guarantee is not judicially enforceable because consent and delivery cannot be compelled.

Distinguishing Disturbance from Breach of Execution
Analytical distinction between two types of execution failure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between disturbance (ikhtilāl) and breach (ikhlāl) of execution in Hanafi contract law and apply the ruling on consent vitiation to determine whether annulment is permitted.

Core Idea
Disturbance always vitiates consent; breach only vitiates if specific performance cannot be compelled.

This lesson explores a key distinction in Hanafi contract law: how a failure to execute a contract affects the consent of the parties. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, clarifies that not all execution failures are equal. Some are external disturbances that make performance impossible, while others are willful breaches by a capable party. The Hanafi school has a nuanced rule: the former always gives the right to annul, while the latter only does so if the court cannot compel performance. This understanding is fundamental for a mufti when advising on contract disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of execution failure
State the definition of ikhtilāl (disturbance) and ikhlāl (breach) as given in the text. Note that disturbance is caused by an external event, while breach is a willful refusal.
Disturbance: e.g., the sold item is destroyed. Breach: e.g., the buyer refuses to pay despite being able to.
2
Determine the effect on consent for each
For disturbance: consent is always vitiated, giving the other party the right to annul. For breach: it depends on whether specific performance can be judicially compelled.
This is the core distinction.
3
Apply the test of judicial compellability for breach
Ask: Can the court force the breaching party to perform exactly as agreed? If yes, consent is not vitiated and the contract stands. If no, the breach becomes like a disturbance and annulment is permitted.
Example: buyer refuses to provide a guarantor—court cannot compel that personal action, so annulment is allowed.
4
State the outcome: annulment or no annulment
Based on the above, determine whether the non-breaching party can annul (ibṭāl) or must continue with the contract.
If annulment is allowed, it is due to a consent defect. If not, the contract remains binding and the party can seek compulsion.
5
Note any intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar disagreed on the buyer's refusal to provide a guarantor: he held that the condition becomes a right enforceable by court, so no annulment. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) held the opposite.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
ومثل ذلك يقال فيما إذا امتنع المشتري عن تقديم الكفيل المشروط . وعندئذ يختل تنفيذ البيع اختلالاً لا يمكن تلافيه عن طريق القضاء، فيختل تبعاً له الرضا السابق من البائع، لأنه لم يبع إلا على أساس تقديم الرهن أو الكفيل فيمنح حق إبطال البيع إن شاء.
وعلى هذا يجب التمييز بين اختلال التنفيذ والإخلال به :
فاختلال التنفيذ هو ما يكون نتيجة لحادث لا مجال معه لتنفيذ العقد على الشكل الذي تم عليه التراضي، وذلك كما في حالة تفرق الصفقة بهلاك بعض المبيع أو استحقاقه وهذا الاختلال يعيب الرضا دائماً ويسوع إبطال العقد.
- أما الإخلال بالتنفيذ فهو الذي يكون امتناعاً من أحد العاقدين عن تنفيذ العقد بالشكل الذي تم عليه التراضي. أي أنه تعنت من أحد العاقدين عن التنفيذ وهو قادر عليه.
وهذا الإخلال من أحد العاقدين لا يؤدي دائماً وحتماً إلى تعييب رضا الآخر وتخييره في إبطال العقد كما في حالة الاختلال، بل يميز فيه بين حالتين : إما أن يكون من الممكن إجبار الممتنع على التنفيذ العيني بقوة القضاء، وحينئذ لا يعيب الرضا وإما أن لا يمكن فيه إجبار الممتنع فيعود عندئذ هذا الإخلال بتأثير منعطف على الرضا السابق، فيعيبه ويجيز إبطال العقد.
وهذا التفصيل الذي رسمناه هنا في حكم الإخلال بالتنفيذ هو الذي تقوم على أساسه نصوص المجلة وسائر النصوص الفقهية في المذهب الحنفي
