١ - أنَّ العقد في الفقه الإسلامي يقوم على أساس التراضي الحر وافتراض الصدق والأمانة وحسن النية بين المتعاقدين في إنشاء العقد ثم في تنفيذه كما تم عليه اتفاقهما، وفاقاً لقول الله تعالى في القرآن العظيم: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُم بَيْنَكُم بِالْبَاطِلِ إِلَّا أَن تَكُونَ تِجَارَةً عَن تَرَاضٍ مِّنكُمْ﴾ (سورة النساء : ٢٩/٤).
(1) النظريات القانونية في آثار العقد تقضي بأن يمنح العاقد حق فسخ العقد عند امتناع العاقد الآخر عن تنفيذ التزامه في العقود الملزمة لجانبين كالبيع والإجارة والصلح، ولو كان من الممكن إجبار الممتنع على التنفيذ العيني قضائياً، هذا ويسمى في اصطلاح علماء القانون حق الفسخ (وهم يميزون في اصطلاحهم بين الفسخ والإبطال. فحق الفسخ يستعمل في هذا المقام عند امتناع العاقد عن التنفيذ؛ وحق الإبطال يستعمل في حالات عيوب الرضا فقط).
وحجة علماء القانون في حق الفسخ هي أن العاقد قد تفوت مصلحته في التنفيذ العيني إذا لم يتم التنفيذ في الموعد المحدد في العقد؛ فيجب أن يعطى مجالاً للتحلل من رباط العقد إن شاء، مع تضمين العاقد الممتنع عن التنفيذ تعويضاً عما لحق رفيقه من ضرر بسبب الامتناع الذي اضطره إلى الفسخ وبهذا أخذ قانوننا المدني الجديد في المادة / ١٥٨/ منه .
والذي أرى أن هذا النظر القانوني وجيه وعادل وهو مقبول في قواعد الشريعة الإسلامية وفقهها الذي من أهم أسسه قاعدة دفع الضرر المستمدة من قول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام : لا ضرر ولا ضرار) (ر : ف ۲/۱۰ و ۱۸/۸۱) وإذا كانت المذهب الحنفي لا تتقبله فإن في قواعد المذاهب الأخرى ونصوص فقهائها ما يؤيده تمام التأييد كما ظهر لي من تتبع هذا الموضوع في فقه المذاهب. وانظر ما سيأتي في بحث فسخ العقد في الفصل (٦/٤٥).
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
And the same is said regarding the case when the buyer refuses to present the stipulated guarantor. Then the execution of the sale is disturbed in a manner that cannot be remedied through the judiciary, so consequently the prior consent of the seller becomes defective, because he did not sell except on the basis of presenting the pledge or guarantor; therefore he is granted the right to annul the sale if he wishes.
On this basis, one must distinguish between disturbance of execution (ikhtilāl al-tanfīdh) and breach of execution (ikhlāl bi al-tanfīdh):
Disturbance of execution is that which occurs due to an event leaving no possibility to execute the contract in the manner upon which agreement was reached, such as in the case of the transaction being broken up due to destruction of some of the sold item or its being rightfully claimed (by a third party). This disturbance always vitiates consent and justifies annulment of the contract.
As for breach of execution, it is a refusal by one of the contracting parties to execute the contract in the manner upon which agreement was reached. That is, it is an obstinacy by one of the parties to execute while he is able to do so.
This breach by one of the parties does not always and necessarily lead to vitiating the consent of the other and giving him the option to annul the contract as in the case of disturbance. Rather, two situations are distinguished: either it is possible to compel the refusing party to specific performance through the power of the judiciary, in which case consent is not vitiated; or it is not possible to compel the refusing party, in which case this breach, by virtue of its adverse effect, affects the prior consent, vitiating it and permitting annulment of the contract.
This detailed ruling that we have laid out here regarding the ruling on breach of execution is what the texts of the Majalla and all other fiqh texts in the Hanafi school are based upon.
Observations
37/6 - From all that has been previously explained in the sections on defects of consent, the following two observations become clear:
1. That the contract in Islamic fiqh is founded on free mutual consent and the assumption of truthfulness, trustworthiness, and good faith between the contracting parties in concluding the contract and then in executing it as agreed upon by them, in accordance with the saying of Allah Almighty in the Great Quran: 'O you who have believed, do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly except it be a trade by mutual consent from you' (Surah an-Nisa' 4:29).
(1) Legal theories regarding the effects of contract dictate that the contracting party be granted the right to rescind the contract when the other party refuses to fulfill his obligation in bilateral contracts such as sale, lease, and settlement, even if it is possible to compel the refusing party to specific performance judicially. This is called in the terminology of legal scholars the right of rescission (khiyār al-faskh) (they distinguish in their terminology between rescission and annulment. The right of rescission is used in this context when a party refuses to execute; the right of annulment is used only in cases of consent defects).
The argument of legal scholars for the right of rescission is that the party's interest in specific performance may be lost if execution is not carried out at the specified time in the contract; therefore he should be given room to dissolve himself from the contract bond if he wishes, along with holding the refusing party liable for compensation for the harm suffered by his counterpart due to the refusal that forced him to rescind. This is what our new Civil Code adopted in its Article 158.
My view is that this legal perspective is sound and just, and it is acceptable within the principles of Islamic Shari'ah and its fiqh, whose most important foundations include the maxim of repelling harm derived from the saying of the Messenger, peace be upon him: 'No harm and no retaliation.' If the Hanafi school does not accept it, then among the principles of other schools and the texts of their jurists there is that which supports it fully, as has become clear to me from following this subject in the fiqh of the schools. And see what will come in the discussion of rescission of contract in Chapter (45/6).
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling from the Hanafi school on the defect of consent caused by problems in contract execution. The author distinguishes between two types of failure in execution: 'ikhtilāl al-tanfīdh' (disturbance of execution) and 'ikhlāl bi al-tanfīdh' (breach of execution). Disturbance occurs due to an external event (e.g., destruction of the sold item) making performance impossible; this always vitiates consent and gives the non-breaching party the right to annul. Breach, however, is a willful refusal by a capable party. In breach, if the court can compel specific performance, consent is not vitiated; if not, then the breach becomes equivalent to disturbance and allows annulment. This nuanced ruling is the basis of the Majalla and Hanafi fiqh texts. The author then makes an observation that contracts are founded on free consent and trust, citing Quran 4:29. He also compares with modern legal theory of rescission, noting that while Hanafi school does not generally accept rescission for breach without impossibility of specific performance, other schools do, and the principle of 'no harm' supports it. The passage ends with a reference to further discussion on rescission. The text also mentions a differing opinion of Imam Zufar (in footnote), who would compel the buyer to provide the pledged item.

Key Terms
اختلال التنفيذ (ikhtilāl al-tanfīdh): A disturbance in contract execution caused by an external event that renders performance impossible as agreed.
الإخلال بالتنفيذ (al-ikhlāl bi al-tanfīdh): A willful refusal by a contracting party to execute the contract despite being able to do so.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract due to failure of performance (used in modern legal terminology).
إبطال (ibṭāl): Annulment of a contract due to a defect in consent, such as from disturbance of execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, when does a breach of execution (ikhlāl) give the right to annul the contract?
A) Whenever the other party refuses to perform.
B) Only when the refusal renders performance impossible and the court cannot compel specific performance.
C) Only when the subject matter is destroyed.
D) Breach never gives the right to annul.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law treats disturbance of execution as always vitiating consent, while breach of execution only vitiates consent when judicial compulsion of specific performance is impossible.

The Qawida of Suspended Contracts: Nafādh and the Māniʿ
Defines the suspended contract and the preventer through examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim regarding contracts affecting third-party rights, apply it to two examples (sale of another's property and marriage without consent), and explain the role of the preventer (māniʿ) in suspending contract effects.

Core Idea
A third party's right blocks contract effects until consent – nafādh requires removal of the māniʿ.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of the suspended contract (al-ʿaqd al-mawqūf) and the underlying theory of the preventer. Through two classic examples – selling someone else's property and a father marrying off his adult daughter without her consent – we see how a contract can be valid in itself yet produce no legal effects until a third party lifts the obstacle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples of suspended contracts
The author gives examples: sale of another's property without permission, and father marrying off adult daughter without her consent. Note the third party whose right is affected: the owner and the daughter.
2
Extract the qawida
From the examples, the maxim: every contract that exceeds the rights of its parties and touches the rights of others is suspended pending consent of that other.
3
Analyse the preventer (māniʿ)
The preventer is the right of the third party. It suspends the effects (transfer of ownership, marital rights) until removed by consent.
4
Distinguish 'māniʿ' from 'shart'
The māniʿ is an external obstacle after contract formation, not a precondition for validity. The contract is valid but ineffective.

Source Text

الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
بحث عيوب الرضا(۱) .
وكذا لو باع شخص ملك غيره بلا إذن منه، أو زوج الأب مثلاً ابنته الكبيرة الراشدة دون إذنها . فالعقد هنا يتجاوز حقوق عاقديه ويمس حقوقاً لغيرهما. وذلك الغير هو مالك الشيء المبيع في مثال البيع، والبنت في مثال التزويج. فحقوق الغير هذه تعتبر مانعاً يمنع من ثبوت آثار هذا العقد فتبقى هذه الآثار معلقة ما بقي هذا المانع فلا تتحقق ولا تسري حتى يزول. ففي مثال البيع لا تنتقل الملكية ولا يستحق الثمن؛ وفي مثال التزويج لا تحل المتعة ولا تثبت الحقوق الزوجية حتى يزول ذلك المانع، بأن يرضى المالك بالبيع الواقع على ملكه وترضى البنت بالتزويج الذي أجراه أبوها دون إذنها ؛ فعندئذ ينفذ ذاك البيع وهذا الزواج، أي تترتب عليه آثاره النوعية المقررة له شرعاً، لأنه بزوال المانع يبرز الممنوع، كما تقدم، في بحث المانع (ف ۷/۲۸).
هذه نظرية التوقف، أو عدم النفاذ، ترتكز كما ترى على نظرية المانع التي سلف شرحها آخر المعلومات التمهيدية المتقدمة في أول نظرية العقد (ف ۷/۲۸).
٤/٣٨ - أنواع هذا المانع :
لكي نستطيع حصر أنواع هذا المانع الذي يمنع نفاذ العقود، ونردها إلى مبادىء عامة كلية يحسن :
أولاً أن نعرض الأنواع الأساسية للعقد الموقوف في مختلف صوره وأشكاله .
ثانياً : أن نستخلص على ضوء ذلك العرض أنواع موانع النفاذ التي تتراءى من خلال أنواع العقد الموقوف، وهذا ما سنعالجه فيما يلي:
٥/٣٨ - :أولاً: أنواع العقد الموقوف:
إن التتبع الفقهي يوقعنا على أنواع كثيرة للعقد الموقوف أوصلها فقهاء الحنفية إلى ثمانية وثلاثين نوعاً (ر ): الدر المختار ورد المحتار، فصل الفضولي من كتاب البيع ١٣٩/٤).
غير أننا نخص بالذكر والإيضاح منها هنا سبعة أنواع نراها الأساسية التي يكفي عرضها لمعرفة موانع النفاذ، وهي:
الأنواع
۱ - العقد بالإكراه على رأي من يرى أنه موقف لا فاسد وهو رأي الإمام زفر الذي اعتمدناه كما تقدم آنفاً. (ف ۳/۳۸).
٢ - عقد الصغير المميز : إذا تصرف في أمواله تصرفاً يحتمل النفع والضرر، كالبيع والإجارة والقسمة والصلح إلخ... ذلك لأنه قاصر ناقص الأهلية فهو محجور عليه شرعاً حماية لحقوقه وأمواله. وإنما يتصرف عنه نائبه الشرعي من ولي أو وصي. فإذا عقد الصغير المميز بنفسه عقداً محتملاً للنفع والضرر، كالبيع مثلاً، كان عقده غير نافذ لأن حق التصرف في ماله ليس له بل لنائبه الشرعي. فيتوقف عقده على إجازة نائبه فإن رأى فيه المصلحة له أجازه فنفذ، وإن رده بطل، كما سنرى في نظرية الأهلية والولاية(۱)
٣ - عقد السفيه المحجور عليه والسفيه هو من يبذر أمواله ويبددها
Chapter (38): Effects of the Contract - Effectiveness. Discussion of Defects of Consent. And likewise if a person sells the property of another without his permission, or a father marries off his adult, mature daughter without her consent. So the contract here exceeds the rights of its two contractors and affects the rights of others. That other is the owner of the sold item in the example of sale, and the daughter in the example of marriage. So these rights of others are considered a preventer that prevents the establishment of the effects of this contract, so these effects remain suspended as long as this preventer remains, and they do not materialize nor take effect until it is removed. So in the example of sale, ownership does not transfer and the price is not owed; and in the example of marriage, marital enjoyment is not permissible and marital rights are not established until that preventer is removed, by the owner consenting to the sale made on his property and the daughter consenting to the marriage conducted by her father without her consent; then that sale and this marriage become effective, i.e., its specific effects prescribed by Sharia follow from it, because with the removal of the preventer, the prevented thing emerges, as previously mentioned in the discussion of the preventer (section 7/28). This is the theory of suspension, or non-effectiveness, which, as you see, is based on the theory of the preventer that was previously explained at the end of the introductory information at the beginning of the theory of the contract (section 7/28). 4/38 - Types of this preventer: In order to enumerate the types of this preventer that prevents the effectiveness of contracts and reduce them to general, universal principles, it is good: First, to present the basic types of the suspended contract in its various forms and shapes. Second, to extract, in light of that presentation, the types of preventers of effectiveness that appear through the types of the suspended contract, and this is what we will address in the following: 5/38 - First: Types of the suspended contract: Jurisprudential investigation leads us to many types of the suspended contract, which the Hanafi jurists have enumerated as thirty-eight types (see: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, chapter on al-Fuduli from the book of sale, 4/139). However, we will specifically mention and clarify here seven types that we consider fundamental, whose presentation is sufficient to know the preventers of effectiveness. They are: 1 - The contract under duress, according to the opinion that it is suspended, not invalid, which is the opinion of Imam Zufar that we have adopted as previously mentioned (section 3/38). 2 - The contract of a discerning minor: if he disposes of his wealth in a disposition that may involve benefit and harm, such as sale, lease, division, compromise, etc. That is because he is a minor with deficient legal capacity, so he is legally interdicted to protect his rights and wealth. Only his legal representative, whether a guardian or executor, may dispose on his behalf. So if the discerning minor himself makes a contract that may involve benefit and harm, such as a sale, his contract is not effective because the right to dispose of his property belongs not to him but to his legal representative. So his contract is suspended pending the approval of his representative: if he sees benefit in it, he approves it and it becomes effective, and if he rejects it, it becomes void, as we will see in the theory of legal capacity and guardianship. 3 - The contract of the interdicted spendthrift: and the spendthrift is one who squanders and wastes his wealth.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi theory of suspended contracts (العقد الموقوف). The author classifies 'preventers of effectiveness' (موانع النفاذ) as obstacles that suspend the effects of a contract until the obstacle is removed. He begins by reiterating the rule: a contract that affects the rights of a third party (e.g., selling another's property, a father marrying off his adult daughter without consent) does not immediately produce legal effects; rather, those effects are suspended until the third party gives consent. This is based on the earlier-discussed theory of the 'preventer' (المانع). The author then states the aim: to enumerate the types of such preventers and reduce them to general principles. He first notes that Hanafi jurists have identified thirty-eight types of suspended contracts, but he will focus on seven fundamental types. The passage then provides the first three: (1) the contract made under duress, adopting the view of Imam Zufar that it is suspended (not invalid) – this contrasts with Abu Hanifa's view that such a contract is fasid; (2) the contract of a discerning minor (الصغير المميز) in transactions of potential benefit or harm, which requires approval of his legal guardian; (3) the contract of an interdicted spendthrift (السفيه المحجور عليه) who wastes wealth. The text notes that the minor is legally interdicted to protect his assets, and his representative has the authority to approve or reject the contract. This commentary follows the author's order: first the general rule, then the listing of types, with explanations for each. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh, and the author relies on the theory of the preventer as a foundational concept.

Key Terms
النفاذ (al-nafādh): The quality of a contract producing its full legal effects (e.g., transfer of ownership, establishment of marital rights).
العقد الموقوف (al-ʿaqd al-mawqūf): A contract that is validly concluded but its effects are suspended pending removal of a preventer (e.g., consent of a third party or guardian).
المانع (al-māniʿ): An external factor that prevents the contract from becoming effective, based on the rights of another person or legal capacity issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi qawida in this lesson, what is the status of a sale of another's property without permission?
A) The sale is void because the seller lacks ownership.
B) The sale is valid and ownership transfers immediately.
C) The sale is suspended pending the owner's consent.
D) The sale is valid but the seller must compensate the owner.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of suspension prevents a contract from producing effects when a third party's right is involved, until that party consents.

Intra-Madhhab Dispute on Duress and Contracts of Minors & Spendthrifts
Lists three types of suspended contracts: duress, minor, spendthrift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on duress (Zufar vs. Abu Hanifa), apply the ruling of suspension to contracts of a discerning minor and an interdicted spendthrift, and evaluate the practical fatwa consequence.

Core Idea
Imam Zufar treats duress as a suspending preventer; Abu Hanifa treats it as an invalidating defect – the chosen view is Zufar's.

The passage continues to enumerate the types of suspended contracts. First, the contract under duress: the author adopts Imam Zufar's view that such a contract is suspended, not void. This differs from the founder of the school, Abu Hanifa, who considered it fasid (invalid). Then we encounter contracts of the discerning minor and the spendthrift – both involve lack of full legal capacity, and their effects are suspended pending guardian approval. These examples illustrate the third-party right (the right of the guardian or the public interest) as a preventer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of suspended contracts listed
The author lists: (1) contract under duress (according to Imam Zufar), (2) contract of a discerning minor in potentially beneficial/harmful transactions, (3) contract of an interdicted spendthrift.
2
Analyse the duress dispute
Zufar: contract is suspended (موقوف). Abu Hanifa: contract is fasid (invalid). Note the practical difference: if consent is later given, under Zufar the contract becomes valid; under Abu Hanifa a new contract is needed.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf; the author adopts Zufar's view.
3
Apply the qawida to the minor's contract
A discerning minor (mumayyiz) lacks full capacity; his contract affecting his wealth is suspended pending guardian approval. The guardian's right to protect the minor's property is the preventer.
4
Apply the qawida to the spendthrift's contract
An interdicted spendthrift (safīh maḥjūr ʿalayh) is legally prevented from disposing of his wealth; his contract is suspended pending guardian approval. The guardian's right to preserve assets is the preventer.

Key Terms
الصغير المميز (al-ṣaghīr al-mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discernment (usually seven years) but not puberty; can make some contracts but they are subject to guardian approval.
السفيه المحجور عليه (al-safīh al-maḥjūr ʿalayh): A person who is legally interdicted due to wasting wealth; all his financial transactions are suspended pending guardian approval.
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Duress; coercion that overrides free consent. In Hanafi fiqh, it is a preventer (māniʿ) according to Zufar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a contract made under duress according to the opinion adopted by the author?
A) The contract is void (bāṭil) from the beginning.
B) The contract is suspended (mawqūf) pending ratification.
C) The contract is valid and effective immediately.
D) The contract is irregular (fāsid) and cannot be rectified.

Key Takeaway
Contract under duress is suspended (Zufar's view adopted); the discerning minor's and spendthrift's contracts are also suspended pending guardian approval.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Contract of the Interdicted Spendthrift
Ruling on contracts made by a legally interdicted spendthrift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that contracts of an interdicted spendthrift are suspended (موقوف) pending guardian approval, and identify the condition that the contract must be 'محتمل للنفع والضرر'.

Core Idea
A contract by an interdicted spendthrift is never final — it hangs on the guardian's nod.

When a person is legally interdicted for foolish spending, his financial decisions become subject to oversight. But what about a contract he makes himself? This passage clarifies that such a contract is not automatically void; rather, it is suspended until a guardian approves or rejects it, provided the contract carries both potential benefit and potential harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject
Recognize the party: the interdicted spendthrift (السفيه المحجور) — a person placed under legal restraint due to irresponsibility.
The judge's interdiction (حجر) makes him legally akin to a discerning minor.
2
Understand the condition
The contract must be 'محتملاً للنفع والضرر' — its outcome is uncertain, it could bring benefit or harm.
A contract that is purely beneficial or purely harmful may not fall under this rule.
3
Determine the ruling
The contract is suspended (موقوف) on the guardian's approval. It is not immediately binding.
The guardian (وصي) appointed by the judge holds the power to decide.
4
Distinguish outcomes
If the guardian approves, the contract becomes binding (نفذ); if he rejects, it becomes void (بطل).
The contract has no effect until approval, but it is not void from the start.
5
Connect to the purpose
This rule protects the spendthrift's wealth while allowing beneficial transactions to proceed with oversight.
It balances protection and permissibility.

Source Text

الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
في طرق غير معقولة. فهذا يحجر عليه القاضي، ولو كان كبيراً، فيصبح بالحجر عليه قاصراً كالصغير المميز، ويقيم القاضي عليه وصياً يتصرف عنه ، صيانة لماله وعياله .
فإذا عقد السفيه المحجور بنفسه عقداً محتملاً للنفع والضرر كان عقده موقوفاً على إجازة ،وصيه فإن أجازه نفذ وإن رده بطل.
Chapter (38): Effects of the Contract - Enforcement. In irrational methods. So the judge places him under interdiction, even if he is an adult, so by the interdiction he becomes a minor like a discerning child, and the judge appoints a guardian over him to act on his behalf, safeguarding his wealth and family. Then if the interdicted spendthrift himself makes a contract that is likely to bring benefit or harm, his contract is suspended upon the approval of his guardian: if he approves it, it is enforced; if he rejects it, it becomes void.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage states a rasm (rule) concerning the effect of contracts made by a person who has been interdicted due to foolishness (سفيه). The judge declares him legally incompetent (حجر), even if he is an adult, and appoints a guardian (وصي) to manage his affairs. The key point is that any contract entered into by the interdicted spendthrift that is 'محتملاً للنفع والضرر' (i.e., its outcome is uncertain, either beneficial or harmful) is not immediately binding. Instead, it is suspended (موقوف) on the guardian's subsequent approval. If the guardian approves, the contract becomes effective; if he rejects it, it is void. This protects the spendthrift's assets from his own imprudence and ensures that only beneficial transactions go through.

Key Terms
السفيه المحجور (al-safīh al-maḥjūr): A person interdicted by a judge for irresponsible financial behavior, even if an adult.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal status of a contract made by an interdicted spendthrift (السفيه المحجور) that is 'محتملاً للنفع والضرر'?
A) It is void from the beginning.
B) It is valid and binding immediately.
C) It is suspended (موقوف) on the guardian's approval.
D) It is valid only if the spendthrift later gains maturity.

Key Takeaway
The contract of an interdicted spendthrift is suspended on guardian's approval only if it carries both potential benefit and harm.

The Contract of a Debtor with Overwhelming Debt
Ruling on contracts made by a debtor whose debts exceed assets after judicial interdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ruling on contracts of a debtor with overwhelming debt after judicial interdiction and explain the later Hanafi fatwa change based on istiṣlāḥ.

Core Idea
After interdiction, contracts are suspended; later fatwa extends this even without formal interdiction for harmful transactions.

This passage addresses a critical issue in mufti practice: how to handle a debtor who tries to dispose of assets to the detriment of creditors. The classical Hanafi position allowed free disposition before interdiction, but later jurists, seeing widespread abuse, shifted the fatwa using the principle of istiṣlāḥ. Understanding this evolution is key to grasping how Hanafi fatwa adapts to changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify legal actors and their roles
Identify the debtor (مدين بدين مستغرق), creditors (غرماء), and judge (قاضي). The passage explains the judge's interdiction upon creditors' request.
Interdiction (حجر) is key.
2
Distinguish the two temporal stages
Understand the ruling before and after judicial interdiction. After interdiction, contracts are suspended (موقوف). Before interdiction, classical texts said contracts are effective.
The footnote explains the later fatwa change.
3
Identify the later fatwa change and its basis
Later Hanafis ruled that endowment of a non-interdicted debtor is ineffective beyond surplus, using قاعدة الاستصلاح (istiṣlāḥ), following Hanbali precedent due to widespread abuse.
The author extends this by priority to all harmful contracts.
4
Apply the reasoning (talil) to new cases
The author argues that if endowment is ineffective, then gratuitous transfers (gifts, sales at undervalue) are even more clearly ineffective; this is an argument from priority (أولوية).
This shows how rasm al-mufti extends a ruling by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - عقد المدين بدين مستغرق : المدين إذا استغرقت ديونه أمواله جاز شرعاً للقاضي ،حجره، أي منعه عن التصرف في ماله، بناء على طلب غرمائه الدائنين صيانة لحقوقهم كيلا يلجأ بالتصرف إلى تهريب أمواله من وجوههم. وعندئذ لا يحق له بعد الحجر عليه أن يتصرف في أمواله. فإذا عقد عليها عقداً كما لو باع أو وهب أو رهن شيئاً منها فعقده موقوف على إجازة الغرماء (۱) .
(1) أما قبل الحجر عليه قضاء فقد كانت نصوص أئمة المذاهب الاجتهادية تقضي بنفاذ تصرفه باعتبار أن الديون متعلقة بذمته وعهدته أما أعيان ماله فهي حرة غير مثقلة بحق لأحد، فينفذ فيها تصرفه هذا هو المنقول في أصول المذهبين الحنفي والحنبلي. ولكن المتأخرين من فقهاء الحنابلة لما كثر لجوء المدينين إلى التصرف بأموالهم تهريباً لها، أفتوا بعدم نفاذ التصرفات المالية من المدين إلا فيما يزيد من أمواله عن وفاء ديونه بناء على قاعدة المصالح المرسلة . ثم تبعهم متأخر و الحنفية فأفتوا أيضاً بعدم نفاذ وقف المدين غير المحجور إلا فيما يزيد من أمواله عن وفاء الدين بناء على قاعدة الاستصلاح أيضاً عندما كثر التجاء المدينين إلى وقف أموالهم ليكيدوا الدائنين ويحرموهم من الاستيفاء. والذي نرى أن نص متأخري الحنفية على عدم نفاذ وقف المدين يدل بطريق الأولوية على عدم نفاذ تبرعاته وسائر عقوده المضرة بحقوق الدائنين. (انظر ما تقدم ف ٦/١٥ الحاشية، وسيأتي أيضاً ف ۲/۷۷ الحاشية).
4. The contract of the debtor with overwhelming debt: If a debtor's debts encompass his assets, it is legally permissible for the judge to interdict him, i.e., prevent him from disposing of his wealth, upon the request of his creditors to safeguard their rights, lest he resort to transferring his assets away from them. At that point, after interdiction, he has no right to dispose of his assets. So if he makes a contract regarding them, such as selling, gifting, or pawning something, his contract is suspended upon the approval of the creditors (1). (1) Before judicial interdiction, the texts of the ijtihādī school imams ruled the validity of his disposition, considering that debts are attached to his liability and responsibility, while his specific assets are free and not encumbered by anyone's right; thus his disposition is effective. This is what is transmitted in the principles of the Hanafī and Hanbalī schools. But the later Hanbalī jurists, when debtors increasingly resorted to transferring their assets to evade them, issued fatwas that the debtor's financial dispositions are not effective except in what exceeds his debts, based on the principle of maṣāliḥ mursalah. Then the later Hanafīs followed them and also issued fatwas that the endowment (waqf) of a non-interdicted debtor is not effective except in what exceeds his debts, based on the principle of istiṣlāḥ, when debtors increasingly resorted to endowing their assets to defraud creditors and prevent them from collection. We see that the text of the later Hanafīs on the non-effectiveness of the debtor's endowment indicates, by priority, the non-effectiveness of his donations and all other contracts harmful to creditors' rights. (See earlier footnote 6/15, and also footnote 2/77).
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage explains the rule for a debtor whose debts exceed his assets. The judge may interdict him upon creditors' request, preventing him from free disposition. Any contract (sale, gift, pawn) made after interdiction is suspended (موقوف) upon the creditors' approval. The footnote clarifies that before court interdiction, the classical Hanafī and Hanbalī positions held such contracts effective. However, later jurists from both schools changed the fatwa due to widespread abuse: debtors were transferring assets to harm creditors. The later Hanafīs specifically ruled that a debtor's endowment is not effective beyond the surplus after debts. The author argues that this ruling on endowments should apply by priority to all gratuitous transfers and harmful contracts.

Key Terms
المدين بدين مستغرق (al-madīn bi-dayn mustaghriq): A debtor whose debts encompass all his assets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a debtor is legally interdicted due to overwhelming debt, what is the status of a gift he makes?
A) It is immediately valid.
B) It is void.
C) It is suspended pending creditors' approval.
D) It is valid only if the debt is later paid.

Key Takeaway
A debtor's contracts after judicial interdiction are suspended; later Hanafi fatwa extends this to all harmful disposals even before interdiction, based on istiṣlāḥ.

Donations of a Person in Terminal Illness (Marad al-Mawt)
Rules on donations and contracts by a person in mortal illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on donations in terminal illness by identifying when a donation is suspended and on whose approval it depends.

Core Idea
A terminally ill person's donations are suspended beyond one-third to non-heirs, and any donation to heirs is suspended regardless of amount.

This passage presents a key rule in Hanafi fatwa: the partial interdiction of a person in terminal illness. Understanding when donations are valid or suspended is crucial for advising clients on estate planning and protecting heirs' rights. The rule balances the donor's freedom with creditors' and heirs' interests.

Guided Path
1
Define marad al-mawt
Identify the criteria: illness that confines the person from usual work and is connected to death. If recovered, it was not terminal.
The definition is consistent across schools.
2
Apply the default rule for donations to non-heirs
Donations up to one-third of the estate are effective. Any excess is suspended on approval of heirs.
This applies only if there is no overwhelming debt.
3
Apply the special rule for donations to heirs
Any donation to an heir, no matter how small, is suspended on approval of other heirs.
Based on the hadith 'No bequest to an heir.'
4
Consider the effect of overwhelming debt
If debts cover the entire estate, all donations are suspended on approval of creditors.
Creditors' rights take precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه - تصرف المريض مرض الموت (۲) : إذا تبرع بأي طريق كان (بهبة، أو بوصية، أو ، أو ببيع مع المحاباة بالثمن بما يزيد عن ثلث تركته . فتبرعه هذا لا ينفذ في القسم الزائد عن ثلث التركة، لأنه محجور عنه شرعاً؛ بل هو موقوف على إجازة ،ورثته فإن أجازوه نقذ، وإن ردوه بطل. هذا إذا تبرع لغير الوارث ولم يكن عليه دين مستغرق لتركته .
فإن تبرع لأحد ورثته كان تبرعه هذا موقوفاً على إجازة باقي الورثة مهما قل مبلغ التبرع، لأن تبرعه المنجز في مرض موته هو في حكم الوصية المضافة إلى ما بعد الموت ومن المقرر في أحكام الشريعة بين جمهور المجتهدين أنه : لا وصية لوارث (۱). (ر: ف ۸/۲۳).
وإن كان عليه دين مستغرق توقف تبرعه على إجازة الدائنين.
(2) مرض الموت هو المرض الذي يُقعد الشخص عن عمله المعتاد ويتصل بالموت، فإن شفي منه فهو مرض عادي وليس مرض الموت.
(1) هذا نص حديث نبوي مختلف في درجته لكنه مروي بطرق عديدة يقوي بعضها بعضاً، وقد تلقاه أئمة المذاهب بالقبول ( ر ) نصب الراية للزيلعي كتاب الوصايا ٤/٤٠٣ وشرح الجامع الصغير للمناوي تحت الحديث ٩٩٣/٣).
5. The disposition of a person in terminal illness (2): If he makes a donation by any means (by gift, bequest, or a sale with favor in price exceeding one-third of his estate), then this donation is not effective in the portion exceeding one-third of the estate, because he is legally prohibited from it; rather it is suspended upon the approval of his heirs: if they approve, it is executed; if they reject, it becomes void. This is if he donates to a non-heir and there is no overwhelming debt on his estate. If he donates to one of his heirs, then this donation is suspended upon the approval of the remaining heirs, no matter how small the donation amount, because his immediate donation during terminal illness is treated as a bequest attached to after death, and it is established in the rulings of Shari'ah among the majority of mujtahids that: 'No bequest to an heir' (1). (See section 8/23). And if he has an overwhelming debt, his donation is suspended upon the approval of the creditors. (2) Terminal illness is the illness that confines the person from his usual work and is connected to death; if he recovers from it, it is an ordinary illness, not a terminal illness. (1) This is the text of a prophetic hadith whose grading is disputed, but it is transmitted through numerous chains that strengthen each other, and the imams of the schools have accepted it.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage explains the rule for a person in terminal illness (مرض الموت). Such a person is partially interdicted: he may donate up to one-third of his estate to non-heirs; any donation exceeding that is suspended on the approval of his heirs. If he donates to an heir, even a small amount, it is suspended on the approval of other heirs, because of the principle 'لا وصية لوارث' (no bequest to an heir). Additionally, if he has debts that cover his entire estate, his donations are suspended on creditors' approval. The definition of terminal illness is provided: it must confine the person and be connected to death; recovery indicates it was ordinary illness.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): An illness that confines a person from their usual work and is connected to death; if cured, it is not considered terminal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person in terminal illness gives a gift to his son worth 10% of his total estate, what is the status of the gift?
A) It is valid because it is less than one-third.
B) It is void because to an heir.
C) It is suspended on the approval of other heirs.
D) It is valid if the son is not a creditor.

Key Takeaway
A terminally ill person's donations are partially suspended: up to one-third to non-heirs is effective; anything beyond that, and any donation to an heir, is suspended on approval.

Impediments to Enforceability: Ishtibah and Its Examples
Defines fuduli and introduces the maxim on impediments to enforceability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim that impediments to enforceability are either ambiguity or third-party rights to classify suspended contracts and determine their ruling upon clarification.

Core Idea
A contract is suspended when a condition essential for validity is hidden; clarity resolves it to validity or voidness.

This passage defines the unauthorized agent (fuduli) and then explains a foundational rule for muftis: all impediments that prevent a contract from being immediately enforceable fall into two categories—ambiguity (ishtibah) and the right of a third party. The focus here is on ambiguity, where a condition is unknown, making the contract suspended until the condition is disclosed. You will learn to identify such cases and apply the correct ruling when the ambiguity is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disposition and its category
Determine whether the act is a verbal disposition (قولي) in another's rights. If it is verbal and without lawful authorization, it falls under fuduli.
Physical delivery after a verbal disposition turns the act into usurpation (غصب).
2
Check for lawful authorization
Verify if the actor had authorization from law (e.g., guardian), judiciary (e.g., executor), or contract (e.g., agent). If so, the contract is not fuduli.
The three sources of authorization are شرع, قضاء, تعاقد.
3
Classify the impediment to enforceability
If the contract is not immediately enforceable, identify whether the impediment is ambiguity (ishtibah) or a third-party right (haqq al-ghayr). Look for a hidden condition whose existence is uncertain.
Ambiguity examples: coercion (unknown consent) and hermaphrodite marriage (unknown gender).
4
Apply the ruling for ambiguity
The contract is suspended (موقوف) until the condition is clarified. If the condition is found to exist, the contract becomes enforceable retroactively; if not, it is void ab initio.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from schools that might deem such contracts immediately void or valid.
5
Consider the mufta bihi and any istithna
Note that some types of coercion may be considered independent causes by other scholars; however, the author treats them under ambiguity. Be aware of exceptions when the ambiguity is immediately resolved.
The author's footnote indicates a shift in his approach, but the relied-upon view in the school may vary.

(۱) المشهور في الإكراه أنه سبب مستقل لتوقف العقد أو فساده. وعلى ذلك جريتا فيما سبق، لكني فيما بعد ترجح لدي اعتباره فرعاً من حالة الاشتباه التي يدخل فيها هو
وغيره .
٥٠٤
Chapter (38): Effects of Contract — Enforceability. Technically: one who performs a verbal disposition in the rights of another without authorization. If he acts with legitimate authorization, he is not an unauthorized agent (fuduli); rather, he is a representative of that other — either by legal representation like the guardian of a minor, or by judicial representation like the executor appointed by the judge over an orphan, or by contractual representation like an agent. That is, authorization originates from either the law, or the judiciary, or contract. So whoever is not authorized to act for another from any of these sources is a fuduli. For example, if someone sells or leases the property of another, or contracts marriage for a woman without her permission, or if one partner sells, leases, mortgages, or gifts the entire jointly-owned property — i.e., his share and his partner's share — without authorization from the partner, then the contract is suspended regarding the partner's share. And the contracting party in all such cases and their like is called a fuduli in the terminology of our jurists; even the father, if he contracts marriage for his adult, rational daughter without her permission, even if she is a virgin, is also considered a fuduli and the enforcement of his contract depends on her ratification. Note: It must be observed here that fuduli applies only within the scope of verbal dispositions. If the verbal disposition as a fuduli is followed by physical execution — e.g., someone sells another's property and delivers it to the buyer — then he becomes a usurper (ghasib), and his act takes the ruling of usurpation. 6/38 — Types of impediment to enforceability: After presenting these basic types of different forms of the suspended contract, it appears that the various impediments that prevent the enforcement of a contract and make it suspended can all be reduced to two types: ambiguity (ishtibah) and the right of a third party (haqq al-ghayr). 7/38 — As for ambiguity, it means the concealment of a condition required for the disposition, whose disclosure the contract's conclusion depends on. Since it is impossible to be certain of its existence or non-existence, the disposition cannot be considered void nor valid and effective; so it is suspended until its disclosure. If it becomes evident that the condition was fulfilled, the disposition becomes effective; if it becomes evident that it was not, it becomes void from the outset. Examples of this: the case of coercion (ikrah) and the marriage of a hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil). (a) Coercion: With coercion, it is unknown whether the coerced party consents to the contract he is forced into because it is in his interest or does not consent and would not have proceeded without coercion. So his contract is suspended and not considered void. If he ratifies it after the coercion ends, it becomes evident that his will in this disposition was present, so it becomes effective and its rulings apply retroactively to the date of the contract. If he rejects it, it is considered void from the moment it was made under coercion. If not for this possibility, a coercive disposition would necessarily be considered void apparently, as previously indicated in the discussion of defects of consent (see: F 2/34-14). (b) Marriage of a hermaphrodite: The marriage of a hermaphrodite is also considered suspended pending clarification of his/her condition. If it becomes clear that he is male and married a woman, or female and married a man, the marriage becomes effective and its rulings are established. If the opposite becomes clear, it is void from the outset. [Footnote: The well-known view is that coercion is an independent cause for suspending or invalidating a contract. I have followed that in earlier writings, but later I came to consider it a branch of the state of ambiguity, which includes it and others.]
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on contract enforceability (النفاذ). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, classifies impediments that make a contract suspended (موقوف). He first defines the unauthorized agent (فضولي): one who makes a verbal disposition in the rights of another without proper authorization (from law, judiciary, or contract). The contract made by a fuduli is suspended pending ratification. The author emphasizes that fuduli is limited to verbal acts; if followed by physical delivery, it becomes usurpation. Then he reduces all impediments to enforceability to two types: ambiguity (الاشتباه) and third-party rights (حق الغير). The focus here is on ambiguity: a hidden condition essential for the contract's validity. The contract cannot be declared void or valid immediately; it remains suspended until the ambiguity is resolved. Examples: coercion (الإكراه) and marriage of a hermaphrodite (الخنثى المشكل). In coercion, we don't know if the coerced person truly consents; after coercion ends, his ratification reveals his true will. If he ratifies, the contract is effective retroactively; if not, it is void ab initio. For a hermaphrodite, the contract is suspended until biological sex is determined; if it matches the spouse's gender, it's valid; otherwise void. This is a rasm rule: a methodological tool for a mufti to determine when a contract is valid, void, or suspended. The later footnote (by the author) notes a shift in his view: coercion is not an independent cause but a subset of ambiguity.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): One who makes a verbal disposition in another's rights without any lawful authorization (legal, judicial, or contractual).
الاشتباه (al-ishtibāh): Concealment of a condition essential for the contract's validity; the contract's status is unknown until the condition is clarified.
نفاذ (nafādh): Enforceability of a contract; immediate legal effect without needing further conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The contract becomes valid immediately, but only from the moment of ratification.
B) The contract becomes valid retroactively to the date it was made.
C) The contract remains void because ratification cannot cure coercion.
D) The contract is considered a new contract and requires new offer and acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Impediments to contract enforceability reduce to ambiguity or third-party rights; contracts affected by ambiguity are suspended until the hidden condition is revealed, then become effective retroactively or void ab initio.

Contracts Binding on Heirs and Representatives: The Rule of Hukm al-'Aqid
Identifying categories of persons treated like the contractor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify categories of persons who are in the status of the contractor (hukm al-'aqid) and apply this rule in fatwa practice.

Core Idea
Heirs and principals are bound by contracts of the deceased/agent because they stand in the same legal position.

The general principle of contract law holds that no contract may impose obligations on a third party. However, Islamic law recognizes exceptions: certain persons are legally considered equivalent to the contractor themselves. This rule is vital for muftis when determining who is bound by a transaction. This lesson unpacks the first category of such persons as presented by Shaykh Mustafa al-Zarqa: the principal in a representation contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and exception
Read the opening statement: the contract does not affect third parties (al-ghayr), but it does affect those who are in the status of the contractor (hukm al-'aqid). Identify this as the core legal principle.
The pawn example illustrates the exception: heirs cannot take the pawned item without paying the debt.
2
Analyze the pawn example
Work through the scenario: a person pawns property, dies. The pawn contract binds the heirs even though they did not consent. They must choose: pay the debt and retrieve the property, or let the pawnbroker keep it. They are not forced to pay from their own pockets if the estate is insufficient, but they cannot recover the property without paying.
This shows the legal consequence of being in hukm al-'aqid.
3
List the first category
The text states that the first category of persons with hukm al-'aqid is the principal (manub 'anhu) in a contract made through representation (niyaba). Identify the three forms: guardianship (wilaya), executorship (wisaya), and agency (wakala).
The footnote provides brief definitions of each.
4
Differentiate from true third parties
Contrast those in hukm al-'aqid with true third parties (al-ghayr). Understand that the word 'ghayr' in the rule refers only to those who have no legal link to the contractor. The categories mentioned are excluded from that term.
This distinction is crucial for correct application.
5
Apply to a fatwa scenario
Imagine a mufti receives a question: a father owes a debt secured by a pawn; he dies, and his son wants to sell the pawned item without paying. Using this rule, the mufti rules that the son, as heir, is in the status of the contractor and must pay the debt before disposing of the item.
This practical step ties the rule to real fatwa work.

المبحث الثاني : مدى نفاذ العقود على الأشخاص ١١/٣٨ - رأينا أنَّ العقد لا يجوز أن يتجاوز على حقوق الغير، فإن تجاوز توقف وامتنع نفاذه كما في عقد الفضولي.
لكننا نرى أشخاصاً غير العاقد ينفذ عليهم عقد العاقد، وتقيدهم التزاماته الحقوقية كما لو كانوا هم ،العاقدين فهم في حكم العاقد نفسه، وليسوا أشخاصاً ثالثين من الغير بالمعنى المقصود من لفظ «الغير». وذلك كما لو رهن شخص بعض ماله في مقابل دين عليه ثم مات، فإن هذا الرهن نافذ على ورثته وإن لم يعقدوه هم. فلا يستطيعون أخذ المرهون من المرتهن واقتسامه بينهم ما لم يوفوه الدين كاملاً؛ وإن كانوا غير مجبرين على وفاء الدين من أموالهم الخاصة إن لم يكن في التركة وفاء .
لذلك وجب أن نعرف من هم الأشخاص الذين في حكم العاقد فتسري عليهم آثار عقده وتنفذ في حقهم، فيكون سواهم هو المقصود بكلمة (الغير) في قولنا: إن العقد لا ينفذ في حقوق الغير.

١ - المنوب عنه في العقد المعقود بطريق النيابة، في جميع صورها من ولاية أو وصاية، أو وكالة) (۱).

Part Six: Theory of Contracts and the Like in Jurisprudence. Second Section: Scope of Enforceability of Contracts on Persons. 11/38 - We have seen that the contract may not exceed the rights of third parties; if it does, it is suspended and its enforceability is prevented, as in the contract of the unauthorized person (fuduli). But we see persons other than the contractor upon whom the contractor's contract is enforced, and its legal obligations bind them as if they were the contractors themselves. They are in the same legal status as the contractor himself, and they are not third parties in the intended sense of the word 'third party'. For example, if a person pawns some of his property against a debt he owes and then dies, this pawn is binding on his heirs even though they did not contract it. They cannot take the pawned item from the pawnbroker and divide it among themselves unless they pay the debt in full; although they are not forced to pay the debt from their own private funds if the estate does not have sufficient payment. Therefore, we must know who are the persons who are in the status of the contractor so that the effects of his contract apply to them and are enforceable against them, and those other than them are what is meant by the word 'third party' in our statement: The contract is not enforceable against the rights of third parties. 12/38 - Indeed, tracing the juristic rulings related to the contract and its effects indicates that the persons who have the same status as the contractor himself in all respects or in some respects, so that its effects are enforceable against them and apply to them within specific limits, are the following categories: 1. The principal on whose behalf the contract is made through representation (niyaba), in all its forms: guardianship (wilaya), executorship (wisaya), or agency (wakala). (1) (1) Guardianship (wilaya): A legal representation established for certain relatives of a minor, such as his father and grandfather in a specific order. Executorship (wisaya): A judicial representation for a person appointed by the judge to act on behalf of minor orphans, called a guardian (wasi). Agency (wakala): A contractual representation, as previously mentioned in the type of suspended contracts, which is the contract of the unauthorized person (fuduli).
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage establishes a rule of contract law: while contracts generally cannot impose obligations on third parties, certain persons are legally considered equivalent to the contractor himself for enforceability purposes. The author provides the example of a pawn: if a person pawns an asset and dies, the heirs are bound by the pawn contract even though they did not consent; they cannot take the item without paying the debt. This shows that heirs are 'in the status of the contractor' regarding that specific transaction. The passage then outlines the first category of such persons: the principal who is represented in a contract through any form of legal representation (guardianship, executorship, or agency). This includes cases where a guardian contracts on behalf of a minor, or an agent contracts on behalf of a principal. In such cases, the contract's effects directly bind the represented person, not just the representative. The footnote defines the three types of representation: wilaya (guardianship by relatives), wisaya (executorship appointed by a judge), and wakala (agency by mutual agreement). Understanding who qualifies as 'in the status of the contractor' is crucial to properly applying the rule that contracts do not affect third parties.

Key Terms
الغير (al-ghayr): A third party whose rights cannot be affected by a contract they did not enter, excluding persons in the status of the contractor.
حكم العاقد (hukm al-'aqid): The legal status of being treated as if one were the original contractor for enforceability purposes.
النيابة (al-niyaba): Legal representation, including guardianship, executorship, and agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A stranger who witnesses the contract.
B) The principal on whose behalf an agent acted.
C) A creditor of the contractor.
D) Any third party who benefits from the contract.

Key Takeaway
Contracts bind not only the signatory but also those who legally stand in his or her shoes, such as heirs and principals in representation.

Distinguishing al-Fudul and al-Tafaddul
Distinguishing suspended agency from binding beneficence with legal examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-fudul (suspended unauthorized acts) and al-tafaddul (binding beneficence) and apply the controlling rule that beneficence must be based on custom or necessity.

Core Idea
Beneficence binds, mere interference suspends – the difference is custom or necessity.

A mufti often encounters cases where someone acts on behalf of another without prior permission. Islamic law distinguishes between two types: al-fudul (unauthorized acts that are suspended) and al-tafaddul (acts of beneficence that are immediately binding). This lesson explores the key distinction through the example of a travel companion who arranges the burial of a deceased fellow traveler. The controlling rule, derived from Imam Muhammad's practice, is that al-tafaddul is valid when supported by custom ('urf) or necessity (idhtirar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Read the passage and locate the definitions of al-fudul and al-tafaddul. Note that both involve acting for another without prior permission.
2
Analyze the travel companion case
Consider the case of a travel companion who sells the deceased's property to pay for burial. Why is he not considered a fuduli? The passage says it is by istihsan.
Imam Muhammad's precedent illustrates the principle.
3
Extract the controlling rule (dabit)
From the examples, the author deduces the rule: beneficence (al-tafaddul) must be based on custom or necessity. Write this rule in your own words.
This dabit restricts the general rule requiring authorization.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider a neighbor who repairs a shared wall without permission. Does the rule apply? Think about whether custom or necessity justifies it.
The mufti must evaluate the presence of 'urf or idhtirar.

۱۳/۳۸ - يتضح من هذه الأحوال التي يسري فيها وينفذ عقد أحد العاقدين أو عدم

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. There are exceptional cases in which a non-representative is considered like a representative in his actions concerning the affairs of others, such as if two people were companions on a journey and one died, and the second undertook his preparation, burial, collection of his wealth, and transfer to his heirs; and in doing so sold his belongings and bought what was needed for his preparation and burial. Then these actions of his are binding upon the right-holders in the deceased's estate by way of istihsan, and the companion is not considered a fuduli (unauthorized agent). He is not liable for what he spent. Imam Muhammad did that when one of his students died; he sold his books to prepare him, and it was said to him: 'He did not appoint a executor,' i.e., he did not appoint before his death a trustee over his estate to manage it. So Muhammad recited the verse: 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer' (Quran 2:220). So whatever is done on this analogy of beneficence is acceptable. Its controlling rule (dabit) indicated by the fiqh branches is that the beneficence must be based on custom or necessity. It has many examples mentioned in the book of Ghasp from Radd al-Muhtar (5/117), and in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, as we will see in the third part of this jurisprudential series: Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Fiqh (1/11). Of this kind is the action of one partner in spending on the repair of a jointly owned property that cannot be divided if it needs essential maintenance and the other refuses to spend, then his partner may spend and recover from him his share of the expenditure. (1) Thus we distinguish between two subjects that must be distinguished by name: al-fudul, which is suspended and does not take effect; and al-tafaddul, which takes effect on others and binds them. (1) Article 1313 of the Majalla stipulates that the spending partner in this case, if he spends with the judge's permission, recovers from his partner his share of the actual expenditure. But if he spends without the judge's permission, he recovers from his partner his share of the value of the buildings and repairs, and the difference is that their value is usually less than what was actually spent on them. Chapter 38: Effects of the Contract – Enforceability. Here again, the contracts of expenditure made by one partner without agency or permission bind the other partner. 3 – The heir: All contracts of his predecessor made while healthy and fully competent, creating for others real rights pertaining to some of his assets, or creating financial obligations upon himself, all of that is considered to encumber the estate and is enforceable; and the heir is considered bound by it, so nothing of the estate becomes free for him except what remains after fulfilling these rights. For example, if the predecessor had pawned some of his wealth, and the heir wanted to take the pawn from the pledgee on the grounds that he inherited it, he is not entitled to do so except after paying the debt of the estate, because the pledgee has a right in the value of the pawn established by the predecessor's contract before the heir's ownership of the estate. And the same applies to other contracts. 4 – The legatee of a specific item from the testator's estate: If this bequeathed item does not exceed one-third of the estate, it passes to the legatee encumbered with all rights that the testator created upon it by a valid contract. For example, if the item was rented, the lease is enforceable against the legatee and binding on him, so he cannot take the item except after the end of the lease term. (1) It is clear from these cases in which a contract of one of the contracting parties is effective and enforceable or not. (1) Islamic ijtihad is divided on the dissolution of a lease contract upon the death of the lessor. The preponderant view in Hanafi ijtihad is dissolution, and in Shafi'i ijtihad the opposite. The example we gave follows the view that the lease remains and is not dissolved by death, which is what the legal provisions in our country are based on.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti exposition distinguishing between two types of unauthorized actions on behalf of others: al-fudul (unsolicited agency), which is suspended and requires ratification, and al-tafaddul (beneficent interference), which is immediately binding and effective. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, opens with a set of exceptional cases where a person who is not a formal representative (na'ib) is treated as one. The first example is the travel companion who arranges the burial and transfer of a deceased companion's property. Such actions are binding on the estate by istihsan (juristic preference), not by the general rule requiring authorization. This is rooted in the practice of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, who sold a deceased student's books for his funeral, reciting Quran 2:220. From this, the author extracts the controlling rule (dabit): beneficence (al-tafaddul) is effective if based on custom ('urf) or necessity (idhtirar). He then applies this to two further cases: one partner spending on necessary repairs of an indivisible common property without the other's consent (with a note on the Majalla's distinction between spending with permission of the judge vs. without), and then the partner's contracts being binding on the other partner. The passage then moves to the heir, who inherits the deceased's estate already encumbered by the deceased's contracts – the heir cannot take assets free of those obligations. The fourth case is the legatee of a specific item, who takes it subject to existing rights like a lease. A footnote clarifies a Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute: the Hanafi view is that a lease dissolves upon the lessor's death, while the Shafi'i view holds it remains. The example given assumes the Shafi'i view, as adopted by modern civil codes. The key takeaway: the mufti must distinguish between al-fudul (suspended) and al-tafaddul (binding), the latter being valid when supported by custom or necessity.

Key Terms
الفضول (al-fudul): Actions performed on behalf of another without authorization, which are suspended (mawquf) and require ratification to become effective.
التفضل (al-tafaddul): Beneficent interference; actions on behalf of another without prior authorization that are binding due to custom or necessity.
نائب (na'ib): A representative or agent authorized to act on behalf of another.
ضابط (dabit): Controlling rule or legal maxim that governs a set of cases; here it restricts the principle that actions without authorization are invalid.
عرف ('urf): Customary practice that gives legal weight to certain actions.
اضطرار (idhtirar): Necessity that justifies an otherwise unauthorized act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key factor that makes al-tafaddul binding according to Hanafi law?
A) The doer's good intentions
B) Prior authorization
C) Custom or necessity
D) The value of the act

Key Takeaway
The mufti must distinguish between al-fudul (suspended) and al-tafaddul (binding), where the latter is validated by custom or necessity.

Applications and the Lease Dispute: Enforceability of Contracts on Heirs and Legatees
Applying the rule of beneficence to partner, heir, and legatee cases, and discussing the lease dissolution ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of al-tafaddul to cases of partnership, inheritance, and bequests, and explain the Hanafi-Shafi'i difference on lease dissolution upon the lessor's death.

Core Idea
Contracts float over the estate – heirs and legatees take assets encumbered, and leases may or may not survive the lessor's death.

In the previous lesson, we established the distinction between al-fudul and al-tafaddul. Now we apply this rule to practical scenarios: the partner who spends on repairs, the heir who inherits encumbered property, and the legatee who receives a bequest subject to an existing lease. The passage also touches on a significant intra-madhhab dispute: does a lease dissolve upon the lessor's death? This lesson will help you navigate these real-life cases.

Guided Path
1
Review the partner's spending case
From the previous lesson, we saw that a partner's spending on essential repairs is binding. Now note the Majalla's distinction between spending with and without judge's permission.
Article 1313: with permission → actual expenses; without → value of improvements.
2
Analyze the heir's position
The heir inherits the estate subject to all pre-existing contracts. He cannot take assets free of debts or pledges.
Example: pawned property must be redeemed first.
3
Analyze the legatee's position
A specific bequest is also encumbered; if the item was leased, the legatee must wait until the lease ends.
The lease is binding on the legatee.
4
Examine the lease dissolution dispute
The footnote reveals a Hanafi-Shafi'i conflict. The Hanafi position: lease dissolves upon lessor's death. Shafi'i: lease continues. The author notes that modern codes follow the Shafi'i view.
Practical consequence: in Hanafi law, the lessee's obligations end; in Shafi'i, they continue.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
يوجد حالات استثنائية يعتبر فيها غير النائب كالنائب في تصرفه بشؤون غيره وذلك كما لو كان شخصان مترافقين في سفر فمات أحدهما، فقام الثاني بتجهيزه ودفنه وجمع أمواله ونقلها إلى ورثته؛ وفي سبيل ذلك باع أمتعته واشترى ما يحتاج إليه تجهيزه ودفنه. فإنَّ تصرفاته هذه تنفذ على أصحاب الحقوق في تركة الميت استحساناً، ولا يعتبر الرفيق فضولياً. ولا يضمن ما أنفق. وقد فعل ذلك الإمام محمد، إذ مات بعض تلامذته ، فباع محمد كتبه لتجهيزه فقيل له : إنه لم يوص أي إنه لم يعين قبل وفاته وصيّاً على تركته لإدارتها فتلا محمد قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ يَعْلَمُ الْمُفْسِدَ مِنَ المصلح﴾ (سورة البقرة ٢٢٠/٢). فما كان على هذا القياس من التفضَّل فمقبول. وضابطه الذي تدل عليه الفروع الفقهية أن يكون التفضل مستنداً إلى عرف أو اضطرار. وله أمثلة كثيرة مذكورة في كتاب الغصب من رد المحتار) (١١٧/٥)، وفي «الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجیم كما سنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية وهو : المدخل إلى النظرية العامة للالتزام في الفقه الإسلامي ۱/۱۱). ومن هذا القبيل تصرف أحد الشريكين في الإنفاق على إصلاح العقار المشترك الذي لا يقبل القسمة إذا احتاج إلى النفقة التعميرية الضرورية وامتنع الآخر عن الإنفاق، فإن لشريكه أن ينفق هو، ويرجع عليه بحصته النفقة. (١) من = على غيره دون سابق إذن .
وبذلك نميز بين موضوعين يجب التمييز بينهما في الاسم، وهما:
- الفضول» الذي يتوقف فلا ينفذ.
والتفضل الذي ينفذ على الغير ويلزمه.
(۱) نصت المادة / ۱۳۱۳/ من المجلة على أن الشريك المنفق في هذه الحال إذا أنفق بإذن الحاكم يرجع على شريكه بحصته من النفقة الفعلية. أما إذا أنفق بدون إذن الحاكم فإنه يرجع على شريكه بحصته من قيمة الأبنية ،والتعميرات، والفرق بينهما أن قيمتها مبنية تكون في العادة أقل مما أنفق عليها فعلاً.

الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
فهنا أيضاً نفذت على الشريك عقود النفقة التي أجراها شريكه الآخر دون نيابة عنه أو إذن .
٣ - الوارث : فإن جميع عقود مورثه التي يعقدها منجزة في حال صحته وكمال رشده وينشىء بها لغيره حقوقاً عينية تتعلق بشيء من أعيان ماله، أو ينشىء بها التزامات مالية على نفسه كل ذلك تعتبر تركته مثقلة نافذاً به، ويعتبر في حق الوارث يحتج به عليه فلا يخلص له من التركة شيء إلا ما فَضَل منها عن وفاء هذه الحقوق.
فمثلاً لو أنَّ المورث كان راهناً لبعض ماله فأراد الوارث أن يأخذ المرهون من المرتهن بحجة أنه قد ملكه إرثاً لا يحق له ذلك إلا بعد وفاء الدين الذي على التركة، لأنَّ للمرتهن حقاً في مالية المرهون ثبت له بعقد المورث قبل ثبوت ملك الوارث في التركة وهكذا يقال فيما سوى ذلك من العقود.
·
٤ - الموصى له بعين معينة من تركة الموصي :
فإن هذه العين الموصى بها إذا كانت لا تتجاوز ثلث التركة تنتقل إلى الموصى له مثقلة بجميع الحقوق التي كان أنشأها عليها الموصي بعقد صحيح .
فلو كانت العين مأجورة تكون الإجارة نافذة في حق الموصى له، وسارية عليه، فلا يستطيع أخذ العين إلا بعد انقضاء مدة الإجارة(1).
۱۳/۳۸ - يتضح من هذه الأحوال التي يسري فيها وينفذ عقد أحد العاقدين أو عدم
(۱) الاجتهادات الإسلامية مختلفة في انفساخ عقد الإجارة بموت المؤجر، والراجح في الاجتهاد الحنفي الانفساخ وفي الاجتهاد الشافعي عكسه. والمثل الذي أوردناه يتمشى على القول ببقاء الإجارة وعدم انفساخها بالموت، وهو الذي عليه الأحكام القانونية لدينا.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. There are exceptional cases in which a non-representative is considered like a representative in his actions concerning the affairs of others, such as if two people were companions on a journey and one died, and the second undertook his preparation, burial, collection of his wealth, and transfer to his heirs; and in doing so sold his belongings and bought what was needed for his preparation and burial. Then these actions of his are binding upon the right-holders in the deceased's estate by way of istihsan, and the companion is not considered a fuduli (unauthorized agent). He is not liable for what he spent. Imam Muhammad did that when one of his students died; he sold his books to prepare him, and it was said to him: 'He did not appoint a executor,' i.e., he did not appoint before his death a trustee over his estate to manage it. So Muhammad recited the verse: 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer' (Quran 2:220). So whatever is done on this analogy of beneficence is acceptable. Its controlling rule (dabit) indicated by the fiqh branches is that the beneficence must be based on custom or necessity. It has many examples mentioned in the book of Ghasp from Radd al-Muhtar (5/117), and in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, as we will see in the third part of this jurisprudential series: Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Fiqh (1/11). Of this kind is the action of one partner in spending on the repair of a jointly owned property that cannot be divided if it needs essential maintenance and the other refuses to spend, then his partner may spend and recover from him his share of the expenditure. (1) Thus we distinguish between two subjects that must be distinguished by name: al-fudul, which is suspended and does not take effect; and al-tafaddul, which takes effect on others and binds them. (1) Article 1313 of the Majalla stipulates that the spending partner in this case, if he spends with the judge's permission, recovers from his partner his share of the actual expenditure. But if he spends without the judge's permission, he recovers from his partner his share of the value of the buildings and repairs, and the difference is that their value is usually less than what was actually spent on them. Chapter 38: Effects of the Contract – Enforceability. Here again, the contracts of expenditure made by one partner without agency or permission bind the other partner. 3 – The heir: All contracts of his predecessor made while healthy and fully competent, creating for others real rights pertaining to some of his assets, or creating financial obligations upon himself, all of that is considered to encumber the estate and is enforceable; and the heir is considered bound by it, so nothing of the estate becomes free for him except what remains after fulfilling these rights. For example, if the predecessor had pawned some of his wealth, and the heir wanted to take the pawn from the pledgee on the grounds that he inherited it, he is not entitled to do so except after paying the debt of the estate, because the pledgee has a right in the value of the pawn established by the predecessor's contract before the heir's ownership of the estate. And the same applies to other contracts. 4 – The legatee of a specific item from the testator's estate: If this bequeathed item does not exceed one-third of the estate, it passes to the legatee encumbered with all rights that the testator created upon it by a valid contract. For example, if the item was rented, the lease is enforceable against the legatee and binding on him, so he cannot take the item except after the end of the lease term. (1) It is clear from these cases in which a contract of one of the contracting parties is effective and enforceable or not. (1) Islamic ijtihad is divided on the dissolution of a lease contract upon the death of the lessor. The preponderant view in Hanafi ijtihad is dissolution, and in Shafi'i ijtihad the opposite. The example we gave follows the view that the lease remains and is not dissolved by death, which is what the legal provisions in our country are based on.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti exposition distinguishing between two types of unauthorized actions on behalf of others: al-fudul (unsolicited agency), which is suspended and requires ratification, and al-tafaddul (beneficent interference), which is immediately binding and effective. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, opens with a set of exceptional cases where a person who is not a formal representative (na'ib) is treated as one. The first example is the travel companion who arranges the burial and transfer of a deceased companion's property. Such actions are binding on the estate by istihsan (juristic preference), not by the general rule requiring authorization. This is rooted in the practice of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, who sold a deceased student's books for his funeral, reciting Quran 2:220. From this, the author extracts the controlling rule (dabit): beneficence (al-tafaddul) is effective if based on custom ('urf) or necessity (idhtirar). He then applies this to two further cases: one partner spending on necessary repairs of an indivisible common property without the other's consent (with a note on the Majalla's distinction between spending with permission of the judge vs. without), and then the partner's contracts being binding on the other partner. The passage then moves to the heir, who inherits the deceased's estate already encumbered by the deceased's contracts – the heir cannot take assets free of those obligations. The fourth case is the legatee of a specific item, who takes it subject to existing rights like a lease. A footnote clarifies a Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute: the Hanafi view is that a lease dissolves upon the lessor's death, while the Shafi'i view holds it remains. The example given assumes the Shafi'i view, as adopted by modern civil codes. The key takeaway: the mufti must distinguish between al-fudul (suspended) and al-tafaddul (binding), the latter being valid when supported by custom or necessity.

Key Terms
الفضول (al-fudul): Actions performed on behalf of another without authorization, which are suspended (mawquf) and require ratification to become effective.
التفضل (al-tafaddul): Beneficent interference; actions on behalf of another without prior authorization that are binding due to custom or necessity.
نائب (na'ib): A representative or agent authorized to act on behalf of another.
ضابط (dabit): Controlling rule or legal maxim that governs a set of cases; here it restricts the principle that actions without authorization are invalid.
عرف ('urf): Customary practice that gives legal weight to certain actions.
اضطرار (idhtirar): Necessity that justifies an otherwise unauthorized act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant Hanafi view, what happens to a lease when the lessor dies?
A) The lease is dissolved.
B) The lease continues.
C) The lease is suspended.
D) The lease is automatically renewed.

Key Takeaway
Contracts on inherited assets bind the heir and legatee, and the lease dissolution upon death is a point of ikhtilaf where the Hanafi view dissolves but modern law may differ.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
