Non-Binding Contracts and the Case of Istisna: A Rasm Rule in Iftaa
Discusses when contracts become binding, focusing on muzara'ah and istisna

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm rule that certain contracts are non-binding until a condition is met, and evaluate the modification of the istisna' rule by the Majallah.

Core Idea
A contract may be non-binding until a condition is fulfilled; the Majallah modified istisna' for public interest.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi contract law: not all contracts are immediately binding. Using the examples of muzara'ah and istisna', the passage explains the rationale behind non-binding contracts and how the Majallah later codified a different rule for istisna' based on a narration from Abu Yusuf. You will understand how a rasm rule (a principle guiding fatwa) can evolve through juristic preference and codification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Determine whether the contract is lazim (binding) or ghayr lazim (non-binding) at inception. For muzara'ah and istisna', the default is non-binding.
Luzum is not the same as nafadh (execution).
2
Understand the condition that triggers bindingness
Identify the specific condition that transforms the contract from non-binding to binding. For muzara'ah: casting the seed. For istisna': manufacture of the item.
Before the condition, both parties may rescind unilaterally.
3
Analyze the rationale for non-bindingness
The rationale is to prevent forcing a party to destroy property without guaranteed return. This is analogized to hiring a worker to demolish a house.
After the condition, forcing continuation does not involve destruction.
4
Examine the dispute on istisna'
The original school holds both parties may rescind before manufacture; after manufacture, only the customer has the option of inspection. The Majallah adopted a binding-from-inception rule for both parties.
The Majallah relied on a narration from Abu Yusuf (recorded in al-Muhit al-Burhani).
5
Evaluate the resolution: Majallah as law
The Majallah's text is unambiguous and backed by sovereign authority. It supersedes the earlier school position, even though it appears to contradict the original rule.
The later narration and public interest (maslaha zamaniyya) justify the modification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٠) آثار العقد - اللزوم
فيجوز للطرف الذي عليه تقديم البذر أن يعدل عن المزارعة ويفسخ عقدها، ولكن ذلك قبل إلقاء البذر في الأرض.
وملحظ الفقهاء في هذا أن إجباره على الاستمرار في المزارعة يقتضي إلزامه بإتلاف ماله وهو البذر، ولا يدري هل ينتج أو لا ينتج؟ فصار كما لو استأجر الإنسان عاملاً لهدم داره ثم بدا له العدول عن الهدم، فإن له ذلك.
أما بعد إلقاء البذر في الأرض فتصبح المزارعة لازمة حتى في حق صاحب البذر. (الدر المختار ورد المحتار ١٧٧/٥)
ويلاحظ أن الفسخ في هذه العقود غير اللازمة ليس له استناد (أي تأثير رجعي) فعزل الوكيل لا ينقض تصرفاته السابقة، ورجوع الواهب بعد ولادة الدابة الموهوبة لا يوجب للواهب أخذ ولدها معها بل يبقى للموهوب له لأنه حصل في ملكه. (ر: المجلة/ ٨٦٩).
وهكذا يلحظ في بقية المسائل.
تلك العقود الأحد عشر التي ينتفي فيها اللزوم انتفاء مطلقاً أو مقيداً على اختلاف درجاته فيها.
وهناك عقد ثاني عشر نفرده بالذكر في هذه المناسبة، كان يعتبر في أصل المذهب الحنفي غير لازم فأصبح لدينا بمقتضى نصوص المجلة لازماً وهو : الاستصناع.
عقد الاستصناع:
فالاستصناع - وهو شراء ما سيصنع بطريق التوصية - يجوز لكل من الطرفين فسخه في أصل المذهب بلا خلاف ما دام الشيء لم يصنع. أما بعد صنعه وإحضاره فيكون للمستصنع حق في الفسخ من قبيل خيار الرؤية على الرأي الراجح. غير أن المجلة في المادة ٣٩٢ أخذت بلزوم العقد في الطرفين منذ انعقاده إلا إذا جاء المصنوع مغايراً للأوصاف المعينة في العقد.
جاء في لائحة الأسباب الموجبة من مقدمة المجلة أنها اختارت قول أبي يوسف بعدم خيار المستصنع إذا أحضر الصانع المصنوع بعد العمل موافقاً للشروط، وذلك وفقاً لما تقضي به المصلحة الزمنية. والمنقول في كتب المذهب أن أبا يوسف لا يقول بعدم خيار المستصنع إلا بعد الصنع وإحضار الصانع المصنوع موافقاً للأوصاف المشروطة. أما قبل الفراغ من صنعه أو بعد الفراغ قبل إحضاره فلا خلاف في أن المستصنع مخير كما في البدائع ورد المحتار وغيرهما.
لكن نص المادة ٣٩٢ من المجلة جاء صريحاً في أنه بعد انعقاد الاستصناع لا خيار لأحد من الطرفين إلا إذا جاء المصنوع مغايراً للأوصاف المشروطة. فأفاد إطلاق نصها اللزوم بحق الطرفين قبل الصنع بعد انعقاد العقد، وهو ليس قولاً لأحد في المذهب. فهل تحمل المادة المذكورة على تفصيل قول أبي يوسف بناءً على ما جاء في لائحة الأسباب الموجبة للمجلة من أنها أخذت بقوله، أو يقال أن نصها صريح في إطلاق اللزوم وهي قانون آمر فيحكم بلزوم العقد مطلقاً ولو قبل العمل، إذ ليس في نص المادة احتمال أو غموض حتى يحمل على تفصيل رأي أبي يوسف الذي أشارت إلى أخذه لائحة الأسباب الموجبة؟
القضية محل نظر، والأوجه الاعتبار الثاني، لأن الظاهر أن المجلة اتخذت نظرية أبي يوسف أساساً وتوسعت فيها بالتعديل في بعض نواحيها بحسب المصلحة الزمنية، وقد اقترنت بالإرادة السلطانية، فأصبحت قانوناً فالعبرة لنصها لا للنصوص التي استمدت منها. هذا وبعد كتابة ما تقدم نبهت إلى أن ما أخذت به المجلة هو رواية أخرى عن أبي يوسف مصرح بها عنه في كتاب المحيط البرهاني، وهو من عمدة مراجع المذهب الحنفي، فلم يبق في صنيع المجلة إشكال.
Chapter (40): Effects of the Contract - Bindingness (Luzum). It is permissible for the party who is to provide the seed to withdraw from the sharecropping (muzara'ah) and rescind its contract, but that is before casting the seed into the ground. The jurists' consideration in this is that forcing him to continue in the sharecropping would necessitate forcing him to destroy his property, which is the seed, and he does not know whether it will produce or not. So it becomes like when a person hires a worker to demolish his house and then decides to abandon the demolition; he has that right. However, after casting the seed into the ground, the sharecropping becomes binding even against the owner of the seed. (Al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 5/177) It is noted that rescission in these non-binding contracts does not have retroactive effect: the dismissal of an agent does not annul his previous actions; the return of a donor after the birth of a donated animal does not entitle the donor to take its offspring with it, but it remains with the donee because it came into his ownership. (See: Majallah, article 869). And so it is observed in the remaining cases. Those are the eleven contracts in which bindingness is negated absolutely or conditionally, to varying degrees. There is a twelfth contract that we single out for mention on this occasion: it was considered non-binding in the original Hanafi school, but according to the texts of the Majallah it has become binding: that is istisna' (commissioned manufacture). Contract of Istisna': Istisna' - which is purchasing what will be manufactured by way of order - both parties have the right to rescind it in the original school without dispute as long as the thing has not been manufactured. After it is manufactured and presented, the customer has the right of rescission based on the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) according to the preponderant view. However, the Majallah in Article 392 adopted the bindingness of the contract for both parties from its inception, unless the manufactured item comes out different from the specified descriptions in the contract. It came in the explanatory memorandum of the introduction of the Majallah that it chose the opinion of Abu Yusuf that there is no option for the customer if the manufacturer presents the manufactured item after work in accordance with the conditions, in accordance with what the temporal interest requires. What is transmitted in the school's books is that Abu Yusuf does not deny the customer's option except after manufacture and the manufacturer's presentation of the item in conformity with the stipulated descriptions. Before completion of manufacture or after completion but before presentation, there is no dispute that the customer has the option, as in al-Bada'i', Radd al-Muhtar, and others. However, the text of Article 392 of the Majallah came explicitly stating that after the conclusion of istisna', there is no option for either party unless the manufactured item comes out contrary to the stipulated descriptions. So its absolute text indicated bindingness for both parties before manufacture after the conclusion of the contract, which is not an opinion of anyone in the school. So is Article 392 to be interpreted according to the detailed view of Abu Yusuf based on what came in the explanatory memorandum that it adopted his opinion, or is it said that its text is explicit in absolute bindingness and it is an imperative law, so bindingness is ruled absolutely even before work, since there is no probability or ambiguity in the text of the article to be interpreted according to the detail of Abu Yusuf's view to which the explanatory memorandum referred? The issue is subject to consideration, and the stronger view is the second consideration, because it appears that the Majallah took the theory of Abu Yusuf as a basis and expanded on it by modifying some aspects according to temporal interest, and it was coupled with the sovereign will, so it became law, and consideration is given to its text, not to the texts from which it was derived. After writing the above, I was alerted that what the Majallah adopted is another narration from Abu Yusuf explicitly mentioned in the book al-Muhit al-Burhani, which is among the major references of the Hanafi school, so there remains no issue in the practice of the Majallah.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the concept of bindingness (luzum) in contracts, focusing on the rule that some contracts are not binding (ghayr lazim) until a certain condition is fulfilled. The author illustrates with the example of muzara'ah (sharecropping): the party providing the seed can rescind before sowing, because forcing him would require destroying his property without guaranteed return. After sowing, the contract becomes binding. This reasoning is analogized to hiring a worker to demolish a house. The passage then lists eleven contracts that are non-binding in varying degrees, and singles out a twelfth: istisna' (commissioned manufacture). In the original Hanafi school, both parties could rescind istisna' before manufacture; after manufacture, only the customer had the option of inspection. However, the Majallah (Ottoman Civil Code) in Article 392 made the contract binding from inception for both parties, unless the item fails to meet specifications. This creates a tension: the Majallah's text appears to contradict the earlier school position. The author discusses the possibility that the Majallah adopted a specific narration from Abu Yusuf (from al-Muhit al-Burhani) and expanded it for reasons of public interest (maslaha zamaniyya). The stronger view is that the Majallah as legislation supersedes the earlier opinions, so its text governs. The lesson demonstrates how a rasm rule (that certain contracts are non-binding) can be modified by later legal codification based on juristic preference and public interest.

Key Terms
اللزوم (al-luzūm): The property of a contract that makes it binding and not subject to unilateral rescission.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a contract by one party, permitted only while the contract remains non-binding.
الاستصناع (al-istiṣnā‘): A contract to purchase an item to be manufactured according to specifications; originally a non-binding contract.
المجلة (al-Majallah): The Ottoman Civil Code (1876), which codified Hanafi fiqh for legal application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the position of the original Hanafi school regarding the right of rescission in an istisna' contract before manufacture?
A) Both parties may rescind.
B) Only the customer may rescind.
C) Neither party may rescind.
D) Only the manufacturer may rescind.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes that some contracts are non-binding until a condition is fulfilled; the istisna' contract was modified by the Majallah to become binding from inception, based on a valid narration from Abu Yusuf and considerations of public interest.

Istisna' Contract: Binding Nature and Khiyar
Analyzing istisna' contract: original ruling, khiyar, and codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa on the istisna' contract by distinguishing the original Hanafi position from the Majalla's codification and applying the rule of legal hierarchy.

Core Idea
The Majalla made istisna' binding from formation, expanding Abu Yusuf's view — positive law now overrides the original madhhab flexibility.

This lesson examines the istisna' (commission to manufacture) contract in Hanafi fiqh. You will see how the original madhhab granted both parties a right to rescind before manufacture, and after manufacture gave the buyer a khiyar al-ru'ya. Then you will analyze how the Ottoman Majalla codified a binding contract from inception, and why this creates a puzzle: the Majalla's text seems to go beyond any known Hanafi view. We will work through the author's resolution, which applies the principle of positive law and an alternate narration from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the Original Hanafi Position
Identify the ruling: before the item is made, both parties may rescind; after manufacture and presentation, the customer has khiyar al-ru'ya if the item does not conform.
This is the default view in the madhhab, found in al-Bada'i' and Radd al-Muhtar.
2
Summarize the Majalla's Codification (Article 392)
State that the Majalla makes the contract binding on both parties from the moment of formation, except if the manufactured item fails to meet specifications.
The text uses 'luzum' (binding) and eliminates the pre-manufacture option.
3
Identify the Discrepancy
Note that the Majalla's explicit text seems to remove the option even before work begins, which is not the view of any Hanafi scholar (including Abu Yusuf as commonly reported).
The footnote clarifies that Abu Yusuf only removes the option after presentation, not before work.
4
Analyze the Interpretive Options
Consider two possibilities: (1) The Majalla intended to adopt Abu Yusuf's detailed view, so its text should be restricted accordingly; (2) The Majalla's text is absolute and overrides the madhhab by force of law.
The author favours the second view due to the Majalla's status as positive law, but then notes a resolution.
5
Evaluate the Author's Resolution
Conclude that the Majalla's rule is actually based on another narration from Abu Yusuf recorded in al-Muhit al-Burhani, which states that the contract is binding from formation. Thus, the Majalla's codification has a basis in the madhhab.
This removes the problem: the Majalla did not invent a new ruling but chose a valid transmission.

Source Text

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh - Istisna' Contract: (0/2) Istisna' - which is purchasing what will be made by way of commission - each of the two parties is permitted to rescind it in the original madhhab without disagreement as long as the thing has not been made. But after its making and presentation, the customer has the right to rescind by way of khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) according to the preponderant view. However, the Majalla in article 392 adopted the binding nature of the contract on both parties from its formation, except if the manufactured item comes contrary to the specified descriptions. (1) The statement of reasons in the introduction of the Majalla says that it chose the view of Abu Yusuf that the customer has no option if the craftsman brings the manufactured item after work in conformity with the conditions, in accordance with what the temporal interest requires. And it is transmitted in the books of the madhhab that Abu Yusuf only says no option for the customer after the making and the craftsman brings the manufactured item conforming to the stipulated descriptions. As for before completion of its making or after completion before its presentation, there is no disagreement that the customer has the option, as in al-Bada'i' and Radd al-Muhtar and others. But the text of article 392 of the Majalla explicitly states that after the conclusion of istisna', there is no option for either party unless the manufactured item comes contrary to the stipulated descriptions. So its explicit text indicates bindingness on both parties before the making after the conclusion of the contract, which is not the view of anyone in the madhhab. So is the mentioned article to be interpreted according to the detail of Abu Yusuf's view based on what came in the statement of reasons of the Majalla that it adopted his view, or is it said that its text is explicit in absolute bindingness and it is a mandatory law, so the contract is binding absolutely even before work, since there is no probability or ambiguity in the text to be interpreted according to the detail of Abu Yusuf's view which the statement of reasons indicated was adopted? The issue is subject to consideration, and the likely view is the second consideration, because it appears that the Majalla took Abu Yusuf's theory as a basis and expanded it with amendment in some aspects according to temporal interest, and it was accompanied by the sovereign will, so it became law. So consideration is given to its text, not to the texts from which it was derived. This, and after writing the above, I was alerted that what the Majalla adopted is another narration from Abu Yusuf explicitly stated in al-Muhit al-Burhani, which is among the major references of the Hanafi madhhab, so there remains no problem in the action of the Majalla.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the istisna' (commission to manufacture) contract and its binding nature. The author explains that in the original Hanafi madhhab, both parties have the right to rescind the contract as long as the item hasn't been made. After manufacture and presentation, the customer has the right of khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) according to the preponderant view. However, the Ottoman Majalla (article 392) adopted bindingness from the moment of contracting, except if the product fails to meet specifications. The footnote clarifies that the Majalla adopted Abu Yusuf's view that the customer loses the option only after the item is made and presented in conformity. The author notes a problem: the explicit wording of the Majalla seems to make the contract binding even before work, which isn't a known position in the madhhab. He presents two possible interpretations and concludes that the second interpretation is more likely: the Majalla took Abu Yusuf's theory as a basis and expanded it according to temporal interest, becoming a law whose text is authoritative. He further notes that this is actually another narration from Abu Yusuf found in al-Muhit al-Burhani, resolving any issue.

Key Terms
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract to purchase something that will be manufactured in the future by the seller according to the buyer's specifications.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of inspection, giving the buyer the right to rescind the contract upon seeing the item if it does not meet agreed specifications.
اللزوم (al-luzum): The binding nature of a contract, meaning neither party can unilaterally rescind without the other's consent or a legal cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the original Hanafi madhhab, before the manufactured item is made, which of the following is true regarding the istisna' contract?
A) The contract is binding on both parties from inception.
B) Both parties have the right to rescind the contract.
C) Only the customer has the right to rescind.
D) The contract is void (batil) if not made immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Majalla codified istisna' as a binding contract from inception, overriding the original Hanafi flexibility, but this is based on a valid transmission from Abu Yusuf in al-Muhit al-Burhani.

Contract Bindingness After Coercion Ends
Ruling on a contract's bindingness after coercion ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rasm al-mufti rule on coercion to determine the bindingness of a contract in a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
A contract made under coercion becomes non-binding (maslub al-luzum) in favor of the coerced party, regardless of whether it is deemed fasid or mawquf.

This lesson addresses a fundamental rasm al-mufti rule: what happens to a contract after the state of coercion disappears? The answer is not simply that the contract is void. Instead, the Hanafi school teaches that the contract is stripped of bindingness towards the coerced party, giving that party the option to enforce or void it. Understanding this rule is crucial for issuing correct fatwas in cases of duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
The rule applies when a contract was entered into under coercion (ikrah) and the coercion has subsequently ended. You must confirm the presence of duress and the fact that it no longer exists.
2
Recognize the Consequence
The contract becomes 'maslub al-luzum' – stripped of bindingness – in favor of the coerced party. This means the coerced party is not bound; the other party remains bound.
The term 'maslub al-luzum' is key: it indicates lack of enforceability, not nullity.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Some Hanafi scholars view the contract as fasid (invalid) due to the defect of coercion; others view it as mawquf (suspended) until the coerced party ratifies. Both views agree on the non-binding outcome.
The disagreement does not affect the fatwa regarding the coerced party's right to void the contract.
4
Apply in a Fatwa
When asked whether a contract made under duress is valid, inform that it is not immediately binding. The coerced party may either enforce it or seek to void it, and if he chooses to enforce it, the contract becomes binding from that point.
This rule protects the coerced party's interest while allowing the transaction to stand if he wishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد زوال الإكراه، كما تقدَّم في موضعه، فهو في النتيجة عقد مسلوب اللزوم في حقه، سواء اعتبر بالإكراه فاسداً أو موقوفاً، على الخلاف الاجتهادي السابق الذكر في صفته ( ر )) ف ٤/٣٤ الحاشية).

Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the status of a contract after the state of coercion (ikrah) has ended. The text states that the contract becomes non-binding (maslub al-luzum) in favor of the coerced party. It does not matter whether one views the contract as invalid (fasid) due to coercion or as suspended (mawquf); in either case, the contract lacks bindingness for the coerced party. This reflects the ijtihadi disagreement among Hanafi scholars regarding the nature of a contract concluded under duress. The passage is a rasm rule: a contract tainted by coercion is not immediately binding on the coerced party.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion or duress that vitiates free consent in a contract.
مسلوب اللزوم (maslub al-luzum): Stripped of bindingness; the contract is not enforceable against the coerced party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The contract becomes binding on both parties.
C) The contract is stripped of bindingness in favor of the coerced party.
D) The contract is automatically rescinded.

Key Takeaway
A contract made under coercion is not binding on the coerced party after coercion ends; he has the option to enforce or void it.

Hanafi Valid Conditions in Contracts: The Role of Custom
Explains Hanafi exceptions to prohibited stipulations in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of valid conditions in Hanafi law, apply the maxim 'Custom is authoritative' to contractual stipulations, and evaluate the Hanafi reasoning for permitting customary conditions.

Core Idea
Three exceptions to the prohibition on conditions: permitted by Sharia, suitable to the contract, and customary — but only valid custom.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains how the Hanafi school, while generally prohibiting conditions that grant a unilateral benefit, makes exceptions for three types. The key is the role of custom (urf): what people customarily agree upon can validate conditions that would otherwise be corrupt, as long as they do not contradict explicit texts or general Sharia objectives. The author also critiques the traditional Hanafi reasoning and suggests that in modern times, virtually all conditions are valid unless explicitly prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general Hanafi principle on conditions
State that Hanafis generally prohibit conditions that give a unilateral benefit without compensation, as they resemble riba.
This is the default ruling before exceptions.
2
List the three exceptions and their basis
Recall the three types: (1) permitted by Sharia (e.g., deferment, options), (2) suitable to the contract (e.g., guarantor for deferred price), (3) customary (e.g., seller transports goods).
The third exception is the most expansive.
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil) behind the custom exception
The Hanafi reasoning: a customary condition restores balance because parties account for it in the price, preventing riba-like benefit.
The author critiques this reasoning as weak.
4
Critique the reasoning according to the author
The author says rational people always balance conditions, so custom is not necessary for balance; custom itself is sufficient justification.
This critique leads to the conclusion that custom is authoritative regardless of the balance argument.
5
Apply the qawida 'Custom is authoritative' to modern scenarios
Use the maxim to validate common commercial conditions (e.g., paying taxes, delivery terms) as long as they do not contradict an explicit text or Sharia objective.
The mufta bihi is that customary conditions are valid.

Chapter (42): Juristic Efforts on the Authority of Contractual Will. But the Hanafi juristic effort excludes from prohibition three types of conditions, considering them valid and binding: 1 - The condition that the law has permitted, such as stipulating deferment of the sale price and stipulating an option for one of the two contracting parties, which is called 'option of condition', and what is analogous to it like the option of payment and the option of specification. 2 - The condition that suits the contract, such as the seller stipulating on the buyer to provide a guarantor or pledge for the deferred price, because it is documentation (see: Radd al-Muhtar 5/37). 3 - The condition that is customary according to valid custom; for custom validates conditions that originally corrupt the contract. For example, if people in some places customarily sell goods on condition that the seller transports them or on condition that the seller repairs the sold machine if it breaks, that is valid. The Hanafi juristic effort builds its stance on freedom of contractual conditions on some exaggeration and expansion in adhering to the principle of balance between the rights of the parties arising from the contract; it holds that stipulating a benefit for one party disrupts the required balance and leads to dispute, because it is an extra benefit beyond the original requirement of the contract without corresponding compensation, thus resembling prohibited usury (riba al-fadl). When custom prevails on stipulating it, the contracting party is aware of the matter and accounts for it in the corresponding compensation, so balance is guaranteed, and custom removes dispute. (See: Radd al-Muhtar 4/123). (1) The meaning of option of payment and option of condition has already been mentioned; see (F 7/3, ninth). As for option of specification, it is that a sale or similar commutative contracts, such as compromise and lease, takes place on an unspecified item from several things, on condition that one of the contracting parties has the right to specify the chosen one (See: Majalla article 316). (Page 521) Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. And it is not hidden that this reasoning is weak in foundation, because the reality is that a rational person does not stipulate in his contract a benefit or obligation unless he has balanced it and accounted for it in the exchange, whether customary or not. In any case, the exception of customary conditions from prohibition has broadened the narrowing of freedom of conditions in Hanafi juristic effort, because custom on conditions has (See: Introduction of Majalla). Rather, if it were said, based on this custom theory in Hanafi juristic effort, that people's general custom of stipulating conditions in contracts is valid and considered, just as their custom on specific forms of conditions in some contracts, that would be good fiqh. Then we could say that the corrupt condition has disappeared from people's transactions by the effect of time, and conditions in this age have all become valid according to the rules of Hanafi juristic effort itself, unless the condition contradicts a specific legislative text that prohibits it or conflicts with the general rules and objectives of Sharia; for today people have become accustomed to tying all their transactions with conditions agreed upon in every contract, especially after legal rulings obliged their respect. And these juristic efforts, except for the Hanbali school, are almost agreed that the marriage contract is the farthest from the authority of the contracting parties' will over its effects and rulings, because its rulings determined by Sharia are not only the rights of the parties, but are family rights and the system of society. We will see. This is also what positive legislation and its theories in modern laws affirm, as (Secondly) Juristic efforts that see that the original principle concerning freedom of contractual conditions is absoluteness (4/42 - These juristic efforts see that the original Sharia principle, by virtue of evidences…) (Page 552).
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the Hanafi position on contractual conditions. The author classifies the passage as explaining the Hanafi juristic effort (ijtihad) regarding the authority of contractual will. He states that the Hanafi school generally prohibits conditions that are not in the contract, but makes an exception for three types: (1) conditions permitted by Sharia, like deferring payment or options; (2) conditions that suit the contract, like requiring a guarantor for a deferred price—these are considered supportive; (3) conditions that are customary (urf), because valid custom can validate conditions that would otherwise be corrupt. Examples include selling goods on condition the seller transports them or repairs a defect. The reasoning is based on maintaining balance between parties: a unilateral benefit without compensation resembles riba al-fadl, but if custom prevails, the parties account for it in the price, restoring balance. The author then critiques this reasoning as weak, noting that rational people always balance conditions. He then shows how custom can broadly validate conditions, leading to the conclusion that in modern times most conditions are valid unless they contradict a clear text or general Sharia objectives. The passage ends with a note that all schools except Hanbali agree that marriage contracts are less subject to party will because of family and societal rights. The subsequent section introduces the opposing view that the original principle is freedom of conditions. For Hanafi students, this passage illustrates the interplay of custom (urf) and legal reasoning, a key feature of the school.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): A stipulation in a contract additional to the essential elements
العرف (al-urf): Customary practice that is valid and recognized by Sharia

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three types of conditions that the Hanafi school exempts from the general prohibition?
A) A condition permitted by Sharia, such as deferring payment
B) A condition that suits the contract, such as requiring a guarantor
C) A condition that is customary, such as seller transporting goods
D) A condition that provides a benefit exclusively to one party without custom

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates three types of conditions: those permitted by Sharia, those suitable to the contract, and those upheld by valid custom, provided they do not contradict clear texts or Sharia objectives.

Hanbali Ruling on Binding Conditions in Marriage: Evidence and Fatwa Implications
Presents Hanbali jurisprudence on binding conditions in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanbali position on binding conditions in marriage, identify their primary textual evidence, and explain how a Hanafi mufti should treat this opinion in issuing fatwa.

Core Idea
For Hanbalis, conditions in marriage are more binding than other contracts—based on the Prophet's hadith and 'Umar's maxim.

This passage introduces the Hanbali school's distinctive approach to conditions in marriage contracts. Unlike other schools, Hanbalis give prime importance to stipulations, citing prophetic and caliphal authority. As a Hanafi mufti, understanding this view is essential for distinguishing between legitimate scholarly difference and operative rulings in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanbali position
The Hanbali school holds that all conditions in marriage, including financial and personal stipulations, are binding and must be fulfilled. This includes conditions about dowry, residence, time limits, and even options to annul.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Hanbalis rely on the Prophet's hadith: 'The most deserving of conditions to fulfill are those by which you make private parts lawful.' They interpret this as a general command covering all marriage conditions.
They also cite the judgment of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab regarding a residence condition and his maxim 'Rights are determined by conditions.'
3
Analyze the Hanbali reasoning
The Hanbalis consider that the hadith's wording ('most deserving') gives conditions in marriage a higher degree of obligation than in other contracts. They do not distinguish between financial and non-financial conditions.
This is a key difference from other schools.
4
Contrast with the Hanafi position and fatwa application
A Hanafi mufti must know that the Hanbali view is a legitimate ikhtilaf but not operative in his madhhab. Hanafis evaluate conditions based on compatibility with the nature of marriage and do not allow options or conditions that contradict permanence.
The mufti should respect the Hanbali view when dealing with Hanbali laypersons but issue fatwa according to Hanafi principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. If one stipulates a time limit for marriage, or no dowry, or no spousal maintenance, or no spousal enjoyment, and the like. The details of that are extensive in the books of the madhhab: al-Mughni, al-Sharh al-Kabir, Kashshaf al-Qina', and the book al-Furu' and its correction, chapter on conditions in marriage. 13/42 - The Hanbali ijtihad is based on the Prophet's saying: 'The most deserving of conditions to fulfill are those by which you make private parts lawful.' So he did not distinguish between financial dowry conditions and other legitimate spousal interests that relate to their will; rather, he considered conditions in marriage more deserving of adherence and stronger than others. The Hanbalis also adhere in this context to what Imam al-Bukhari narrated in his Sahih regarding the judgment of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, that a man married a woman and stipulated residence in her house, then wanted to move her, so they came to 'Umar, who said: 'She has her condition.' The man said, 'Then they will divorce us!' So 'Umar answered him with that eternal constitutional phrase: 'Rights are determined by conditions, and you have what you stipulated.' (See I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in 3/338, Kashshaf al-Qina' 3/53). The Hanbali ijtihad also permitted, with a difference among the madhhab's jurists, stipulating an option in the marriage contract for a specified period as in commutative contracts; when the period passes without the option-holder annulling, the marriage becomes binding. (See al-Furu' and its correction 3/60). (1) Reported by al-Bukhari (5/323 in Kitab al-Shurut, and 9/217 in Kitab al-Nikah) and Muslim (5/323, 4/140) from the hadith of 'Uqbah ibn 'Amir. (2) Suspended by al-Bukhari in two places: in al-Shurut, chapter on conditions in dowry at marriage, 5/322, and in al-Nikah, chapter on conditions in marriage, 9/217; al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said in al-Fath that Sa'id ibn Mansur connected it.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a ruling exposition on Hanbali jurisprudence regarding conditions in marriage contracts. The author begins by giving examples of permissible conditions (time limit, no dowry, no maintenance, no enjoyment) and refers to major Hanbali works for details. Then he presents the primary textual evidence: the Prophet's hadith that the most deserving conditions to fulfill are those that make sexual relations lawful. The Hanbalis interpret this to mean that all conditions in marriage, whether financial or personal, are binding and even more binding than conditions in other contracts. They also rely on 'Umar's ruling on a residence condition and his famous maxim 'Rights are determined by conditions.' The passage concludes with a note that Hanbalis allow stipulating an option period in marriage, akin to sale contracts, though there is internal disagreement. The fatwa rule is clear: conditions in marriage are taken seriously and must be honored.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition stipulated in a contract, especially in marriage, that must be fulfilled according to Hanbali jurisprudence.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option to annul a contract within a stipulated period; Hanbalis allow this in marriage contracts, but Hanafis generally do not.
مقاطع الحقوق عند الشروط (maqati' al-huquq 'inda al-shurut): 'Rights are determined by conditions' — a legal maxim attributed to 'Umar, used by Hanbalis as a general principle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school holds conditions in marriage as exceptionally binding, but a Hanafi mufti must follow his own school's nuanced position.

The Void Condition: Contradicting the Qur'an
States the exception to freedom of contract in Islamic law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify conditions that contradict Sharia and apply the rasm rule to determine the mufta bihi in contracts and dispositions.

Core Idea
A condition that forbids the lawful or permits the unlawful is void; all other conditions are valid.

The principle 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' grants broad contractual freedom, but with a critical exception: any condition that contradicts the Qur'an is void. This lesson unpacks that exception, distinguishing between suspensive and restrictive conditions, and grounding it in the mufti's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule and Its Exception
Recall the hadith: 'al-mu'minun 'ala shurutihim' (Muslims are bound by their conditions). The exception: a condition that contradicts the Qur'an is void. This is a settled rasm rule.
The exception covers both suspensive and restrictive conditions equally.
2
Define the Two Types of Conditions
A suspensive condition (shart ta'liqi) makes the contract's effect contingent on a future event. A restrictive condition (shart taqyidi) qualifies the obligations, e.g., specifying time or place. Both are permissible unless they violate Sharia.
The passage explicitly states that both types are equal in this ruling.
3
Apply the Test: Does the Condition Contradict the Qur'an?
The criterion is whether the condition makes halal haram or haram halal. Use this test to classify any condition. If it contradicts a definitive text, it is void; the contract remains valid unless the condition is fundamental.
This applies to all contracts: marriage, sale, divorce, donations, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستثن منه إلا الشرط الذي ينافي كتاب الله تعالى، وهو الشرط الذي يحل حراماً أو يحرم حلالاً؛ فيستوي فيه الشروط التعليقية والشروط التقييدية(۱) ويقول ابن القيم في تعليل ذلك : «إن تعليق العقود والفسوخ والتبرعات والالتزامات وغيرها بالشروط أمر قد تدعو إليه الضرورة أو الحاجة أو المصلحة، فلا يستغني عنه المكلف. وقد نص الإمام أحمد على جواز تعليق النكاح بالشرط كما يتعلق الطلاق وعلى جواز تعليق البيع والإبراء إعلام الموقعين، طبعة المنيرية ۲۳۷/۳ - ۲۳۸ / باختصار) . وصحة تعليق العقود بالشروط هو ما يقتضيه اليوم مبدأ سلطان الإرادة في القوانين الحديثة ومقتضى المادتين / ٢٦٥ - ٢٦٦/ من قانوننا المدني.
An exception is made from it except for a condition that contradicts the Book of Allah Almighty, which is a condition that makes lawful what is forbidden or forbids what is lawful; suspensive conditions and restrictive conditions are equal in this. And Ibn al-Qayyim says in its explanation: 'Suspending contracts, dissolutions, donations, commitments, and others by conditions is a matter that necessity, need, or interest may call for, so the legally responsible person cannot do without it. Imam Ahmad explicitly stated the permissibility of suspending marriage on a condition, just as divorce is suspended, and the permissibility of suspending sale and absolution.' (I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, Muniriyya edition, 3/237-238, abridged). And the validity of suspending contracts on conditions is what is required today by the principle of the autonomy of the will in modern laws and by the requirement of Articles 265-266 of our Civil Code.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule: the general principle that Muslims are bound by their conditions (al-mu'minun 'ala shurutihim) has an exception—any condition that contradicts the Qur'an is void. The exception covers both suspensive conditions (shart ta'liqi) and restrictive conditions (shart taqyidi). The author cites Ibn al-Qayyim, who explains that conditions serve practical needs and are permissible unless they violate divine law. The Hanbali school permits suspensive conditions in various contracts. The passage also connects this Islamic principle to modern legal concepts of freedom of contract and specific provisions in the Syrian Civil Code.

Key Terms
الشرط التعليقي (al-shart al-ta'liqi): A suspensive condition that makes the contract's effect contingent upon a future event
الشرط التقييدي (al-shart al-taqyidi): A restrictive condition that qualifies the obligations of the parties, such as stipulating a specific time or place
الشرط المنافي لكتاب الله (al-shart al-munafi li-kitab Allah): A condition that contradicts the explicit rulings of the Qur'an, either making halal haram or haram halal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A condition that makes mandatory something already obligatory
B) A condition that prohibits what is lawful in the Qur'an or permits what is prohibited
C) A condition that delays payment in a sale
D) A condition that restricts the buyer's right to resell

Key Takeaway
The exception to freedom of contract is clear: any condition that contradicts the legal provisions of the Qur'an is void, preserving the supremacy of divine law over human stipulations.

Idafa vs Ta'liq: Immediate Cause vs Suspended Contract
Distinguishing idafa and ta'liq in terms of immediate legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between idafa and ta'liq in Hanafi contract law and apply the rule that idafa creates an immediate legal cause even though its effect is delayed.

Core Idea
Idafa creates an immediate legal cause; ta'liq suspends the contract until a condition is fulfilled.

When a mufti encounters a contract that mentions a future time or a condition, they must determine whether the contract is immediately binding as a cause or whether it is suspended. This distinction—known as idafa vs ta'liq—has significant practical consequences, especially in areas like manumission and agency. This passage from Al-Zarqa's introduction lays out the Hanafi rule clearly, using concrete examples to illustrate the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate where the author introduces 'idafa' and 'ta'liq'. Note their Arabic forms and basic definitions: idafa connects the contract to a certain future time, ta'liq suspends it on an uncertain condition.
The passage also compares idafa to taqyid (restriction).
2
Compare the legal effects
Understand that in idafa, the contract is concluded immediately as a cause (sabab) but its ruling (hukm) is delayed. In ta'liq, both the cause and effect are suspended until the condition occurs.
The difference is that idafa binds the parties from the moment of utterance, while ta'liq leaves the contract non-existent until the condition.
3
Apply to the manumission example
Consider the test case: a master says 'You are free tomorrow' (idafa) vs 'If my son returns, you are free' (ta'liq). Identify the ruling on whether the master can sell the slave before the future time or condition occurs.
In idafa, the master cannot sell because the cause of freedom is already concluded. In ta'liq, he can sell because the cause is not yet concluded.
4
Apply to the agency example
Examine the agency scenario: 'When you receive the goods, you are my agent.' Note that agency begins only upon receipt, and any sale before that is invalid (fuduli).
This mirrors the manumission logic but in a bilateral contract context.
5
Summarize the rule for fatwa
Formulate the operative principle: In idafa, the contract is a concluded cause from the moment of utterance; in ta'liq, the contract is not concluded until the condition is fulfilled.
This rule guides the mufti in determining rights and obligations between parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٣) أنواع الشروط العقدية
المضاف مؤخر الظهور، فلا يبدأ إلا في المستقبل المعين؛ وتشبه التقييد من وجه آخر لأن الزمن المضاف إليه محقق القدوم وليس على خطر الوجود والعدم كما في الشرط المعلق عليه.
ولكن شبهها بالتقييد أقوى لأنَّ المؤخّر فيها ليس هو أصل العقد كما في التعليق، بل حكمه فقط .
١٠/٤٣ - وعن هذا كان مقتضى الإضافة في نظر الفقهاء أن العقد المضاف ينعقد في الحال سبباً للحكم المستقبل، فهو عقد قائم بين الطرفين منذ إنشاء الإضافة كما في حالة التقييد وليس معدوماً للحال كما في التعليق .
وبناء على ذلك قرروا في العتق أنَّ مَنْ أضاف عتق عبده إضافة إلى المستقبل، بأن قال له مثلاً: «أنت حر غداً»، لم يعد يملك بيعه رغم بقائه رقيقاً قبل الغد، لأنه قد انعقد له سبب الحرية بهذه الإضافة. فيعتبر الإعتاق موجوداً، ولكن ظهور حكمه مؤخر تأخيراً إلى أجل مسمى بخلاف ما لو علق عتقه تعليقاً بأن قال له مثلاً: إن عاد ابني من سفره سالماً فأنت حر»، فإنه رغم صحة التعليق لا يعتبر سبباً منعقداً للعتق في الحال، بل يملك المولى بيعه قبل عودة ابنه لأن المعلق بالشرط هو عدم قبل وجود الشرط (١).
(١) مثل ذلك لو صيغت الإضافة بطريق التعليق على الزمن المستقبل كما لو قال المولى في المثال المذكور: إذا جاء غد فأنت حرا، فإنه يملك بيعه قبل حلول الغد، لأن الإضافة عندئذ تصبح تعليقاً يربط فيه أصل العقد بمجيء الزمن المستقبل (ر: ف ٤٦/۵۷۹).
استناد إلى تاريخ التعليق أي أثر رجعي للزم ذلك تقدم العقد المشروط على شرطه وهو خلاف المعقول.
فلو علق إنسان الوكالة مثلاً بقوله لشخص آخر: «تسلمت متاع [؟] الفلانية فقد وكلتك ببيعها» فباعها المخاطب قبل تسليمها ثم تسلمها ربها لا ينفذ عليه البيع، لأنَّ وكالة ذلك الشخص إنما تثبت منذ تسلّم البضاعة، فقبل ذلك يكون فضولياً في بيعه.
Chapter 43: Types of Contractual Conditions. The annexed (mudaf) delays its appearance, so it only begins at a specified future time; it resembles restriction (taqyid) from another perspective because the time to which it is annexed is certain to come and is not at risk of existence or non-existence as in the case of a conditional suspended clause. But its resemblance to restriction is stronger because what is delayed in it is not the essence of the contract as in suspension (ta'liq), but only its ruling. 10/43 - And from this, in the view of jurists, the effect of annexation (idafa) is that the annexed contract is concluded immediately as a cause for the future ruling, so it is a contract existing between the parties since the creation of the annexation, as in the case of restriction, and not non-existent at present as in suspension. Accordingly, they ruled in manumission that whoever annexes the manumission of his slave to a future time, by saying to him, for example, 'You are free tomorrow,' he no longer has the right to sell him despite his remaining a slave before tomorrow, because the cause of freedom has been concluded for him by this annexation. Thus, the manumission is considered existing, but its appearance is delayed to a specified term, unlike if he suspended his manumission conditionally by saying, for example, 'If my son returns safely from his travel, you are free,' for despite the validity of the suspension, it is not considered a concluded cause for manumission at present, but the master may sell him before his son's return because what is suspended by the condition is non-existent before the existence of the condition. (1) An example of that: if the annexation is formulated by way of suspension upon a future time, as if the master in the mentioned example says, 'When tomorrow comes, you are free,' then he may sell him before the arrival of tomorrow, because the annexation then becomes a suspension that ties the essence of the contract to the arrival of the future time (ref: F 46/579). Relying on the history of suspension, that is, retroactive effect, would necessitate the conditioned contract preceding its condition, which is contrary to reason. For example, if a person suspends agency by saying to another person, 'You have received the goods of so-and-so, so I have appointed you as agent to sell them,' and the addressee sells them before receiving them, then the owner receives them, the sale is not binding on him, because that person's agency is only established from the time of receiving the goods; before that, he is an unauthorized agent (fuduli) in his sale.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the distinction between two types of contractual conditions: idafa (annexation to a future time) and ta'liq (suspension upon a condition). The author explains that idafa resembles taqyid (restriction) in that the contract is immediately effective as a cause, even though its effect appears later. The key difference from ta'liq is that in idafa the contract itself is not suspended; only its ruling is delayed. The Hanafi position is illustrated with examples from manumission ('itaq) and agency (wakala). In idafa, the master cannot sell the slave before the future time because the cause of freedom is already concluded. In ta'liq, the master retains full ownership and may sell the slave before the condition occurs. The same logic applies to agency: if one says 'When you receive the goods, you are my agent,' the agency begins only upon receipt, so any prior sale is invalid as it is an act of a fuduli (unauthorized agent). The passage emphasizes that idafa creates an immediate legal cause, while ta'liq does not take effect until the condition is fulfilled.

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-idafa): ربط العقد بزمن مستقبل محقق القدوم، بحيث ينعقد العقد في الحال سبباً للحكم المستقبل
التعليق (al-ta'liq): ربط العقد بشرط مستقبل غير محقق، بحيث لا يثبت الحكم ولا العقد إلا عند وجود الشرط
التقييد (al-taqyid): ربط العقد بزمن محدد بحيث يبدأ أثره من ذلك الزمن، كالإضافة لكنه يشبهها من حيث عدم تعليق أصل العقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on selling a slave whose manumission was annexed (idafa) to a future time before that time arrives?
A) The master may sell him freely because he is still a slave.
B) The master may not sell him because the cause of freedom is already concluded.
C) The master may sell him only with the slave's consent.
D) The sale is valid but subject to the slave's right of freedom upon the arrival of the time.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an annexed contract (idafa) creates an immediate legal cause, restricting the party's right to act inconsistently, while a suspended contract (ta'liq) leaves the contract non-existent until the condition occurs.

Contractual Conditions in Hanafi Fiqh: Classification and Rulings
Classifying contracts by condition susceptibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify contracts by their acceptance of suspension, addition, and restriction, and apply the Hanafi rules to determine the validity of conditional contracts.

Core Idea
Contracts requiring immediacy reject conditions; authorizations accept with suitable conditions; pure drops accept unconditionally.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi classification of contracts and dispositions regarding their ability to accommodate conditions. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents a systematic framework to determine which contracts can be suspended on a condition, added to a future time, or restricted by a condition. Understanding these distinctions is essential for a mufti advising on contractual arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
From the list in the passage, determine which category the contract falls into: financial exchange, donation, marriage, authorization, appointment, restriction, commitment, or pure drop.
The passage lists six categories: financial exchange, donation, marriage, authorizations, appointments, restrictions, commitments, and pure drops.
2
Apply the general rule for suspension (ta'liq) and addition (idafa)
For financial exchanges, donations (except bequest and endowment), and marriage: no ta'liq or idafa allowed; contract must be immediate. For authorizations, appointments, restrictions, commitments: ta'liq allowed only with a suitable condition; idafa allowed. For pure drops: both ta'liq and idafa allowed unconditionally.
Suitable condition means there is an appropriateness between the condition and the suspended matter.
3
Check exceptions
Bequest and endowment accept ta'liq on death and idafa after death. Lease and loan (ariyya) accept idafa but not ta'liq.
Lease and loan are temporal contracts; addition to future is inherent in their nature.
4
Determine the ruling
Based on the categories and exceptions, state whether the conditional contract is valid, void, or requires a suitable condition.
If the condition is unsuitable in authorizations, the contract is void.

- التبرعات: أي التمليك بلا عوض كالهبة والصدقة، والوصية والوقف ويدخل فيها الإبراء عن الدين لمعنى التبرع فيه، وإن كان في صورة إسقاط . فهو إسقاط غير محض، بل فيه معنى التمليك.

فلو قال لآخر: إذا ولدت فرسي هذه فقد بعتكها بكذا» أو وهبتكها ، أو قال لامرأة بحضرة شهود تزوجتك اعتباراً من أول الشهر القادم، وتم القبول، لا تصبح الفرس مبيعة أو موهوبة إذا ولدت ولا المرأة زوجة إذا حلَّ الشهر المضاف إليه .

والشرط التعليقي الملائم هو الذي يكون بينه وبين الأمر المعلق عليه مناسبة تستدعي ترتيبه عليه .

أما لو قال مثلاً: إن هبت الريح أو إن نزل المطر، فأنت وكيلي، يبطل التعليق لعدم ملاءمة الشرط .

Chapter 43: Types of Contractual Conditions
- Donations: i.e., ownership without compensation, such as gift, charity, bequest, endowment, and includes acquittance of debt due to the meaning of donation in it, even if it is in the form of a drop (of right). So it is an impure dropping, rather it contains the meaning of ownership.
4 - Authorizations: such as agency, and permission to trade for the discerning minor (1) from his guardian, for in them there is release of a dispositive power for the agent and the minor who were interdicted from it.
- Appointments: such as appointment of judges, workers, and all employees from branches of administrative authority.
6 - Restrictions: such as dismissal of agent and employee, and interdiction of the permitted minor (2).
7 - Commitments: such as guarantee of all types.
- Pure drops: such as divorce, manumission, and dropping of preemption.
14/43 - And based on that, they decide regarding the susceptibility of these contracts and dispositions to conditions the following detail:
1 - Financial exchange contracts, donations, and marriage do not accept suspension (ta'liq) or addition (idafa) absolutely; rather they must be concluded immediately. So if they are suspended on a condition or added to a future time, they become void; even if the suspended condition occurs, or the added time comes, the contract is not concluded.
So if one says to another: "If my mare gives birth, I have sold it to you for such-and-such" or "I have gifted it to you", or says to a woman in the presence of witnesses: "I have married you effective from the beginning of next month", and acceptance is done, the mare does not become sold or gifted if it gives birth, nor does the woman become a wife when the added month arrives.
But excepted from that are bequest, lease, and loan (ariya).
(1) This minor is then called "permissee" (ma'dhun). It is permissible for his guardian who permitted him to interdict him after permission. The detailed rulings of the permissee and his interdiction will come in the theory of capacity and guardianship.
a - Bequest and endowment accept suspension on death and addition to after it, to facilitate acts of goodness and charity and encourage them.
b - Lease and loan (ariya) accept addition to the future but not suspension; the meaning of addition exists in their nature because they are temporal contracts (meaning future time is a fundamental element in their execution), since if lease and loan were immediate, they would be considered as renewed added contracts with the moments of future time, because they pertain to future renewed benefits, as we will see in the discussion of contract classification.
2 - Authorizations, appointments, restrictions, and commitments accept suspension with a suitable condition, not an unsuitable one. They also accept restriction by condition and addition to the future.
The suitable suspensory condition is that between it and the suspended matter there is an appropriateness that necessitates attaching it to it.
So if a man says to another: "If my certain goods arrive, I have appointed you agent to sell them", or "If your debtor travels, I am his guarantor", the suspension is valid and the addressee becomes agent, and the speaker becomes guarantor upon fulfillment of the suspended condition of arrival of goods or travel of debtor.
But if he says, for example: "If the wind blows" or "If rain falls, you are my agent", the suspension is invalid due to the unsuitability of the condition.
- Pure drops accept suspension by condition absolutely, suitable or unsuitable, and also accept addition to the future.
4 - Financial exchange contracts like sale, lease, and the like accept restriction (taqayyud).
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a classification from Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' on the rules of contractual conditions in the Hanafi school. The author first lists the types of contracts and dispositions: donations (including acquittance), authorizations, appointments, restrictions, commitments, and pure drops. Then he presents the detailed rules on whether these contracts accept suspension (ta'liq) on a condition, addition (idafa) to a future time, or restriction by a condition. The key principle is that financial exchange contracts, donations (other than bequest and endowment), and marriage must be immediate; they do not accept suspension or addition. Exceptions are bequest and endowment (which accept suspension on death and addition), and lease and loan (which accept addition but not suspension). Authorizations, appointments, restrictions, and commitments accept suspension only with a suitable condition (one that is appropriate to the subject matter), and they also accept addition and restriction. Pure drops accept suspension and addition unconditionally. The passage also defines suitable condition for suspension as one that has a meaningful connection to the suspended matter. Muftis must apply these rules when advising on the validity of conditional contracts.

Key Terms
التعليق (at-ta'liq): Suspension of a contract or disposition on a future condition; the contract does not take effect until the condition occurs.
الإضافة (al-idafa): Addition of a contract to a future time; the contract is valid but its effect is delayed until that time.
التقييد (at-taqayyud): Restriction of a contract by a condition that does not suspend the contract but qualifies its terms.
الشرط التعليقي الملائم (ash-shart at-ta'liqi al-mula'im): A suitable suspensory condition: one that has an appropriateness (munasaba) with the suspended matter, such that it is reasonable to attach the contract to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rules presented, which of the following contracts accept suspension (ta'liq) only with a suitable condition?
A) Sale and marriage
B) Bequest and endowment
C) Agency (wakalah) and guarantee (kafalah)
D) Divorce and manumission

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies contracts into categories based on their capacity for conditional clauses; the key is whether the contract requires immediacy or can accommodate a condition.
